Elife 12:.<BR><HR></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.1.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.1.1&tcname=">1.A.1.1.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Two TMS K<sup>+</sup> and water channel (conducts K<sup>+</sup> (K<sub>D</sub> = 8 mM); blocked by Na<sup>+</sup> (190 mM) (<span>Renart <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>) and tetrabutylammonium (<span>Iwamoto <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>)). Ion permeation occurs by ion-ion contacts in single file fashion through the selectivity filter (<span>K&ouml;pfer <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). A narrow pore lined with four arrays of carbonyl groups is responsible for
ion selectivity, whereas a conformational change of the four inner transmembrane helices (TMS2) is
involved in gating (<span>Baker <em>et al.</em> 2007</span>). Two gates have been identified; one is located at the inner bundle crossing and is activated by H<sup>+</sup> while the second gate is in the selectivity filter (<span>Rauh <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). The C-terminal domain mediates pH modulation (<span>Hirano <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>;&nbsp;<span>Pau <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>). KcsA exhibits a global twisting motion upon gating (<span>Shimizu <em>et al.</em>, 2008</span>).&nbsp; Activity is influenced by the phase of the lipid bilayer (<span>Seeger <em>et al.</em> 2010</span>), and occupancy of nonannular lipid binding sites increases the stability of the tetrameric complex (<span>Triano <em>et al.</em> 2010</span>).&nbsp; The open conformation of KcsA can disturb the bilayer integrity and catalyze the flipping of
phospholipids (<span>Nakao <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).&nbsp; This protein is identical to the KcsA orthologue (P0A333) in <em>Streptomyces coelicolor</em>.&nbsp; The stability of the pre domain in KcsA is stabilized by GCN4 (<span>Yuchi <em>et al.</em> 2008</span>).&nbsp; The
potential role of pore hydration in channel gating has been evaluated (<span>Blasic <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; Having multiple K<sup>+</sup> ions bound simultaneously
is required for selective K<sup>+</sup> conduction, and a reduction in the number of bound K<sup>+</sup> ions
destroys the multi-ion selectivity mechanism utilized by K<sup>+</sup> channels (<span>Medovoy <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).&nbsp; The channel accomodates K<sup>+</sup> and H<sub>2</sub>O molecules alternately in a K<sup>+</sup>-H<sub>2</sub>O-K<sup>+</sup>-H<sub>2</sub>O&nbsp; series through the channel (<span>Kratochvil <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). Insertion of KcsA is spontaneous and directional as the
cytosolic part of the protein does not translocate across the membrane barrier. Charged residues, not hydrophobic residues, are crucial for insertion of the unfolded protein
into the membrane via electrostatic interactions between
membrane and protein.&nbsp; A two-step
mechanism was proposed. An initial electrostatic attraction between membrane and protein
represents the first step prior to insertion of hydrophobic residues into the hydrocarbon core of
the membrane (<span>Altrichter <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). Bend, splay, and twist distinguish KcsA gate opening, filter opening, and filter-gate coupling, respectively (<span>Mitchell and Leibler 2017</span>). Details of the water permeability have been presented. Water flow through KcsA is halved by 200 mM K<sup>+</sup> in the aqueous 
solution, which indicates an effective K<sup>+</sup> dissociation constant in 
that range for a singly occupied channel. (<span>Hoomann <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>). A parameterized MARTINI program can be used to predict the hinging motions of the protein (<span id="ref34549584.30762370" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Activation of KcsA is initiated by proton binding to the pH gate upon an intracellular drop in pH which prompts a conformational switch, leading to a loss of affinity for potassium ions at the selectivity filter and therefore to channel inactivation (<span id="ref34551170.27044983" class="cite">Rivera-Torres <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). An alteration in the conformational equilibrium of the intracellular K<sup>+</sup>-gate is one of the fundamental mechanisms underlying the dysfunctions of K<sup>+</sup> channels caused by disease-related mutations (<span id="ref34552212.33057011" class="cite">Iwahashi <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Folding and misfolding of KcsA monomers during assembly and tetramerization has been examined (<span id="ref34553593.34413192" class="cite">Song <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). The flexible C-terminus stabilizes KcsA tetramers at a neutral pH with decreased stabilization at acidic pH (<span id="ref34555253.36009016" class="cite">Howarth and McDermott 2022</span>). Under equilibrium conditions, in the absence of a transmembrane voltage, both water and K<sup>+</sup> occupy the selectivity filter of the KcsA channel in the closed conductive state (<span id="ref34557126.37578394" class="cite">Ryan <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Gram-positive bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Skc1 (KcsA) of <em>Streptomyces lividans</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.1&tcname=">1.A.1.10.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-sensitive Na<sup>+</sup> channel, Na<sub>V</sub>1.7 (<span id="ref3628.17167479" class="cite">Cox <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>). The human orthologue, SCN3A or Nav1.3, when mutated causes cryptogenic pediatric partial epilepsy (<span id="ref4786.18242854" class="cite">Holland <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>; <span id="ref34551745.32515017" class="cite">Zaman <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Batrachotoxin (BTX) is a steroidal alkaloid
neurotoxin that activates NaV channels through interacting with transmembrane domain-I-segment 6
(IS6) of these channels. Ginsenoside inhibits BTX binding (<span id="ref34545521.18321475" class="cite">Lee <em>et al.</em> 2008</span>). VGSCs are heterotrimeric complexes consisting of a single pore-forming alpha subunit joined by two beta subunits, a noncovalently linked beta1 or beta3 and a covalently linked beta2 or beta4 subunit (<span id="ref34547290.28927993" class="cite">Hull and Isom 2017</span>).&nbsp; The binding mode and functional components of the analgesic-antitumour peptide from<em> Buthus martensii </em>Karsch to human voltage-gated sodium channel 1.7 have been characterized (<span id="ref34549807.31099313" class="cite">Zhao <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). <span id="ref34553359.34036922" class="cite">Dvorak <em>et al.</em> 2021 </span>developed allosteric modulators of ion channels by targeting their PPI interfaces, particularly in the C-terminal domain of the Nav, with auxiliary proteins. Fenestrations are key functional regions of Nav that modulate drug binding, lipid binding, and influence gating behaviors (<span id="ref34554473.35222049" class="cite">Gamal El-Din and Lenaeus 2022</span>). Compartment-specific localizations and trafficking mechanisms for VGSCs are regulated separately to modulate membrane excitability in the brain (<span id="ref34554905.35672149" class="cite">Liu <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Naview is a library for drawing and annotating voltage-gated sodium channel membrane diagrams (<span id="ref34555548.36304314" class="cite">Afonso <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Deltamethrin (DLT) is a type-II pyrethroid ester insecticide used in agricultural and domestic applications as well as in public health. Exposure to DLT produced a differential and dose-dependent stimulation of peak Na+ currents, Conversely, tefluthrin (Tef), a type-I pyrethroid insecticide, accentuates I(Na) with a slowing in inactivation time course of the current (<span id="ref34556926.36499059" class="cite">Lin <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). MicroRNA-335-5p suppresses voltage-gated sodium channel expression and may be a target for seizure control (<span id="ref34556972.37463203" class="cite">Heiland <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; Voltage-gated sodium channels are enhancing factors in the metastasis of metastatic prostate cancer cells (<span id="ref34557517.37937335" class="cite">Yildirim-Kahriman 2023</span>). Decreasing microtubule detyrosination 
modulates Nav1.5 subcellular distribution and restores sodium current in Mdx cardiomyocytes (<span id="ref34557831.38395031" class="cite">Nasilli <em>et al.</em> 2024</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-sensitive Na<sup>+</sup> channel of <em>Rattus norvegicus</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.10"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.10&tcname=">1.A.1.10.10</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="1.A.1.10.10"></a></p>
<p><a id="1.A.1.10.10"></a></p>
<p>The insect (cockroach) Na<sup>+ </sup>channel. Batrachotoxin, pyrethroids, and BTG 502 share overlapping binding sites (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref8495">Du <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>).&nbsp; Insecticides tagetting Na<sup>+ </sup> channels include indoxacarb and metaflumizone (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref9655">Casida and Durkin 2013</a>). They preferably
bind to and trap sodium channels in the slow-inactivated non-conducting state, a mode of action
similar to that of local anesthetics (<span id="ref34544584.26407935" class="cite">Jiang <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; Asp802 is involved in gating and action, but not binding, of pyrethroid insecticides (<span id="ref9715.20561903" class="cite">Du <em>et al.</em> 2010</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals (Insects)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Na<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;channel of <em>Blattella germanica</em>&nbsp;(O01307)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.11"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.11&tcname=">1.A.1.10.11</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Sodium channel of 2215 aas and 24 TMSs, VmNa.&nbsp; An L925V mutation in the channel domain renders the honey bee mites resistant to pyrethroids such as tau-
fluvalinate and flumethrin (<span id="ref10493.24367572" class="cite">Gonz&aacute;lez-Cabrera <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals (Insects)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>VmNa of <em> Varroa destructor</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.12"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.12&tcname=">1.A.1.10.12</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Type 2 Na<sup>+</sup> channel, SCN2A or NaV1.2, of 2,005 aas and 24 TMSs.&nbsp; Mutations give rise to epileptic encephalophathy, Ohtahara syndrome (<a>Nakamura <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>). They may also give rise to autism (ASD) (<span>Tavassoli <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). This protein is orthologous to the rat Na<sup>+</sup> channel, TC# 1.A.1.10.1 and very similar to the type 1 Na<sup>+</sup> channel (1.A.1.10.7). Na<sub>V</sub>1.2 has a single pore-forming alpha-subunit and two transmembrane beta-subunits. Expressed primarily in the brain, Na<sub>V</sub>1.2 is critical for initiation and propagation of action potentials. Milliseconds after the pore opens, sodium influx is terminated by inactivation processes mediated by regulatory proteins including calmodulin (CaM). Both calcium-free (apo) CaM and calcium-saturated CaM bind tightly to an IQ motif in the C-terminal tail of the alpha-subunit. Thermodynamic studies and solution structure (2KXW) of a C-domain fragment of apo 13C,15N- CaM (CaMC) bound to an unlabeled peptide with the sequence of the rat NaV1.2 IQ motif showed that apo CaMC (a) was necessary and sufficient for binding, and (b) bound more favorably than calcium-saturated CaMC. CaMN&nbsp; apparently does not influence apo CaM binding to NaV1.2IQp <span>(Mahling <em>et al.</em> 2017)</span>. The phenotypic spectrum of SCN2A-related epilepsy is broad, ranging from benign epilepsy in neonate and infancy to severe epileptic encephalopathy. Oxcarbazepine and valproate are the most effective drugs in epilepsy patients with SCN2A variants. Sodium channel blockers often worsen seizures in patients with seizure onset beyond 1 year of age. Abnormal brain MRI findings and de novo variations are often related to poor prognosis. Most SCN2A variants located in transmembrane regions were related to patients with developmental delay (<span id="ref34554660.35431799" class="cite">Zeng <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The beta4-subunit and PRRT2 form a push-pull system that finely tunes the membrane expression and function of Na<sub>V</sub> channels and the intrinsic neuronal excitability (<span id="ref34555669.36441479" class="cite">Valente <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Icariin can be used to treat epilepsy by inhibiting neuroinflammation via promoting microglial polarization to the M2 phenotype (<span id="ref34557468.37866741" class="cite">Wang <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<hr /></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SCN2A of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.13"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.13&tcname=">1.A.1.10.13</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-sensitive Na<sup>+ </sup>channel of 2821 aas and 24 TMSs (<span id="ref34543882.19574227" class="cite">Cohen <em>et al.</em> 2009</span>). Pyrethroid, an insecticide,
binds to insect Na<sup>+</sup> channels at two sites called pyrethroid,receptors, PyR1 (initial) and PyR2, located in the domain interfaces II/III and I/II, respectively, and binding residues have been identified (<span id="ref34544125.25972447" class="cite">Du <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Na<sup>+</sup> channel of <em>Drosophila melanogaster</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.14"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.14&tcname=">1.A.1.10.14</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The <span class="recommended-name">voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel (VDCC; CAV2), &alpha;-subunit of 2027 aas and 24 TMSs in </span>four domains, each
with six transmembrane segments and EEEE loci in the ion-selective filter, typical of
VDCCs in vertebrates. CAV2 primarily localizes in the distal part
of flagella and is transported toward the flagellar tip via
intraflagellar transport (IFT) although CAV2 accumulates near the flagellar base when IFT is
blocked. Thus, Ca<sup>2+</sup> influx into <em>Chlamydomonas</em> flagella is
mediated by the VDCC, CAV2, whose distribution is biased to the distal region of the flagellum, and this is required for flagellar waveform conversion <span class="recommended-name">(<span id="ref34544099.19167228" class="cite">Fujiu <em>et al.</em> 2009</span>).</span></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants (Algae)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CAV2 of <em>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii (Chlamydomonas smithii)</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.15"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.15&tcname=">1.A.1.10.15</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The sodium channel of 1989 aas and 24 TMSs.&nbsp; 80% identical to the characterized channel of the crayfish (<em>Astacus leptodactylus </em>(Turkish narrow-clawed crayfish)<em> (Pontastacus leptodactylus)</em><em></em>) in which functional regions responsible for the selectivity filter, inactivation gate,

A novel strategy for screening mutations in 
the voltage-gated sodium channel gene of <em>Aedes albopictus</em> based on 
multiplex PCR-mass spectrometry minisequencing technology, has appeared (<span id="ref34557037.37580776" class="cite">Mu <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<p><span style="text-decoration: underline;"><strong></strong></span></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Na<sup>+</sup> channel of <em>Aedes aegypti </em>(Yellowfever mosquito)<em> (Culex aegypti)</em>, the most prevalent vector of dengue and
yellow fever viruses.</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.17"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.17&tcname=">1.A.1.10.17</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-gated Na<sup>+</sup> Channel protein of 2,139 aas and 24 TMSs.&nbsp; <span>Mediates voltage-dependent sodium ion permeability of excitable membranes.&nbsp; 3-d modeling revealed </span>spacial clustering of evolutionarily conserved acidic residues at extracellular sites (<span id="ref34546203.27919210" class="cite">Vinekar and Sowdhamini 2016</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>PARA sodium channel of <em>Anopheles gambiae </em>(African malaria mosquito)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.18"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.18&tcname=">1.A.1.10.18</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Sodium channel protein, &alpha;-subunit, FPC1, of 2050 aas and 24 TMSs.&nbsp; The 3-d structure has been solved by cryoEM to 3.8 &Aring; resolution (<span id="ref34546485.28183995" class="cite">Shen <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). One residue at the corresponding selectivity filter (SF) locus in each repeat, Asp/Glu/Lys/Ala (DEKA), determines Na<sup>+</sup> selectivity. The S1 to S4 segments in each repeat form a 
voltage-sensing domain (VSD), wherein S4 carries repetitively occurring 
positive residues essential for voltage sensing. There are seven extracellular glycosylation sites (<span id="ref34546485.28183995" class="cite">Shen <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>FPC1 of <em>Periplaneta americana </em>(American cockroach)<em> (Blatta americana)</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.19"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.19&tcname=">1.A.1.10.19</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Sodium channel Nav1.4-beta complex of 1820 and 209 aas, respectively.&nbsp; Voltage-gated sodium (Nav) channels initiate and propagate action potentials.&nbsp;<span>Yan <em>et al.</em> 2017</span> presented the cryo-EM structure of EeNav1.4, the Nav channel from electric eel, in complex with the beta1 subunit at 4.0&nbsp;&Aring; resolution. The immunoglobulin domain of beta1 docks onto the extracellular L5I and L6IV loops of EeNav1.4 via extensive polar interactions, and the single transmembrane helix interacts with the third voltage-sensing domain (VSDIII). The VSDs exhibit ""up"" conformations, while the intracellular gate of the pore domain is kept open by a digitonin-like molecule. Structural comparison with closed NavPaS shows that the outward transfer of gating charges is coupled to the iris-like pore domain dilation through intricate force transmissions involving multiple channel segments. The IFM fast inactivation motif on the III-IV linker is plugged into the corner enclosed by the outer S4-S5 and inner S6 segments in repeats III and IV, suggesting a potential allosteric blocking mechanism for fast inactivation (<span>Yan <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). The PDB# for the complex is 5XSY, and that for the two subunits are 5XSY_A and 5XSY_B. Domain 4 TMS 6 of Nav1.4 plays a key role in channel gating regulation, and is targeted by the neurotoxin, veratridine (VTD) (<span id="ref34549215.30389410" class="cite">Niitsu <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Nav1.4-beta subunits of <em>Electrophorus electricus </em>(Electric eel)<em> (Gymnotus electricus)</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.2&tcname=">1.A.1.10.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Na<sup>+</sup> channel, &alpha;-subunit, SCAP1, of 1993 aas and 24 TMSs (<span id="ref34557519.9115644" class="cite">Dyer <em>et al.</em> 1997</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Metazoa</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SCAP1 from <em>Aplysia californica</em> (P90670)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.20"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.20&tcname=">1.A.1.10.20</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-gated sodium channel of 1836 aas and 24 TMSs, PaFPC1. The 3-d structure has been determined (<span id="ref34546485.28183995" class="cite">Shen <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).&nbsp; <span>It mediates the voltage-dependent sodium ion permeability in excitable membranes.</span></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>t</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>PaFPC1 of <em>Periplaneta americana</em> (American cockroach) <em>(Blatta americana)</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.21"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.21&tcname=">1.A.1.10.21</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Putative two component voltage-gated Na<sup>+</sup> channel, subunit 1 of 1149 aas and subunit 2 of 958 aas.&nbsp; Decreased expression of these genes, encoding this system, gives rise to mortality of the peach-potato aphid, <em>Myzus persicae (<span id="ref34549674.30923355" class="cite">Tariq <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).</em></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>NaV of <em>Myzus persicae.</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.22"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.22&tcname=">1.A.1.10.22</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The Na<sup>+</sup>-activated Na<sup>+</sup> channel (Nax; also called SCN7A; of 1737 aas and ~24 TMSs) and salt-inducible kinase (SIK, see TC# 8.A.104.1.14) are stimulated by increases in local Na<sup>+</sup> concentration, affecting the Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase activity (see TC# 3.A.3.1.1). It mediates the voltage-dependent sodium ion permeability of excitable 
membranes. Assuming opened or closed conformations in response to the 
voltage difference across the membrane, the protein forms a 
sodium-selective channel through which Na<sup>+</sup> ions may pass in accordance with their electrochemical gradient (<span id="ref34556440.37175599" class="cite">Gonsalez <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Rare SCG genetic variants may contribute to the development of painful neuropathy (<span id="ref34556442.37175987" class="cite">Almomani <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Nax of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.3&tcname=">1.A.1.10.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Ca<sup>2+</sup>-regulated heart Na<sup>+</sup> channel, Na<sub>v</sub>1.5, SCN5A or INa channel of 2016 aas. The COOH terminus functions in the control of channel inactivation and in pathologies caused by inherited mutations that disrupt it (<span>Glaaser <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>); regulated by ProTx-II Toxin (<span>Smith <em>et al.</em> 2007</span>), telethonin, the titin cap protein (167aas; secreted protein; O15273) (<span>Mazzone <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>), and the Mog1 protein, a central component of the channel complex (<span>Wu <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>). Na<sub>v</sub>1.5, the principal Na<sup>+</sup> channel in the heart, possesses an ankyrin binding site, and direct interaction with ankyrin-G is required for the expression of Na<sub>v</sub>1.5 at the cardiomyocyte cell surface (<span>Bennett and Healy, 2008</span>; <span>Lowe <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>). Mutations cause type 3 long QT syndrome and type 1 Brugada syndrome, two distinct heritable arrhythmia syndromes (<span>Mazzone <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>; <span>Kapplinger <em>et al.</em> 2010</span>; <span>Wang <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp;SCN5A mutations causing arrhythmic dilated cardiomyopathy,  commonly localized to the voltage-sensing mechanism, and giving rise to gating pore currents (currents that go through the voltage sensor) have been identified (<span>McNair <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>; <span>Moreau <em>et al.</em>, 2014</span>).&nbsp; Patients with Brugada syndrome
are prone to develop ventricular tachyarrhythmias that may lead to syncope, cardiac arrest or sudden
cardiac death (<span>Sheikh and Ranjan 2014</span>) and (<span>Kapplinger <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). Mutations causing disease have been identified (<span>Qureshi <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). These give rise to arrhythias and cardiomyopathies (<span>Moreau <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; Mutations that cause relative resistance to slow inactivation have been identified (<span>Chancey <em>et al.</em> 2007</span>).&nbsp; Green tea catechins are potential anti-arrhythmics because of the
significant effect of Epigallocatechin-3-Gallate (E3G) on&nbsp; cardiac sodium
channelopathies that display a hyperexcitability phenotype (<span>Boukhabza <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). A mutatioin, R367G, causes the familial cardiac conductioin disease (<span>Yu <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). The C-terminal domain of calmodulin (CaM) binds to an IQ motif in the C-terminal tail of the alpha-subunit of all NaV isoforms, and contributes to calcium-dependent pore-gating in some (<span>Isbell <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Ventricular fibrillation in patients with Brugada syndrome (BrS) is often initiated by premature ventricular contractions, and the presence of SCN5A mutations increases the risk upon exposure to sodium channel blockers in patients with or without baseline type-1 ECG (<span>Amin <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). A mutation (R367G) is associated with familial cardiac conduction disease (<span id="ref34548112.28534967" class="cite">Yu <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).  Among ranolazine, flecainide, and mexiletine, only mexiletine restored inactivation kinetics of the currents of the mutant protein, A1656D (<span id="ref34551006.31506521" class="cite">Kim <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).&nbsp; Epigallocatechin-3-gallate (EGCG) is protective against cardiovascular disorders due in part to its action on multiple molecular pathways and transmembrane proteins, including the cardiac Nav1.5 channels (<span id="ref34551012.32085432" class="cite">Amarouch <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). <span style="font-weight: normal;">An SCN1B variant affects both cardiac-type (NaV1.5) and brain-type 
(NaV1.1) sodium currents and contributes to complex concomitant brain 
and cardiac disorders (<span id="ref34552242.33134290" class="cite">Martinez-Moreno <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). </span>Mice null for Scn1b, which encodes NaV beta1 and beta1b subunits, have defects in neuronal development and excitability, spontaneous generalized seizures, cardiac arrhythmias, and early mortality <span style="font-weight: normal;">(Martinez-Moreno et al., 2020; <span id="ref34552244.33134290" class="cite">Martinez-Moreno <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). </span>The structural basis of cytoplasmic NaV1.5 and NaV1.4 regulation has been reviewed (<span id="ref34552437.33306788" class="cite">Nathan <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Fibroblast growth factor 21 ameliorates NaV1.5 and Kir2.1 channel dysregulation in human AC16 cardiomyocytes (<span id="ref34554032.34630093" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). The interaction of Nav1.5 with MOG1 (RANGRF), a<strong> </strong>Ran guanine nucleotide release factor and chaparone, provides a possible molecular mechanism for Brugada syndrome (<span id="ref34554154.34843967" class="cite">Xiong <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Arrhythmic phenotypes are a defining feature of dilated cardiomyopathy-associated SCN5A variants (<span id="ref34554275.34949099" class="cite">Peters <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). A SCN5A genetic variant, Y739D, is associated with Brugada syndrome (<span id="ref34554580.35300108" class="cite">Zaytseva <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Melatonin treatment causes an increase of conduction via enhancement of sodium channel protein expression and increases of sodium current in the ventricular myocytes (<span id="ref34554670.35384053" class="cite">Durkina <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Quantification of Nav1.5 expression has been published (<span id="ref34554683.35456996" class="cite">Adams <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Cardiac sodium channel complexes play a role in arrhythmia, and the structural and functional roles of the beta1 and beta3 subunits have been determined (<span id="ref34555725.36354758" class="cite">Salvage <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Brugada Syndrome (BrS) treatment is 
electrocardiography with ST-segment elevation in the direct precordial 
derivations. The clinical presentation of the disease is highly 
variable. Patients can remain completely asymptomatic, but they can 
also develop episodes of syncope, atrial fibrillation (AF), sinus node 
dysfunction (SNF), conduction disorders, asystole, and ventricular 
fibrillation (VF). This disease is caused by mutations in the genes 
responsible for the potential action of cardiac cells. The most commonly
 involved gene is SCN5A, which controls the structure and function of 
the heart's sodium channel (<span id="ref34556160.36946855" class="cite">Brugada 2023</span>).</p>

<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Na<sub>v</sub>1.5 of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (Q14524)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.4&tcname=">1.A.1.10.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The skeletal muscle Na<sup>+</sup> channel, Na<sub>V</sub>1.4 of 1836 aas and 24 TMSs. Mutations in charged residues in the S4 segment cause hypokalemic periodic paralysis (HypoPP)) due to sustained sarcolemmal depolarization (<span>Struyk and Cannon 2007</span>; <span>Sokolov <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>; <span>Groome <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). Also causes myotonia; regulated by calmodulin which binds to the C-terminus of Na<sub>v</sub>1.4 (<span>Biswas <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>). Na<sub>V</sub>1.4 gating pores are permeable to guanidine as well as Na<sup>+</sup> and H<sup>+</sup> (<span>Sokolov <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>). The R669H mutation allows transmembrane
permeation of protons, but not larger cations, similar to the conductance displayed by histidine
substitution at Shaker K<sup>+</sup> channel S4 sites (<span>Struyk and Cannon 2007</span>).&nbsp; The mechanism of inactivation involves transient
interactions between intracellular domains resulting in direct pore occlusion by the IFM motif and
concomitant extracellular interactions with the beta1 subunit (<span>S&aacute;nchez-Solano <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). Potassium-sensitive hypokalaemic and normokalaemic periodic paralysis are inherited skeletal muscle diseases in humans, characterized by episodes of flaccid muscle weakness. They are caused by single mutations in positively charged residues ('gating charges') in the S4 transmembrane segment of the voltage sensor of the voltage-gated sodium channel Nav1.4 or the calcium channel Cav1.1. Mutations of the outermost gating charges (R1 and R2) cause hypokalaemic periodic paralysis by creating a pathogenic gating pore in the voltage sensor through which cations leak in the resting state. Mutations of the third gating charge (R3) cause normokalaemic periodic paralysis owing to cation leak in both activated and inactivated states (<span>Jiang <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). The neurotoxic cone snail peptide &mu;-GIIIA specifically blocks skeletal muscle voltage-gated sodium (Na<sub>V</sub>1.4) channels (<span id="ref34546318.27916464" class="cite">Leipold <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). the cryo-electron microscopy structure of the human Na<sub>v</sub>1.4-&beta;1
 complex at 3.2-&Aring; resolution. Accurate model building was made for the 
pore domain, the voltage-sensing domains, and the &beta;1 subunit (<span id="ref34548979.30190309" class="cite">Pan <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>) provided 
insight into the molecular basis for Na<sup>+</sup> permeation and 
kinetic asymmetry of the four repeats. Structural analysis of reported 
functional residues and disease mutations corroborates an allosteric 
blocking mechanism for fast inactivation of Na<sub>v</sub> channels. the S4-S5L of the DI, DII and DIII domains allosterically modulate the activation gate and stabilize its open state (<span id="ref34551295.32246066" class="cite">Malak <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). The structural basis of cytoplasmic NaV1.5 and NaV1.4 regulation has been reviewed (<span id="ref34552437.33306788" class="cite">Nathan <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Mutations in SCN4A give rise to a variety of pathological conditions (<span id="ref34553178.33965302" class="cite">Sun <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Hypokalemic periodic paralysis (HypoPP) is a rare autosomal dominant disease caused by mutations in either calcium or sodium transmembrane voltage-gated ion channels in the ER of skeletal muscle (<span id="ref34557150.37601992" class="cite">Calise <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Na<sub>V</sub>1.4 of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (P35499)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.5&tcname=">1.A.1.10.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p>Voltage-sensitive Na<sup>+ </sup> channel, type 9, &alpha;-subunit, Nav1.7 or SCN9A (orthologous to 1.A.1.10.1).  Loss of function, resulting from point mutations, results in a channelopathy called Congenital Insensitivity to Pain (CIP) (<span id="ref34548662.30037327" class="cite">He <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>), that causes the congenital inability to experience pain (<a>Cregg <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>; <a>Kleopa, 2011</a>). An S241T mutation causes inherited erythromelalgia IEM; erythermalgia, an autosomal dominant neuropathy characterized by burning pain in the extremities in response to mild warmth (due to altered gating) (<a>Lampert <em>et al</em>., 2006</a>; <a>Drenth and Waxman, 2007</a>). Gain-of-function mutations in the Na(v)1.7 channel lead to DRG neuron hyperexcitability associated with severe pain, whereas loss of the Na(v)1.7 channel in patients leads to indifference to pain (<a>Dib-Hajj <em>et al</em>., 2007</a>). Blocked by 1-benzazepin-2-one (Kd = 1.6 nM) (<a>Williams <em>et al</em>., 2007</a>). Mutations in the Nav1.7 Na<sup> </sup> channel &alpha;-subunit give rise to familial pain syndromes called chronic non-paoxysmal neuropathic pain (<a>Catterall <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>; <a>Fischer and Waxman, 2010;&nbsp;</a><a>Dabby <em>et al.</em> 2011</a> ). It interacts with the sodium channel beta3 (Scn3b), rather than the beta1 subunit, as well as the collapsing-response mediator protein (Crmp2) through which the analgesic drug lacosamide regulates Nav1.7 current (<span>Kanellopoulos <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). The R1488 variant is totally inactive (<span id="ref34548662.30037327" class="cite">He <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Nav1.7 is inhibited by knottins (see TC# 8.B.19.2) (<span id="ref34548778.30148615" class="cite">Agwa <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Nav1.7 interacts with the following proteins: syn3b (TC# 8.a.17.1.2; the &beta;3 subunit), Crmp2, Syt2 (Q8N9I0) and Tmed10 (P49755), and it also regulates opioid receptor efficacy (<span id="ref34547803.29335280" class="cite">Kanellopoulos <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Mutations in TRPA1 and Nav1.7 to&nbsp; insensitivity to pain-promoting algogens such as 
capsaicin, acid, and allyl isothiocyanate (AITC), have been documented (<span id="ref34549831.31147513" class="cite">Eigenbrod <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Na<sub>v</sub>1.7 is associated with endometrial cancer (<span id="ref34551005.31598168" class="cite">Liu <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>) and fever-associated seizures or epilepsy (FASE) (<span id="ref34551125.31394368" class="cite">Ding <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Nav1.7 and Nav1.8 peripheral nerve sodium channels are modulated by protein kinases A and C (<span id="ref34555302.14657190" class="cite">Vijayaragavan <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). Sodium channel Na<sub>V</sub>1.7 and potassium channel K<sub>V</sub>7.2 promote and oppose excitability in nociceptors, respectively. Inflammation differentially controls transport
 of depolarizing Nav versus hyperpolarizing Kv channels to drive rat 
nociceptor activity (<span id="ref34556065.36897973" class="cite">Higerd-Rusli <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). The structural basis for severe pain, caused by mutations in the S4-S5 linkers of voltage-gated sodium channel Na<sub>V</sub>1.7, have been revealed (<span id="ref34556197.36996107" class="cite">Wisedchaisri <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Nav1.7 of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (Q15858)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.6&tcname=">1.A.1.10.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Tetrodotoxin-resistant voltage-gated Na<sup>+</sup> channel of dorsal ganglion sensory neurons, Na<sub>v</sub>1.8 (<span id="ref6096.8538791" class="cite">Akopian <em>et al</em>., 1996</span>). Essential for pain at low temperatures (<span id="ref4097.17568746" class="cite">Zimmermann <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>). Na<sub>v</sub>1.8 is the sole electrical impulse generator in a nociceptor that transmits information to the central nervous system.&nbsp; Bark scorpion venom induces pain in many mammals (house mice, rats, 
humans) by activating Nav1.7 but has no
 effect on Nav1.8. Grasshopper mice Nav1.8 has amino acid variants that 
bind bark scorpion toxins and inhibit Na<sup>+</sup> currents, blocking action 
potential propagation and inducing analgesia.&nbsp; These mice thereby can use scorpions as a food source (<span id="ref10389.24159034" class="cite">Zhu <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>; <span id="ref10390.24159039" class="cite">Rowe <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>). Nav1.8 is involved in bull spermatozoa dynamics including motility, membrane integrity, acrosome integrity, capacitation
and mitochondrial transmembrane potential (<span id="ref34546456.28166971" class="cite">Chauhan <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). <span style="font-weight: normal;">Selective inhibition of Na<sub>V</sub>1.8 with VX-548 aleviates acute pain in humans (<span id="ref34556998.37530822" class="cite">Jones <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</span></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Nav1.8 of <em>Rattus norvegicus</em><br /> (Q62968)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.7&tcname=">1.A.1.10.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-sensitive Na<sup>+</sup> channel, Na<sub>v</sub>1.1 or SCN1A (causes epilepsy when mutated) (<span id="ref4424." class="cite">Rusconi <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>).&nbsp; Mutations are associated with a wide range of mild to severe
epileptic syndromes with phenotypes ranging from the relatively mild
generalized epilepsy with febrile seizures to other severe epileptic encephalopathies (<span id="ref34546152.17565594" class="cite">Colosimo <em>et al.</em> 2007</span>),
including myoclonic epilepsy in infancy (SMEI), cryptogenic focal epilepsy (CFE), cryptogenic
generalized epilepsy (CGE) and a distinctive subgroup termed as severe infantile multifocal epilepsy
(SIMFE) (<span id="ref34544725.25590135" class="cite">Ben Mahmoud <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). Mutations can give rise to familial sporadic hemiplegic migranes (<span id="ref34549586.28058944" class="cite">Prontera <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). <span style="font-weight: normal;">An SCN1B variant affects both cardiac-type (NaV1.5) and brain-type 
(NaV1.1) sodium currents and contributes to complex concomitant brain 
and cardiac disorders (<a class="reflink" href="http://www.tcdb.org/search/result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref34552242">Martinez-Moreno <em>et al.</em> 2020</a>). </span>Mice
 null for Scn1b, which encodes NaV beta1 and beta1b subunits, have 
defects in neuronal development and excitability, spontaneous 
generalized seizures, cardiac arrhythmias, and early mortality <span style="font-weight: normal;">(<a class="reflink" href="http://www.tcdb.org/search/result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref34552244">Martinez-Moreno <em>et al.</em> 2020</a>). </span>The Melkersson-Rosenthal Syndrome and Migraine may be associated with <em>SCN1A</em> variants (<span id="ref34556956.37510386" class="cite">Azzar&agrave; <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). A <span style="font-weight: normal;">variant in the SCN1A gene confirms Dravet syndrome in a Moroccan child (<span id="ref34557766.38282049" class="cite">El Mouhi <em>et al.</em> 2024</span>).<br /></span></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Na<sub>v</sub>1.1 of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (P35498)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.8"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.8&tcname=">1.A.1.10.8</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The Voltage-gated Na<sup>+</sup> channel &alpha;-subunit, Na<sub>v</sub>1.6, encoded by the Scn8a gene which when defective gives rise to the ENU-induced neurological mutant ataxia3 which gives rise to ataxia, tremors, and juvenile lethality.&nbsp;&nbsp; 75% identical to 1.A.1.10.7. Nav1.6 is the dendritic, voltage-gated sodium channel (responsible for dendritic excitability (<span id="ref.20466935" class="cite">Lorincz and Nusser, 2010</span>)). Nav1.6 (SCN8A) interacts with microtubule-associated protein (<a href="http://tcdb.org/search/result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref">O'Brien <em>et al., </em>2012</a>). Scorpion alpha&nbsp;toxins bind at receptor site-3 and inhibit channel inactivation, whereas beta toxins bind at receptor site-4 and shift the voltage-dependent activation toward more hyperpolarizing potentials (<span id="ref8891.22694883" class="cite">Gurevitz, 2012</span>).&nbsp; Mutations give rise to epileptic encephalopathy and intellectual disability (<span id="ref10486.24194747" class="cite">O'Brien and Meisler 2013).&nbsp; </span>A gain-of-function mutation gave rise to increased channel activation and infantile epileptic encephalopathy (<span id="ref11282.24874546" class="cite">Estacion <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). Benign familial infantile seizures (BFIS), paroxysmal kinesigenic dyskinesia (PKD), and
their combination - known as infantile convulsions and paroxysmal choreoathetosis (ICCA) - are
related autosomal dominant diseases involving SCN8A (<span id="ref34544909.26677014" class="cite">Gardella <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; Mutations can lead to chronic movement disorder in the mouse (<span id="ref34545046.26807988" class="cite">Jones <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>), and loss of function mutations in humans can lead to intellectual disability without seizures (<span id="ref34548896.28702509" class="cite">Wagnon <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Nav1.6 has been quantitated in mouse brain and proved to be present in 2-fold decreased amounts in epileptic mice (<span id="ref34549418.30724401" class="cite">Sojo <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). SCN8A developmental and epileptic encephalopathy results in intractable seizures including spasms, focal seizures, neonatal status epilepticus, and nonconvulsive status epilepticus (<span id="ref34550450.31675620" class="cite">Kim <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Mutations in the SCN8A gene causes early infantile epileptic encephalopathy (<span id="ref34551002.31715021" class="cite">Pan and Cummins 2020</span>). Amitriptyline is a tricyclic antidepressant that binds to the anesthetic binding site in the &alpha;-subunit of the channel protein (<span id="ref34551414.15275764" class="cite">Wang <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). A heterobivalent ligand (mu-conotoxin KIIIA, which occludes the pore of 
the NaV channels, and an analogue of huwentoxin-IV, a spider-venom 
peptide that allosterically modulates channel gating (TC#8.B.3.1.3)) 
slows ligand dissociation and enhances potency (<span id="ref34552133.33078946" class="cite">Peschel <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Several 
FDA‑approved drugs that are highly correlated with Nav1.6 could be 
candidate drugs for patients with glioma (<span id="ref34556299.37052249" class="cite">Ai <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Clinical and electrophysiological features of SCN8A variants cause episodic or chronic ataxia (<span id="ref34557749.38251463" class="cite">Lyu <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Epilepsy can be due to variants of the SCN8A gene (<span id="ref34557772.38311555" class="cite">Zhang <em>et al.</em> 2024</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Na<sub>v</sub>1.6 of <em>Homo sapiens </em>(Q9UQD0)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.10.9"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.10.9&tcname=">1.A.1.10.9</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The voltage-gated Na<sup>+</sup> channel &alpha;-subunit, Na<sub>v</sub>1.9. It is present in excitable membranes and is resistant to tetrodotoxin and saxitoxin (<span id="ref7942.21670206" class="cite">Bosmans <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>).&nbsp; The mutation, S360Y, makes NaV1.9 channels sensitive to tetrodotoxin and
saxitoxin, and the unusual slow open-state inactivation of Na<sub>V</sub>1.9 is mediated by the isoleucine-phenylalanine-methionine inactivation motif located in the linker connecting domains
III and IV (<span id="ref34543993.25916202" class="cite">Goral <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; Gain-of function mutations can lead to heritable pain disorders, and painful small-fibre neuropathy (<span id="ref34545703.27503742" class="cite">Han <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). It is a threshold channel that regulates action potential firing, and is preferentially expressed in myenteric neurons, the small-diameter dorsal root ganglion (DRG) and trigeminal ganglion neurons including nociceptors. There is a monogenic Mendelian link of Nav1.9 to human pain disorders including<strong> </strong>episodic pain due to a N816K mutation (<span id="ref34550310.31551682" class="cite">Huang <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). The human neuronal sodium channel Nav1.9 is inhibitied by ACEA (arachidonyl-2-chloroethylamide), an analogue of anandamide (<span id="ref34555425.36156289" class="cite">Marchese-Rojas <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p>
<p><strong>.</strong></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Na<sub>v</sub>1.9 of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (Q9UI33)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.1&tcname=">1.A.1.11.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Voltage-sensitive Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel (transports Ca<SUP>2+</sup>, Ba<SUP>2+</sup> and Sr<SUP>2+</sup>) </TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Animals </TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Voltage-sensitive Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel, &#945;-1 chain of <I>Rattus norvegicus</i> </TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.10"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.10&tcname=">1.A.1.11.10</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="1.A.1.11.10"></a></p>
<p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p>Plasma membrane voltage-gated, high affinity Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel, Cch1/Mid1; activated by mating pheromones and environmental stresses; required for growth in low Ca<sup>2+</sup> (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref4069">Locke <em>et al</em>., 2000</a>; <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref4257">Paidhungat and Garrett, 1997</a>). Also essential for tolerance to cold stress and iron toxicity (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref8921">Peiter <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>). Ecm7, (448aas; 4 TMS; TC# 1.H.1.4.6), a member of the PMP-22/EMP/MP20 Claudin superfamily of transmembrane proteins that includes gamma-subunits of voltage-gated calcium channels appears to interact with Mid1 TC# 8.A.41.1.1) and regulate the activity of the Cch1/Mid1 channel (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref9074">Martin <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>). Ecm7p is related to members of TC families 1.H.1, 1.H.2 and 1.A.81. The two indispensable subunits, Cch1 and Mid1 are equivalent to the mammalian <span class="highlight">pore</span>-<span class="highlight">forming</span> &alpha;<sub>1</sub> and auxiliary &alpha;<sub>2</sub> /&delta; subunits, respectively.&nbsp;<span id="ref34546248.27935186" class="cite">Cho <em>et al.</em> 2016</span> screened candidate <span class="highlight">proteins</span> that interact with Mid1 and identified the plasma <span class="highlight">membrane</span> H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase, Pma1 (TC#3.A.3.3.6). Mid1 co-immunoprecipitated with Pma1. At the nonpermiss, and Mid1-EGFP colocalized with Pma1-mCherry at the plasma <span class="highlight">membrane</span>.&nbsp; Using a temperature-sensitive mutant, <em>pma1-10</em>, the <span class="highlight">membrane</span> potential was less negative, and Ca<sup>2+</sup> uptake was lower than in wild-type cells. Thus, Pma1 interacts physically with Cch1/Mid1 Ca<sup>2+</sup> channels to enhance their activity via its H<sup>+</sup>-pumping activity (<span id="ref34546248.27935186" class="cite">Cho <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Yeast</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cch1/Mid1 of <em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em><br /> Cch1 (P50077)<br />Mid1 (P41821)<br />Ecm7p (Q06200)&nbsp;</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.11"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.11&tcname=">1.A.1.11.11</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The Ca<sub>v</sub>1.4 L-type Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel (gene CACNA1F). Mutations resulting in increased activity cause x-linked incomplete congenital stationary night blindness (CSNB2) (<span id="ref4070.15897456" class="cite">Hemara-Wahanui <em>et al.</em>, 2005</span>; <span id="ref4423.17949918" class="cite">Peloquin <em>et al.</em>, 2007</span>).&nbsp; Aland Island eye disease (AIED), also known as Forsius-Eriksson syndrome, is an X-linked
recessive retinal disease characterized by a combination of fundus hypopigmentation, decreased
visual acuity, nystagmus, astigmatism, protan color vision defect, progressive myopia, and defective
dark adaptation. Since the clinical picture of AIED is
quite similar to CSNB2, these disorders are allelic or form a
single entity. Thus, AIED is also caused by CACNA1F gene
mutations (<span id="ref34546187.17525176" class="cite">Jalkanen <em>et al.</em> 2007</span>). Ca<sub>v</sub>1.4 calcium channels play roles in the pathophysiology of psoriasis (<span id="ref34555400.36094228" class="cite">Pelletier and Savignac 2022</span>). Cav1.4 L-type calcium channels are predominantly expressed at the 
photoreceptor terminals and in bipolar cells, mediating neurotransmitter
 release. Mutations in its gene, <em>CACNA1F</em>, can cause congenital stationary night-blindness type 2 (CSNB2). Water wires in both, resting and active channel states have been proposed (<span id="ref34556158.36943941" class="cite">Heigl <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Ca<sub>v</sub>1.4 of <em>Homo sapiens</em><br /> (O60840)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.12"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.12&tcname=">1.A.1.11.12</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>T-type Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel (CACNA1G; Ca<sub>v</sub>3.1d), (&sigma;1G T-type Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel) in developing heart (fetal myocardium (<span id="ref4071.11230107" class="cite">Cribbs <em>et al</em>., 2001</span>)) and elsewhere. Both Ca<sub>v</sub>3.1 and Ca<sub>v</sub>3.2 are permeated by divalent metal ions, such as Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>2+</sup>, and possibly Cd<sup>2+</sup> (<span id="ref6946.20204475" class="cite">Th&eacute;venod, 2010</span>).&nbsp; Ca<sub>V</sub>3.1 channels are activated at low votage and regulate neuronal excitability in the spinal cord (<span id="ref34543569.25255145" class="cite">Canto-Bustos <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).&nbsp; It is regulated by protein kinase C (PKC) and the RanBPM protein (Q96S59) (<span id="ref34544601.18801335" class="cite">Kim <em>et al.</em> 2009</span>).&nbsp; <span>T-type calcium channels belong to the</span> <span>"low-voltage activated (LVA)" group and are strongly blocked by</span><span> mibefradil. A particularity of this type of channel is an opening at</span><span> quite negative potentials and voltage-dependent inactivation. T-type</span><span> channels serve pacemaking functions in both central neurons and cardiac</span><span> nodal cells, and support calcium signaling in secretory cells and</span><span> vascular smooth muscle (<span id="ref34549032.26456284" class="cite">Coutelier <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). The human ortholog is 85% identical to the mouse protein. These channels also </span>determines the angiogenic potential of pulmonary microvascular endothelial cells (<span id="ref34549296.30649917" class="cite">Zheng <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Ca<sub>v</sub>3.1d of <em>Mus musculus</em> (Q9WUT2)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.13"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.13&tcname=">1.A.1.11.13</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Two-pore Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel protein 1, TPC1 (K<sub>m</sub>(Ca<sup>2+</sup>))=50 &micro;M; voltage gated; 461 aas; 12 TMSs) (<span id="ref4254.15111725" class="cite">Hashimoto <em>et al</em>., 2004</span>; <span id="ref4255.15215504" class="cite">Kurusu <em>et al</em>, 2004</span>; 2005). Each TPC subunit contains 12 TMSs that can be divided into two homologous copies of an S1-S6 Shaker-like 6-TMS domain. A functional TPC channel assembles as a dimer. The plant TPC channel is localized in the vacuolar membrane and is also called the SV channel for generating the slow vacuolar (SV) current. Three subfamilies of mammalian TPC channels have been defined - TPC1, 2, and 3 - with the first two being ubiquitously expressed in animals and TPC3 being expressed in some animals, but not in humans. Mammalian TPC1 and TPC2 are localized to endolysosomal membranes (<span id="ref34554996.35879575" class="cite">She <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TPC1 of <em>Oryza sativa</em> (Q5QM84)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.14"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.14&tcname=">1.A.1.11.14</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Voltage-dependent calcium channel, &#945;-1 subunit (1911aas), CyCa&#945;1</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Animals</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>CyCa&#945;1 of <I>Cyanea capillata</I> (O02038)</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.15"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.15&tcname=">1.A.1.11.15</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Neuronal nonselective cation channel, NALCN (forms background leak conductance and controls neuronal excitability; <span id="ref4258.17448995" class="cite">Lu <em>et al.</em>, 2007</span>).&nbsp; It is also found in the pancreatic&nbsp;&beta;-cell (<span id="ref9742.21099296" class="cite">Swayne <em>et al.</em> 2010</span>). NALCN serves as a variable
sensor that responds to calcium or sodium ion flux, depending on whether the total cellular current
density is generated more from calcium-selective or sodium-selective channels (<span id="ref9831.23442378" class="cite">Senatore and Spafford 2013</span>). It functions in a complex with Unc80 (3258 aas; Q8N2C7) and Unc79 (2635 aas; Q9P2D8) (<span id="ref34549031.30167850" class="cite">Bramswig <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Heterozygous de novo NALCN missense variants in the S5/S6 pore-forming 
segments lead to congenital contractures of the limbs and face, 
hypotonia, and developmental delay (<span id="ref34549031.30167850" class="cite">Bramswig <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Overexpression of the NALCN gene ablates allyl isothiocyanate-promoting pain reception by nociceptors (<span id="ref34549832.31147513" class="cite">Eigenbrod <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>NALCN of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (Q6P2S6)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.16"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.16&tcname=">1.A.1.11.16</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>4 domain-type voltage-gated ion channel, &#945;-1 subunit NCA-2 (<span id="ref5193.17825559" class="cite">Jospin <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>) (dependent on Unc-80 (3225aas; CAB042172) for proper localization).</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Animals</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>NCA-2 of <I>Caenorhabditis elegans</I> (Q06AY4)</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.17"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.17&tcname=">1.A.1.11.17</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The high affinity Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel; associates with elongation factor 3 (EF3) to target Cch1/Mid1 to the plasma membrane (<span id="ref5331.18503003" class="cite">Liu and Gelli, 2008</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Fungi</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cch1/Mid1 of <em>Cryptococcus neoformans</em> <br />Cch1 (Q1HHN2)<br />Mid1 (Q5KM96)&nbsp;</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.18"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.18&tcname=">1.A.1.11.18</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The nicotinic acid adenine dinucleotide phosphate (NAADP)- dependent two pore Ca<sup>2+</sup>- channel, TPC3 (<span id="ref7222.19940116" class="cite">Brailoiu <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>).<span id="ref7221.19940116" class="cite"> </span>Phosphoinositides regulate dynamic movement of the S4 voltage sensor in the second repeat in two-pore channel 3 (<span id="ref34554148.34800436" class="cite">Hirazawa <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Each TPC subunit contains 12 TMSs that can be divided into two homologous
 copies of an S1-S6 Shaker-like 6-TMS domain. A functional TPC channel 
assembles as a dimer. The plant TPC channel is localized in the vacuolar
 membrane and is also called the SV channel for generating the slow 
vacuolar (SV) current. Three subfamilies of mammalian TPC channels have 
been defined - TPC1, 2, and 3 - with the first two being ubiquitously 
expressed in animals and TPC3 being expressed in some animals, but not 
in humans. Mammalian TPC1 and TPC2 are localized to endolysosomal 
membranes (<a class="reflink" href="https://tcdb.org/search/result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref34554996">She <em>et al.</em> 2022</a>).</p>

<p><a id="1.A.1.11.19"></a></p>
<p>The phosphoinositide (PI(3,5)P<sub>2</sub>)-activated Na<sup>+ </sup>two pore<sup> </sup>channel-2, TPC2, in endosomes and lysosomes (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref9328">Wang <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>). Previously thought, incorectly, according to Wang et al. 2012, to be a nicotinic acid adenine dinucleotide phosphate (NAADP)-dependent two pore Ca<sup>2+ </sup>channel.&nbsp; TPC2, like TPC1, has a 12 TMS topology (two channel units) (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref7846">Hooper <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>). The two domains of human TPCs can insert into the membrane independently (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref8091">Churamani <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>).&nbsp; <span id="ref10253.23394946" class="cite">Cang <em>et al.</em> (2013), </span>showed that TPC1 and TPC2 together form an ATP-sensitive two-pore Na<sup>+</sup> channel that senses the metabolic state of the cell.&nbsp; The channel complex detects nutrient status, becomes constitutively open
 upon nutrient removal, and controls the lysosome's membrane potential, pH stability, 
and amino acid homeostasis.&nbsp; Essential for Ebola virus (EBOV) host entry. Several inhibitors of TPC2 that
 act in the nM (tetrandrine) or &mu;M (verapamil; Ned19) range block channel activity, prevent Ebola Virus from escaping cell vesicles and may be 
used to treat the disease (<span id="ref34543823.25722412" class="cite">Sakurai <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; TPC2 may transport both Na<sup>+</sup> and Ca<sup>2+</sup> (<span id="ref34543823.25722412" class="cite">Sakurai <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). Lipid-gated monovalent ion fluxes, mediated by TPC1 and TPC2 in mice, regulate endocytic traffic and support immune surveillance. This is in part achieved by catalyzing Na<sup>+</sup> export from visicles derived from the plasma membrane by phagocytosis or pinocytosis, causing contraction and allowing the maintenance of a uniform cell volume (<span id="ref34550803.31806695" class="cite">Freeman <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). This system is important for melanocyte function (<span id="ref34553298.32333855" class="cite">Wiriyasermkul <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TPC2 of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (Q8NHX9)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.2&tcname=">1.A.1.11.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Muscle plasmalemma, voltage-gated, L-type dihydropyridine receptor Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel, &alpha;-1 subunit (DHPR) (Ba<sup>2+</sup> &gt; Ca<sup>2+</sup>), Cav1.1, CACNA1S,CACH1 CDCN1, CACNL1A3 of 1873 aas in the human orthologue.&nbsp; Distinc voltage sensor domains control voltage sensitivity and kinetics of current activation (<span id="ref34545440.27185857" class="cite">Tuluc <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). Rapid changes in the transmembrane potential are detected by the voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel, dihydropyridine receptor (DHPR), embedded in the sarcolemma. DHPR transmits the contractile signal to another Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel, the ryanodine receptor (RyR1), embedded in the membrane of the sarcoplasmic reticulum (SR), which releases a large amounts of Ca<sup>2+</sup> from the SR that initiate muscle contraction (<span id="ref34550846.31950344" class="cite">Shishmarev 2020</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>DHPR of <em>Oryctolagus cuniculus</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.20"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.20&tcname=">1.A.1.11.20</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The voltage-gated Ca<sup>2+</sup>&nbsp;channel, L-type &alpha;-subunit, Eg1-19 regulated by Macoilin (8.A.38.1.2)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Eg1-19 of <em>Caenorhabditis elegans</em>&nbsp;(A8PYS5)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.21"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.21&tcname=">1.A.1.11.21</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-gated L-type Ca<sup>2+</sup>&nbsp;channel, Egl-19, isoform a.
