<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id=""1.A.1.11.22""></a></p>
<p>The phosphoinositide (PI(3,5)P<sub>2</sub>)-activated Na<sup>+ </sup>two pore<sup> </sup>channel-1, TPC1 of endosomes and lysosomes (<a class=""reflink"" href=""result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref9328"">Wang <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>). Previously thought, incorectly, according to Wang et al. (2012), to be an NAADP-activated two pore voltage-dependent calcium channel protein.&nbsp; However, <a class=""reflink"" href=""result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref10253"">Cang <em>et al.</em> (2013), </a>showed that TPC1 and TPC2 (TC# 1.A.1.11.19) together form an ATP-sensitive two-pore Na<sup>+</sup> channel that senses the metabolic state of the cell.&nbsp; The channel complex detects nutrient status, becomes constitutively open
 upon nutrient removal, and controls the lysosome's membrane potential, pH stability, 
and amino acid homeostasis.&nbsp; May be regulated by the HCLS-associated X-1 (HAX-1) protein (<span id=""ref10500.24188827"" class=""cite"">Lam <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>). The cryoEM 3-D structure has been ellucidated (<span id=""ref34547933.29562233"" class=""cite"">She <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). This voltage-dependent, phosphatidylinositol 3,5-bisphosphate (PtdIns(3,5)P<sub>2</sub>)-activated Na<sup>+</sup> channel was solved in both the apo closed state and ligand-bound open state. The channel has a coin-slot-shaped ion pathway in the filter that defines the selectivity of mammalian TPCs. Only the voltage-sensing domain from the second 6-TMS domain confers voltage dependence while endolysosome-specific PtdIns(3,5)P<sub>2</sub> binds to the first 6-TMS domain and activates the channel under conditions of depolarizing membrane potential. Structural comparisons between the apo and PtdIns(3,5)P2-bound structures show the interplay between voltage and ligand activation. These MmTPC1 structures reveal lipid binding and regulation in a 6-TMS voltage-gated channel (<span id=""ref34547933.29562233"" class=""cite"">She <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Tpcn1 of <em>Mus musculus </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.23"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.23&tcname=">1.A.1.11.23</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cch1 calcium channel, alpha subunit; acts with Mid1 (8.A.41.1.7) which is required for function.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Fungi</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Mid1 of <em>Schizosaccharomyces pombe</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.24"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.24&tcname=">1.A.1.11.24</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-sensitive calcium channel of 2693 aas (<span id=""ref10770.24291099"" class=""cite"">Docampo <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>). F<span><span>ound</span><span> </span><span>to</span><span> </span><span>be</span></span><span> essential</span><span> </span><span>for</span><span> </span><span>bloodstream-form</span><span> </span><span><em><span>Trypanosoma</span><span> </span><span>brucei</span></em><span> </span></span><span>through</span><span> </span><span>a</span><span> genome-wide</span><span> </span><span>RNAi</span><span> </span><span>screen (<span id=""ref34548493.29063677"" class=""cite"">Schmidt <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).</span></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Euglenozoa (Protozoa)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Calcium channel of <em>Trypanosoma brucei</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.25"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.25&tcname=">1.A.1.11.25</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Endosomal/lysosomal, two pore Na<sup>+</sup>and Ca<sup>2+</sup>-release channel&nbsp; (Na<sup>+</sup>&gt; Ca<sup>2+</sup>) protein of 816 aas and 12 TMSs, TPC1 or TPCN1 (<span id="ref34546364.28096396" class="cite">Guo <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).&nbsp; Endosomes and lysosomes
are electrically excitable organelles (<span id="ref11092.24776928" class="cite">Cang <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). In a subpopulation of endolysosomes, a brief electrical
stimulus elicits a prolonged membrane potential depolarization spike. The organelles have a depolarization-activated, non-inactivating Na<sup>+</sup> channel (lysoNaV). The
channel is formed by a two-repeat six-transmembrane-spanning (2x6 TMS) protein, TPC1, which represents
the evolutionary transition between 6 TMS and 4x6 TMS voltage-gated channels. Luminal alkalization also
opens lysoNaV by markedly shifting the channel's voltage dependence of activation toward
hyperpolarization. Thus, TPC1 is a voltage-gated Na<sup>+</sup> channel that senses
pH changes and confers electrical excitability to organelles (<span id="ref11092.24776928" class="cite">Cang <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). Essential for Ebola virus (EBOV) host entry.&nbsp; Several inhibitors act in the nM (tetrandrine) or &mu;M (verapamil; Ned19) range to block Na<sup>+</sup> and Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel activity, inhibit virus escape from membrane vesicles and may possibly be used to treat the disease (<span id="ref34543823.25722412" class="cite">Sakurai <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). A cluster of arginine residues in the first domain required for selective voltage-gating of TPC1 map not to the voltage-sensing fourth transmembrane region (S4) but to a cytosolic downstream region (S4-S5 linker). These residues are conserved between TPC isoforms suggesting a generic role in TPC activation. Accordingly, mutation of residues in TPC1 but not the analogous region in the second domain prevents Ca<sup>2+</sup> release by NAADP in intact cells (<span id="ref34547633.29129203" class="cite">Patel <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Dramatic conformational changes in the cytoplasmic domains communicate directly with the VSD during activation (<span id="ref34548709.30190435" class="cite">Kintzer <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).&nbsp; PGRMC1 (the progesterone receptor membrane component1), an ER transmembrane protein that undergoes a unique heme-dependent dimerization, is an interactor of the endosomal two pore channel, TPC1. It regulates ER-endosomal coupling with functional implications for cellular Ca<sup>2+</sup> dynamics (<span id="ref34557497.37866635" class="cite">Gunaratne <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TPC1 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.26"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.26&tcname=">1.A.1.11.26</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Two pore Ca<sup>2+ </sup>&gt; Na<sup>+</sup>, Li<sup>+</sup> or K<sup>+</sup> (non-selective for these three monovalen caions) channel protein of 733 aas and 12 TMSs, TPC1 (<span id="ref34546364.28096396" class="cite">Guo <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). The crystal structure of this vacuolar two-pore channel, a homodimer, has been solved (<span id="ref34544896.26689363" class="cite">Guo <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>) (<span id="ref34545092.26961658" class="cite">Kintzer and Stroud 2016</span>). Activation requires both voltage and cytosolic Ca<sup>2+</sup>.
Ca<sup>2+</sup> binding to the cytosolic EF-hand domain triggers conformational changes coupled to the pair of
pore-lining inner helices from the first 6-TMS domains, whereas membrane potential only activates the
second voltage-sensing domain, the conformational changes of which are coupled to the pair of inner
helices from the second 6-TMS domains. Luminal Ca<sup>2+</sup> or Ba<sup>2+</sup> modulates voltage activation by
stabilizing the second voltage-sensing domain in the resting state and shift voltage activation
towards more positive potentials. The basis for understanding ion permeation,
channel activation, the location of voltage-sensing domains and regulatory ion-binding sites is partially explained by the 3-d structure (<span id="ref34545092.26961658" class="cite">Kintzer and Stroud 2016</span>). Only the second Shaker domain senses voltage (<span id="ref34545359.27270880" class="cite">Jaślan <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). It has a selectivity filter that is passable by hydrated divalent cations (<span id="ref34549168.29916203" class="cite">Demidchik <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).&nbsp;<span id="ref34554456.35210362" class="cite">Dickinson <em>et al.</em> 2022</span> determined structures at different stages along its activation coordinate. These structures of activation intermediates, when compared with the resting-state structure, portray a mechanism in which the voltage-sensing domain undergoes dilation and in-membrane plane rotation about the gating charge-bearing helix, followed by charge translocation across the charge transfer seal. These structures, in concert with patch-clamp electrophysiology, showed that residues in the pore mouth sense inhibitory Ca<sup>2+</sup> and are allosterically coupled to the voltage sensor. These conformational changes provide insight into the mechanism of voltage-sensor domain activation in which activation occurs vectorially over a series of elementary steps (<span id="ref34554456.35210362" class="cite">Dickinson <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).&nbsp; Inhibition of the Akt/PKB kinase increases Nav1.6-mediated currents and neuronal excitability in CA1 hippocampal pyramidal neurons (<span id="ref34554457.35163623" class="cite">Marosi <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TPC1 of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.27"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.27&tcname=">1.A.1.11.27</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-dependent P/Q-type Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel subunit &alpha;1A, CACNA1A (CACH4; CACN3; CACNL1A4) of 2,505 aas. The CACNA1A gene is widely expressed throughout the CNS. The encoding protein is 90% identical to 1.A.1.11.8.&nbsp; Associated with four neurological phenotypes: familial and sporadic hemiplegic migraine type 1 (FHM1, SHM1), episodic ataxia type 2 (EA2), spinocerebellar ataxia type 6 (SCA6) and epileptic encephalopathy with nerve atrophy (<span id="ref34545390.27250579" class="cite">Reinson <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). A gain of function mutation gave symptoms of congenital ataxia, abnormal eye movements and developmental delay with severe attacks of hemiplegic migraine (<span id="ref11117.24836863" class="cite">Garc&iacute;a Segarra <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). Mutations can cause F/SHM with high penitrance (<span id="ref34549586.28058944" class="cite">Prontera <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). CACNA1A variants lead to a wide spectrum of neurological disorders including epileptic or non-epileptic paroxysmal events, cerebellar ataxia, and developmental delay. The variants are either gain of function GOF) or loss of function (LOF) mutations (<span id="ref34552605.33425808" class="cite">Zhang <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). CACNA1A pathogenic variants have been linked to several neurological disorders including severe early onset developmental encephalopathies and cerebellar atrophy. Y1384 variants exhibit differential splice variant-specific effects on recovery from inactivation (<span id="ref34552789.33557884" class="cite">Gandini <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Patients with CACNA1A mutational variants located in the transmembrane region may be at high risk of status epilepticus (<span id="ref34554271.34263451" class="cite">Niu <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Patients with ataxia in the absence of epilepsy can be caused by a CACNA1A mutationand respond to pyridoxine (<span id="ref34555390.28088730" class="cite">Du <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). lamotrigine can be used to treat patients with refractory epilepsy due to calcium channel mutations (<span id="ref34555391.36193018" class="cite">Hu <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>; <span id="ref34555392.10605165" class="cite">De Romanis and Sopranzi 2018</span>). Eupatilin depresses glutamate exocytosis from cerebrocortical synaptosomes by decreasing P/Q-type Ca<sup>2+</sup> channels and synapsin I phosphorylation and alleviates glutamate 
excitotoxicity caused by kainic acid by preventing glutamatergic alterations in 
the mamalian cortex. Thus, eupatilin is a potential therapeutic agent in the treatment of brain 
impairment associated with glutamate excitotoxicity (<span id="ref34555575.36362193" class="cite">Lu <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CACNA1A Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.28"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.28&tcname=">1.A.1.11.28</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-dependent L-type calcium channel subunit &alpha;, VDCC, CCA-1 or CaACNa1S, of 1873 aas and 24 TMSs. <span>Ca<sup>2+</sup> channels containing the alpha-1S subunit play an important role in excitation-contraction coupling in skeletal muscle.&nbsp; They are regulated by dystrophin-1 (<span id="ref34544901.25232639" class="cite">Zhan <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).<br /></span></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>VDCC of <em>Caenorhabditis elegans</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.29"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.29&tcname=">1.A.1.11.29</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-gated calcium channel (VDCC) of 3097 aas and 24 TMSs, Cav7 (<span id="ref34545353.18703378" class="cite">Wheeler and Brownlee 2008</span>). The photoreceptor potential in <em>Chlamydomonas</em> triggers the generation of all or no flagellar Ca<sup>2+</sup> currents that cause membrane depolarization across the eyespot and 
flagella (<span id="ref34556682.37343610" class="cite">Sanyal <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Modulation in membrane potential causes changes in the 
flagellar waveform, and hence, alters the beating patterns of 
<em>Chlamydomonas </em>flagella. The eyespot membrane potential is 
rhodopsin-mediated and is generated by the photoreceptor Ca<sup>2+</sup> current or P-current. However, flagellar Ca<sup>2+</sup> currents are mediated by unidentified voltage-gated calcium (VGCC or 
CaV) and potassium channels (VGKC). The voltage-dependent ion channel 
that associates with ChRs to generate Ca<sup>2+</sup> influx across the 
flagella and its cellular distribution has been 
identified. <span id="ref34556682.37343610" class="cite">Sanyal <em>et al.</em> 2023</span> presented evidence on Chlamydomonas reinhardtii predicting that CrVGCC4 
localizes to the eyespot and flagella and associates 
with channelrhodopsins. Further in silico interactome analysis of 
CrVGCCs suggested that they interact with photoreceptor 
proteins, calcium signaling, and intraflagellar transport components. 
Expression analysis indicated that these VGCCs and their putative 
interactors can be perturbed by light stimuli. Thus, VGCCs in general, and VGCC4 in particular, might be 
involved in the regulation of the phototactic response of <em>Chlamydomonas</em>.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cav7 of <em>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.3&tcname=">1.A.1.11.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-dependent R-type Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel, &alpha;-1E subunit (Cav2.3) (brain Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel type II) (Ca<sup>2+</sup> &gt; Ba<sup>2+</sup>). voltage-gated calcium channel, of 1873 aas and 24 TMSs, Cav1.1; CACNA1S; CACN1; CACH1; CACNL1A3, that gives rise to L-type calcium currents
 in skeletal muscle. Calcium channels containing the alpha-1S subunit 
play an important role in excitation-contraction coupling in skeletal 
muscle via their interaction with RYR1, which triggers Ca<sup>2+</sup> release from the sarcplasmic reticulum and ultimately results in muscle 
contraction. Long-lasting (L-type) calcium channels belong to the 
'high-voltage activated' (HVA) group (<span>Jiang <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). The 3-d structure of a bacterial homologue has been solved </span><span><span>(<span>Jiang <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Mutations in arginly residues in the TMS4 voltage lead to increased leak currently that may be responsible for hypokalaemic periodic paralysis (<span id="ref34552231.33005891" class="cite">Kubota <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). </span></span>Mutations in the voltage sensor domain of CaV1.1, the alpha1S subunit of the L-type calcium channel in skeletal muscle cause hypokalemic periodic paralysis (HypoPP), and these mutations give rise to gating pore currents (<span id="ref34553658.34463712" class="cite">Wu <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). The voltage-gated T-type calcium channel is modulated by kinases and phosphatases (<span id="ref34555934.36766802" class="cite">Sharma <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Advances in Ca<sub>V</sub>1.1 gating, dealing with permeation and voltage-sensing mechanisms, have been reviewed (<span id="ref34555942.36642864" class="cite">Bibollet <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). It is possible to prevent calcium leak associated with short-coupled polymorphic ventricular tachycardia in patient-derived cardiomyocytes (<span id="ref34557290.37740238" class="cite">Sleiman <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Far-infrared ameliorates Pb-induced renal toxicity via voltage-gated calcium channel-mediated calcium influx (<span id="ref34557540.37958813" class="cite">Ko <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Verapamil mitigates chloride and calcium bi-channelopathy in a myotonic dystrophy mouse model (<span id="ref34557662.38165038" class="cite">Cisco <em>et al.</em> 2024</span>).</p>

In the trunk of developing zebrafish embryos, adjacent myotome blocks transmit contractile force via myoseptal junctions (MJs), dynamic structures that connect the actin cytoskeleton of skeletal muscle cells to extracellular matrix components via transmembrane protein complexes in the sarcolemma.<span id="ref34554634.35393618" class="cite"> Rice <em>et al.</em> 2022</span> reported that the endolysosomal ion channel, TPC1, generates highly localized, non-propagating Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients that play a distinct and required role in the capture and attachment of superficial slow skeletal muscle cells (SMCs) at MJs. Disruption of the<em> tpcn1 </em>gene resulted in abnormal MJ phenotypes including SMCs detaching from or crossing the myosepta. TPC1-decorated endolysosomes are dynamically associated with MJs in a microtubule-dependent manner, and&nbsp; attenuating <em>tpcn1 </em>expression or function disrupted endolysosomal trafficking and resulted in an abnormal distribution of beta-dystroglycan (a key transmembrane component of the dystrophin-associated protein complex). Thus, localized TPC1-generated Ca<sup>2+</sup> signals facilitate essential endolysosomal trafficking and membrane contact events, which help form and maintain MJs following the onset of SMC contractile activity (<span id="ref34554634.35393618" class="cite">Rice <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TPC1 of <em>Danio rerio </em>(Zebrafish)<em> (Brachydanio rerio)</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.4&tcname=">1.A.1.11.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The voltage-dependent L-type Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel &alpha;-subunit-1C (L-type Ca<sub>v</sub>1.2), CACNA1C (CACH2, CACN2, CACNL1A1, CCHL1A1) of 2221 aa. Mutations cause Timothy's syndrome, a disorder associated with autism (<span>Splawski <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>). The C-terminus of Ca<sub>v</sub>1.2 encodes a transcription factor (<span>Gomez-Ospina <em>et al.</em>, 2006</span>). Ca<sub>v</sub>1.2 associates with the &alpha;-2, &delta;-1, &beta; and &gamma;&nbsp;subunits (<span>Yang <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>). The CRAC channel activator STIM1 binds and inhibits L-type voltage-gated calcium channel, Ca<sub>v</sub>1.2 (<span>Park <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>).&nbsp; This channel appears to function as the molecular switch for synaptic transmission (<span>Atlas 2013</span>). expression, suggesting that IA-2beta and miR-153 have opposite functional effects on the
secretory pathway (<span>Xu <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp;&nbsp; Co-localizes with Syntaxin-1A in nano clusters at the plasma membrane (<span>Sajman <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). It is a high voltage-activated Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel in contrast to Ca<sub>v</sub>3.3 which is a low voltage-activated Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel (<span>Sanchez-Sandoval <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Nifedipine blocks and potentiates this and other L-type VIC Ca<sup>2+</sup> channels (<span>Wang <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).&nbsp; Cav1.2 is upregulated when STIM1 is deficient (<span id="ref34550416.30088035" class="cite">Pascual-Caro <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). CaV1.2 regulates chondrogenesis during limb development (<span id="ref34550518.31591237" class="cite">Atsuta <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). CACNA1C may be a prognostic predictor of survival in ovarian cancer (<span id="ref34553660.34210324" class="cite">Chang and Dong 2021</span>). Kinase and phosphatase modulation of T-type Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel (TTCC) isoforms Cav3.1, Cav3.2, and 
Cav3.3, are mostly described for roles unrelated to cellular 
excitability (<span id="ref34555934.36766802" class="cite">Sharma <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>), and potential modulations that are 
yet to be explored are also discussed. Palmitoylation of the pore-forming subunit of Ca(v)1.2 controls channel voltage sensitivity and calcium transients in cardiac myocytes (<span id="ref34556899.36745790" class="cite">Kuo <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; A novel binding site between the voltage-dependent calcium channel Ca<sub>V</sub>1.2 subunit and the Ca<sub>V</sub>&beta;2 subunit has been discovered using a new analysis method for protein-protein interactions (<span id="ref34557097.37634019" class="cite">Murakami <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). CACNA1C is one of the top risk genes for schizophrenia; <strong class="sub-title"> </strong> A novel 17-variant block across introns 36-45 of CACNA1C was significantly associated with schizophrenia; a novel 17-variant block across introns 36-45 of CACNA1C was responsible (<span id="ref34557193.37706495" class="cite">Guo <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). A novel binding site has been found between the voltage-dependent calcium channel Ca<sub>V</sub>1.2 subunit and Ca<sub>V</sub>&beta;2 subunit (<span id="ref34557097.37634019" class="cite">Murakami <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CACNA1C of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (2221 aas; Q13936)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.11.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.11.5&tcname=">1.A.1.11.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The voltage-dependent L-type Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel &alpha;-subunit-1H (T-type Ca<sub>v</sub>3.2), CACNA1H (mutations can cause an increased propensity for autism spectrum disorders (ASD) characterized by impaired social interactions, communication skills and restricted and repetitive behaviors) (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref3523">Splawski <em>et al</em>., 2006</a>). It may mediate host cell membrane depolarization and K<sup>+</sup> loss (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref6455">Agarkova <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>; <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref6447">Balss <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>).&nbsp; It is inhibited by Ba<sup>2+</sup> and amantidine. (Reviewed by <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref7056">Thiel <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). </span>The presence of charged amino acids which form dynamic inter- and intra-
subunit salt bridges is crucial for channel activity (<span id="ref34543957.19390850" class="cite">Hertel <em>et al.</em> 2010</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Virus</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Kcv1 K<sup>+</sup><span style="font-family: Times;"> channel of Chlorella virus PBCV-1</span></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.12.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.12.2&tcname=">1.A.1.12.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><em>Acanthocystis turfacea chlorella </em>virus cation, K<sup>+</sup>-preferring, channel, ATCV1 (82aas and 2 TMSs) (<span id="ref6436.19267691" class="cite">Gazzarrini <em>et al.</em>, 2009</span>; <span id="ref34543792.25441713" class="cite">Siotto <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). The difference in open probability between close isoforms is caused by one long closed state in Kcv<sub>S</sub> versus Kcv<sub>NTS</sub>. This state is structurally created in the tetrameric channel by a 
transient, Ser mediated, intrahelical hydrogen bond. The resulting kink 
in the inner transmembrane domain swings the aromatic rings from 
downstream Phenylalanines in the cavity of the channel, which blocks ion flux. The most conserved region of the Kcv protein is the filter, the turret and the pore helix, and the outer and the inner transmembrane domains of the protein are the most variable (<span id="ref34551852.32585987" class="cite">Murry <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Algal virus</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATCV1 (KCVS/KCVNTS) of <em>Acanthocystis turfacea chlorella </em>virus (A7K9J5)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.12.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.12.3&tcname=">1.A.1.12.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The viral K<sup>+ </sup>channel, Kesv of 124 aas.&nbsp; <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref6447">Balss <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>; <span id="ref34543792.25441713" class="cite">Siotto <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).&nbsp; A combination of hydrophobicity and codon usage bias determines sorting of the Kesv K<sup>+</sup> channel protein to either mitochondria or the endoplasmic reticulum (<span id="ref34557169.37578147" class="cite">Engel <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Phaeovirus</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Kesv of <em>Ectocarpus siliculosus </em>virus 1 (Q8QN67)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.12.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.12.4&tcname=">1.A.1.12.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Viral K<sup>+</sup> channel of 96 aas and 2 TMSs, Kcv (<span id="ref34543792.25441713" class="cite">Siotto <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).&nbsp; gates (<span id="ref34544588.26403836" class="cite">Hoffgaard <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Viruses</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Kcv of <em>Paramecium bursaria Chlorella </em>virus</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.12.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.12.5&tcname=">1.A.1.12.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Potassium ion channel protein of 86 aas and 2 TMSs (<span id="ref34548771.30149667" class="cite">Greiner <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>K<sup>+</sup> channel protein of <em>Micromonas pusilla</em> virus SP1</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.12.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.12.6&tcname=">1.A.1.12.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Potassium channel of 101 aas and 2 TMSs (<span id="ref34552219.33003637" class="cite">Kukovetz <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>K+ channel of Rhizochromulina virus RhiV-SA1</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.13.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.13.1&tcname=">1.A.1.13.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>6TMS K<sup>+</sup> channel (<span id="ref34549759.12220501" class="cite">Munsey <em>et al.</em> 2002</span>; <span id="ref2540.12912904" class="cite">Kuo <em>et al</em>., 2003</span>).&nbsp; The <em>kch </em>gene, the only known potassium channel gene in <em>E. coli</em>, has 
the property to express both full-length Kch and its cytosolic domain 
(RCK) due to a methionine at position 240. The RCK domains form an octameric ring structure and regulate the gating of the 
potassium channels after having bound certain ligands. Several different
 gating ring structures have been reported for the soluble RCK domains.&nbsp; The octameric structure of Kch may be composed of two 
tetrameric full-length proteins through RCK interaction (<span id="ref10408.24055821" class="cite">Kuang <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).&nbsp; The RCK domains face the solution, and an RCK octameric gating ring 
arrangement does not form under certain conditions (<span id="ref34549758.25497729" class="cite">Kuang <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Gram-negative bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Kch of <em>E. coli</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.13.10"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.13.10&tcname=">1.A.1.13.10</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>K<sup>+</sup> channel protein with 343 aas and 2 N-terminal TMSs, MjK2.&nbsp; Binding of the MjK2 RCK domain to membranes takes place via an electrostatic interaction with anionic lipid surfaces (<span id="ref34550852.15628846" class="cite">Ptak <em>et al.</em> 2005</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MjK2 of <em>Methanocaldococcus jannaschii (Methanococcus jannaschii)</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.13.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.13.2&tcname=">1.A.1.13.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="1.A.1.13.2"></a></p>
<p>2 TMS ( P-loop) Ca<sup>2+</sup>-gated K<sup>+ </sup> channel, MthK (see <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref2964">Jiang <em>et al</em>., 2002</a> for the crystal structure, and <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref3522">Parfenova <em>et al</em>., 2006</a> for mutations affecting open probability). For the studies of ion permeation and Ca<sup>2+ </sup> blockage, see&nbsp;<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref7655">Derebe <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>. (structures: 3LDD_A and 2OGU_A.).&nbsp;Voltage-dependent K<sup>+ </sup> channels including MthK which lacks a canonical voltage sensor can undergo a gating process known as C-type inactivation, which involves entry into a nonconducting state through conformational changes near the channel's selectivity filter (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref9068">Thomson and Rothberg, 2010</a>). C-type inactivation may involve movements of transmembrane voltage sensor domains. In the absence of Ca<sup>2+</sup>, a single structure in a closed state was observed by cryoEM that was highly flexible with large rocking motions of the gating ring and bending of pore-lining helices (<span id="ref34551294.32269335" class="cite">Fan <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). In Ca<sup>2+</sup>-bound conditions, several open-inactivated conformations were present with the different channel conformations being distinguished by rocking of the gating rings with respect to the transmembrane region. In all conformations displaying open channel pores, the N-terminus of one subunit of the channel tetramer sticks into the pore and plugs it. Deletion of this N terminus led to non-inactivating channels with structures of open states without a pore plug, indicating that this N-terminal peptide is responsible for a ball-and-chain inactivation mechanism (<span id="ref34551294.32269335" class="cite">Fan <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Lipid-protein interactions influence the conformational equilibrium between two states of the channel that differ according to whether a TMS has a kink. Two key residues in the kink region mediate crosstalk between two gates at the selectivity filter and the central cavity, respectively. Opening of one gate eventually leads to closure of the other (<span id="ref34551329.32358584" class="cite">Gu and de Groot 2020</span>). Activation of MthK is exquisitely regulated by temperature (<span id="ref34552438.33274718" class="cite">Jiang <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Euryarchaea</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MthK of <em>Methanothermobacter thermoautotrophicus</em> (<em>Methanobacterium thermoautotrophicum</em>)(O27564)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.13.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.13.3&tcname=">1.A.1.13.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Divalent cation (Ca<sup>2+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Mn<sup>2+</sup>, Ni<sup>2+</sup>)-activated K<sup>+</sup> channel, TuoK (contains a RCK domain) (<span id="ref4214.17588939" class="cite">Parfenova <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>)<br /</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Archaea</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TuoK of <em>Thermoplasma volcanium</em> (Q979Z2)<a href="http://www.uniprot.org/uniprot/Q979Z2"></a></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.13.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.13.4&tcname=">1.A.1.13.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The Biofilm-inducing putative K<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;channel, BikC or YugO (<span id="ref34546717.26503040" class="cite">Prindle <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). BikC has an N<span>-</span>terminal 2 TMS + P-loop channel domain and a C-terminal NADB_Rossman superfamily domain (TrkA domain). <em>YugO</em> is in a two cistronic operon where Mistic (MstX; 9.A.66; <span id="ref7972.21097953" class="cite">Debnath <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>; <span id="ref7973.15731457" class="cite">Roosild <em>et al.</em>, 2005</span>) is encoded by the gene that precedes <em>yugO</em>. Both play a role in biofilm formation, probably by functioning together (<span id="ref34545858.23737939" class="cite">Lundberg <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>; <span id="ref34545859.25297828" class="cite">Marino <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). These K<sup>+</sup> channels in bacterial biofilms provide an active, long-range electrical signalling for cellular communities (<span id="ref34546717.26503040" class="cite">Prindle <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; Metabolic co-dependency gives rise to collective electrical oscillations in biofilms (<span id="ref34546718.26200335" class="cite">Liu <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). This oscillatory electrical signalling, due to periodic release of K<sup>+</sup>, giving rise to K<sup>+</sup> gradients, increasing as swimming cells approach the biofilm that generates the gradiens, allows cells of the same and different speices to find and then incorporate themselves into existing biofilms (<span id="ref34546719.28086091" class="cite">Humphries <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>BikC of <em>Bacillus subtilis</em>&nbsp;(Q795M8)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.13.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.13.5&tcname=">1.A.1.13.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Putative 2 TMS ion channel protein (N-terminus) with C-terminal TrkA_N (NADB Rossman) domain.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Actinobacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Ion channel protein of <em>Streptomyces coelicolor</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.13.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.13.6&tcname=">1.A.1.13.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Ca<sup>2+</sup>-activated K<sup>+</sup> channel, SynCaK.
