Arginine residues in the S4 segment play a role in voltage-sensing (<span id="ref34550839.15635808" class="cite">Chahine <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). Transmembrane and extramembrane regions contribute to thermal stability (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref8445">Powl <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>). Deprotonation of arginines in S4 is involved in NaChBac gating (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref8560">Paldi, 2012</a>).&nbsp;Hinge-bending motions in the pore domain of NaChBac have been reported (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref8706">Barber <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>).&nbsp; The C-terminal coiled-coli stabilizes subunit interactions (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref9152">Mio <em>et al.</em> 2010</a>).&nbsp; Within the 4 TMS voltage sensor, coupling between residues in S1 and S4 determines its resting conformation (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref9714">Paldi and Gurevitz 2010</a>). The conserved asparagine was changed to aspartate, N225D, and this substitution shifted the voltage-dependence of inactivation by 25 mV to more hyperpolarized potentials. The mutant also displays greater thermostability (<span id="ref34547245.28825121" class="cite">O'Reilly <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).&nbsp; Possibly, the side-chain amido group of asn<sub>225</sub> forms one or more hydrogen bonds with different channel elements, and&nbsp; these interactions are important for normal channel function. The T1-tetramerization domain of Kv1.2 (TC# 1.A.1.2.10) rescues expression and preserves the function of a truncated form of the NaChBac sodium channel (<span id="ref34554372.35015304" class="cite">D'Avanzo <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The structure of NaChBac embedded in liposomes has been solved by cryo electron tomography (<span id="ref34556873.37460541" class="cite">Chang <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). The small channel has most of its residues embedded in the membrane, and these are flexible, determining the channel dimensions.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Gram-positive bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>NaChBac of <em>Bacillus halodurans</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.14.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.14.2&tcname=">1.A.1.14.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Voltage-gated Na<sup>+</sup> channel, Na<sub>v</sub>PZ (<span id="ref2796.14665618" class="cite">Koishi <em>et al</em>., 2004</span>)</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Gram-negative bacteria</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Na<sub>v</sub>PZ of <i>Paracoccus zeaxanthinifaciens</i> (CAD24429)</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.14.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.14.3&tcname=">1.A.1.14.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Na<sup>+</sup> channel, Na<sub>v</sub>BP, involved in motility, chemotaxis and pH homeostasis (<span id="ref2981.15243157" class="cite">Ito <em>et al</em>., 2004</span>). Na<sub>v</sub>BP colocalizes with a methyl-accepting chemotaxis protein (MCP) at the cell poles (<span id="ref4575.18048917" class="cite">Fujinami <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>). </TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Bacteria</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Na<sub>v</sub>BP of <i>Bacillus pseudofirmus</i> (AAT21291)</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.14.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.14.4&tcname=">1.A.1.14.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-gated Na<sup>+</sup> channel, VGSC (<span id="ref2796.14665618" class="cite">Koishi <em>et al</em>., 2004</span>; <span id="ref7843.21454659" class="cite">McCusker <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>) Changing the selectivity filter from LESSM to LDDWSD yielded a Calcium-selective channel (<span id="ref7858.21746903" class="cite">Shaya <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Proteobacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>VGSC of <em>Silicibacter pomeroyi</em> (56676695)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.14.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.14.5&tcname=">1.A.1.14.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-gated Na<sup>+</sup> channel, Na<sub>v</sub>Ch or Na<sub>v</sub>Ab. The 3d-structure is known (3ROW; <span>Payandeh <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>; 4MW3A-D; 4+&nbsp;selectivity through partial dehydration of Na<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;via its direct interaction with conserved glutamate side chains).&nbsp;The pore is preferentially occupied by two ions, which can switch between different configurations by crossing low free-energy barriers (<span>Furini and Domene, 2012</span>). <span>Jiang <em>et al.</em> 2018</span> presented high-resolution structures of NavAb with the analogous gating-charge mutations that have similar functional effects as in the human channels that cause hypokalaemic and normokalaemic periodic paralysis.&nbsp;&nbsp;<span id="ref34549988.31353218" class="cite">Wisedchaisri <em>et al.</em> 2019</span> presented a cryo-EM structure of the resting state and a complete 
voltage-dependent gating mechanism via the voltage sensor (VS). The S4 segment of the VS is drawn 
intracellularly, with three gating charges passing through the 
transmembrane electric field. This movement forms an elbow connecting S4
 to the S4-S5 linker, tightens the collar around the S6 activation gate,
 and prevents its opening. This structure supports the classical "sliding
 helix" mechanism of voltage sensing and provides a complete gating 
mechanism for voltage sensor function, pore opening, and activation-gate
 closure based on high-resolution structures of a single sodium channel 
protein (<span id="ref34549988.31353218" class="cite">Wisedchaisri <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).</p>
<p>(see also TC#s 1.A.1.10.4 and 1.A.1.11.32).</p>
<p>The transport reaction catalyzed by Na<sub>v</sub>Ch is:</p>
<p style="text-align: center;">Na<sup>+</sup> (in) ⇌ Na<sup>+</sup> (out)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Na<sub>v</sub>Ch of <em>Arcobacter butzleri&nbsp;</em>(A8EVM5)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.14.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.14.6&tcname=">1.A.1.14.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="1.A.1.14.6"></a></p>
<p>Bacterial voltage-gated sodium channel, Nav.&nbsp; 3-d crystal structures of vaious conformations are known (4P_3A A-D; 4PA7_A-D; 4P9P_A-D. etc.)&nbsp; (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref9093">McCusker <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>).&nbsp; It has its internal cavity accessible to the cytoplasmic surface as a result of a bend/rotation about a central residue in the carboxy-terminal TMS that opens the gate to allow entry of hydrated sodium ions. The molecular dynamics of ion transport through the open conformation has been analyzed (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref9740">Ulmschneider <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>).&nbsp; The C-terminal four helix coiled coil bundle domain couples inactivation with channel opening, depedent on the negatively charged linker region (<span id="ref10425.24051986" class="cite">Bagn&eacute;ris <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>). A NaVSp1-specific S4-S5 linker peptide induced both an increase in NaVSp1 current density and a negative shift in the activation curve, consistent with the S4-S5 linker stabilizing the open state (<span id="ref34551295.32246066" class="cite">Malak <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Nav of <em>Magnetococcus marinus </em>(also called sp. strain MC-1)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.14.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.14.7&tcname=">1.A.1.14.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Tetrameric 6 TMS subunit Na<sup>+</sup> channel protein, Na<sub>V</sub>.&nbsp; Two low resolution cyroEM structures revealed two conformations 
 reconstituted in lipid bilayers (<span id="ref10813.23831224" class="cite">Tsai <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>). Despite a voltage sensor arrangement 
identical with that in the activated form, <span id="ref10813.23831224" class="cite">Tsai <em>et al.</em> 2013</span> observed two distinct pore
 domain structures: a prominent form with a relatively open inner gate, 
and a closed inner-gate conformation similar to the first prokaryotic <span class="highlight">Na<sub>v</sub></span> structure. Structural differences, together with mutational and 
electrophysiological analyses, indicated that widening of the inner gate
 was dependent on interactions among the S4-S5 linker, the N-terminal 
part of S5 and its adjoining part in S6, and on interhelical repulsion 
by a negatively charged C-terminal region subsequent to S6 (<span id="ref10813.23831224" class="cite">Tsai <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Firmicutes</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Nav of <em><span class="highlight">Caldalkalibacillus</span> <span class="highlight">thermarum</span></em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.14.8"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.14.8&tcname=">1.A.1.14.8</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-gated Na<sup>+</sup> channel, Nsv, of 277 aas and 6 TMSs with a structually defined C-terminal regulatory domain (<span id="ref34545412.27106836" class="cite">Miller <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). Voltage-gated sodium channels (Na<sub>V</sub>s) are activated by 
transiting the voltage sensor from the deactivated to the activated 
state. <span id="ref34546620.28400471" class="cite">Tang <em>et al.</em> 2017 </span>identified peptide toxins stabilizing the deactivated 
VSM of bacterial Na<sub>V</sub>s. A cystine knot toxin, called JZTx-27, from the venom of the tarantula <em>Chilobrachys jingzhao</em> proved to be a high-affinity antagonist. JZTx-27 stabilizes the inactive form of the voltage sensor, thereby inhibiting channel activity (<span id="ref34548895.28400471" class="cite">Tang <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Nsv of <em>Bacillus
alcalophilus</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.14.9"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.14.9&tcname=">1.A.1.14.9</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><span class="recommended-name">Bacterial type voltage-activated sodium channel of 718 aas, NaV.<br /></span></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>NaV of <em>Phaeodactylum tricornutum</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.15.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.15.1&tcname=">1.A.1.15.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>6 TMS basolateral tracheal epithelial cell/voltage-gated, small conductance, K<sup>+</sup> &alpha;-chain, KCNQ1, [acts with the KCNE3 &beta;-chain]. Mutations in human K<sub>v</sub>7 genes lead to severe cardiovascular and neurological disorders such as the cardiac long QT syndrome and neonatal epilepsy (<span id="ref4843.18218681" class="cite">Haitin and Attali, 2008</span>). KCNE3 can co-assemble with KCNQ1 (1.A.1.15.6) (<span id="ref7046.20044833" class="cite">Kang <em>et al.,</em> 2010</span>). KCNQ1 regulates insulin secretion in &nbsp;the MIN6 beta-cell line (<span id="ref8772.21426901" class="cite">Yamagata <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>).&nbsp; The S4-S5 linker of KCNQ1 forms a scaffold with S6 controlling gate closure (<span id="ref9034.21059661" class="cite">Labro <em>et al.</em> 2011</span>).&nbsp; <span style="font-family: Times;"><span style="font-family: Times;"><span style="font-family: times;">The KCNQ1 channel is differentially regulated by KCNE1 and KCNE2 (<span id="ref11154.24827085" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>.&nbsp; </span></span></span>Slow-activating channel complexes formed by KCNQ1 and KCNE1 are
essential for human ventricular myocyte repolarization, while constitutively active KCNQ1-KCNE3
channels are important in the intestine. Inherited sequence variants in human KCNE1 and KCNE3 cause
cardiac arrhythmias but by different mechanisms, and each is important for hearing in unique ways (<span id="ref34544576.26410412" class="cite">Abbott 2015</span>). The topology and dynamics of the voltage sensor domain of KCNQ1 reconstituted in a lipid bilayer environment has been studied (<span id="ref34549290.30620191" class="cite">Dixit <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).&nbsp; KCNQ1 (Kv 7.1) alpha-subunits and KCNE1 beta-subunits co-assemble to form channels that conduct the slow delayed rectifier K<sup>+</sup> current (IKs) in the heart. Mutations in either subunit cause long QT syndrome (LQTS), an inherited disorder of cardiac repolarization (<span id="ref34550815.15649981" class="cite">Seebohm <em>et al.</em> 2005</span>). KCNE1 modulates KCNQ1 potassium channel activation by an allosteric mechanism (<span id="ref34552143.33095155" class="cite">Kuenze <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). The membrane electric field regulates the PIP<sub>2</sub>-binding site to gate the KCNQ1 channel (<span id="ref34556451.37192161" class="cite">Mandala and MacKinnon 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Mammals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KCNQ1 K<sup>+</sup> channel of <em>Mus musculus</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.15.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.15.2&tcname=">1.A.1.15.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="1.A.1.15.2"></a></p>
<p>6 TMS voltage-gated K<sup>+ </sup> channel, KCNQ2 or Kv7.2.&nbsp; Mutations cause benign familial neonatal convulsions (BNFC; epilepsy; <span id="ref34545996.27781029" class="cite">Maljevic <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>; <span id="ref34550634.31295832" class="cite">Soldovieri <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).&nbsp; It forms homotetramers or heterotetramers with KCNQ3/Kv7.3) (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref3370">Soldovieri <em>et al</em>., 2006</a>; <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref5130">Uehara <em>et al</em>., 2008</a>)). Like all other Kv7.2 channels, it is activated by phosphatidyl inositol-4,5-bisphosphate and hence can be regulated by various neurotransmitters and hormones (<span id="ref9613.23291709" class="cite">Telezhkin <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).&nbsp; Gating pore currents that go through the gating pores in TMSs1-4 (the voltage sensor) may give rise to peripheral nerve hyperexcitability (<span id="ref11070.24772081" class="cite">Moreau <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). Retigabine and ICA73, two anti-epileptic drugs, act via distinct mechanisms due to interactions with specific residues that underlie subtype specificity of KCNQ channel openers (<span id="ref34545744.27506413" class="cite">Wang <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). A&nbsp; tight spatial and functional relationship between the DAT/GLT-1 transporters and the Kv7.2/7.3 potassium channel immediately readjusts the membrane potential of the neuron, probably to limit the neurotransmitter-mediated neuronal depolarization (<span id="ref34549658.30885609" class="cite">Bartolom&eacute;-Mart&iacute;n <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). E-2-dodecenal from cilantro (<em>Coriandrum sativum</em>) is a potent 
activator and anticonvulsant that binds with an affinity of 60 nM to 
TMS5 in several KCNQ channels including KCNQ2 and 3 (<span id="ref34549992.31311306" class="cite">Manville and Abbott 2019</span>). The activities of Kv7 channels are modulated by polyunsaturated fatty acids (<span id="ref34552399.32595524" class="cite">Larsson <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Anticancer effects of FS48 from salivary glands of <em>Xenopsylla cheopis</em> <em>via</em> its blockage of voltage-gated K<sup>+</sup> channels has been demonstrated (<span id="ref34555814.36692462" class="cite">Xiong <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). The drug, ezogabine restoresnormal activity ,decreasing depressive symptoms in major depressive disorder patients (<span id="ref34556804.33653118" class="cite">Costi <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Both L- and D-isomers of S-nitrosocysteine (CSNO) can bind to the intracellular domain of voltage-gated potassium channels in vitro. CSNO binding inhibits these channels in the carotid body, leading to increased minute ventilation in vivo (<span id="ref34557400.37741264" class="cite">Krasinkiewicz <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Mammals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KCNQ2 K<sup>+</sup> channel of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (O43526)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.15.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.15.3&tcname=">1.A.1.15.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>6 TMS voltage-gated K<sup>+</sup> channel, KCNQ3 or K<sub>v</sub>7.3.&nbsp; Mutations cause benign familial neonatal convulsions (BNFC; epilepsy; <span id="ref34545996.27781029" class="cite">Maljevic <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). Forms homotetramers or heterotetramers with KCNQ2 (<span id="ref3370.16260777" class="cite">Soldovieri <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>; <span id="ref5130.18425618" class="cite">Uehara <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>).&nbsp; Retigabine and ICA73, two anti-epileptic drugs, act via distinct 
mechanisms due to interactions with specific residues that underlie 
subtype specificity of KCNQ channel openers (<span id="ref34545744.27506413" class="cite">Wang <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). Gabapentin at low concentrations is a activator of KCNQ3, KCNQ2/3 and KCNQ5 but not KCNQ2 or KCNQ4 (<span id="ref34548749.30021858" class="cite">Manville and Abbott 2018</span>). At high concentrations it can be inhibitory.&nbsp; A&nbsp; tight spatial and functional relationship between the DAT/GLT-1 
transporters and the Kv7.2/7.3 potassium channel immediately readjusts 
the membrane potential of the neuron, probably to limit the 
neurotransmitter-mediated neuronal depolarization (<span id="ref34549658.30885609" class="cite">Bartolom&eacute;-Mart&iacute;n <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). E-2-dodecenal from cilantro (<em>Coriandrum sativum</em>) is a potent activator and anticonvulsant that binds with an affinity of 60 nM to TMS5 in several KCNQ channels including KCNQ2 and 3 (<span id="ref34549992.31311306" class="cite">Manville and Abbott 2019</span>). Pathogenic variants in KCNQ2 and KCNQ3, paralogous genes encoding Kv7.2 and Kv7.3 voltage-gated K<sup>+</sup> channel subunits, are responsible for early-onset developmental/epileptic disorders characterized by heterogeneous clinical phenotypes ranging from benign familial neonatal epilepsy (BFNE) to early-onset developmental and epileptic encephalopathy (DEE). KCNQ2 variants account for the majority of pedigrees with BFNE, and KCNQ3 variants are responsible for a much smaller subgroup (<span id="ref34552155.33013448" class="cite">Miceli <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). The M240R variant mainly affects the voltage sensitivity, in contrast to previously analyzed BFNE Kv7.3 variants that reduce current density (<span id="ref34552155.33013448" class="cite">Miceli <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Mammals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KCNQ3 K<sup>+</sup> channel of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (O43525)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.15.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.15.4&tcname=">1.A.1.15.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>6 TMS cell volume sensitive, voltage-gated K<sup>+</sup> channel, KCNQ4 or Kv7.4 (mutations cause DFNA2, an autosomal dominant form of progressive hearing loss) (forms homomers or heteromers with KCNQ3) (localized to the basal membrane of cochlear outer hair cells and in several nuclei of the central auditory pathway in the brainstem). Four splice variants form heterotetramers; each subunit has different voltage and calmodulin-sensitivities (<span id="ref4215.17561493" class="cite">Xu <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>).&nbsp; Autosomal dominant mutant forms leading to progressive hearing loss (DFNA2) have been characterized (<span id="ref9094.20966080" class="cite">Kim <em>et al.</em> 2011</span>). Phosphatidylinositol 4,5-bisphosphate (PIP2) and polyunsaturated fatty
acids (PUFAs) impact ion channel function (<span id="ref34546063.27818172" class="cite">Taylor and Sanders 2016</span>). This channel may be present in mitochondria (<span id="ref34550683.31834993" class="cite">Parrasia <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Polyunsaturated fatty acids are modulators of KV7 channels (<span id="ref34552399.32595524" class="cite">Larsson <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). The pathogenicity classification of KCNQ4 missense variants in clinical genetic testing has been described (<span id="ref34554913.35760561" class="cite">Zheng <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). KCNQ4 potassium channel subunit deletion leads to exaggerated acoustic startle reflex in mice (<span id="ref34555966.36789839" class="cite">Maamrah <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Mammals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KCNQ4 K<sup>+</sup> channel of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.15.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.15.5&tcname=">1.A.1.15.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p>The KCNQ5 K<sup>+ </sup> channel (modulated by Zn<sup>2+ </sup>, pH and volume change) (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref4072">Jensen <em>et al</em>., 2005</a>).&nbsp; A triple cysteine module within M-type K<sup>+</sup> channels mediates reciptrocal channel modulation by nitric oxide and reactive oxygen species (<span id="ref9762.23554485" class="cite">Ooi <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>). Gabapentin at low concentrations is a activator of KCNQ3, KCNQ2/3 and KCNQ5 but not of KCNQ2 or KCNQ4 (<span id="ref34548749.30021858" class="cite">Manville and Abbott 2018</span>). At high concentrations, it can be inhibitory.&nbsp; KCNQ5 controls perivascular adipose tissue-mediated vasodilation (<span id="ref34557893.38354270" class="cite">Tsvetkov <em>et al.</em> 2024</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KCNQ5 of <em>Mus musculus</em><br /> (Q9JK45)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.15.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.15.6&tcname=">1.A.1.15.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>K<sup>+</sup> voltage-gated channel, LQT-like subfamily; Kv7.1; KvLQT1. KCNQ1 (regulated by KCNE peptides (TC# 8.A.58) affect voltage sensor equilibrium (<span>Rocheleau and Kobertz, 2007</span>). Almost 300 mutations of KCNQ1 have been identified in patients, and most are linked to the long QT syndrome (LQT1), some in the voltage sensor (<span>Peroz <em>et al</em>., 2008; <span>Eldstrom <em>et al.</em> 2010</span></span>; <span>Qureshi <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>;<span> Ikrar <em>et al.</em> 2008</span>). KCNQ1-KCNE1 complexes may interact intermittently with the actin cytoskeleton via the C-terminal region (<span>Mashanov <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>). The stoichiometry of the KCNQ1 - KCNE1 complex is flexible, with up to four KCNE1 subunits associating with the four KCNQ1 subunits of the channel (<span>Nakajo <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>). A familial mutation in the voltage-sensor of the KCNQ1 channel results in a cardiac phenotype (<span>Henrion <em>et al.</em>, 2012</span>).&nbsp;KCNQ1 regulates insulin secretion in  the MIN6 beta-cell line (<span>Yamagata <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>; <span>Gofman <em>et al.</em>, 2012</span>).&nbsp; Electrostatic interactions of S4 arginines with E1 and S2 contribute to gating movements of S4, but coupling requires the
lipid phosphatidylinositol 4,5-bisphosphate (PIP2) as voltage-sensing domain activation
failed to open the pore in the absence of PIP2 (<span>Zaydman <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>). The D242N mutation causes impaired action potential adaptation to exercise and an increase in heart rate. Moreover, the D242 amino acyl position is involved in the KCNE1-mediated regulation of the voltage-dependence of activation of the K<sub>V</sub>7.1 channel (<span>Moreno <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). The KCNQ1 channel interacts with MinK (KCNE1) to cause pore constriction, generating the slow delayed rectifier (IKs) current in the heart (<span>Jalily Hasani <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). KCNQ1 rescues TMC1 plasma membrane expression but not mechanosensitive channel activity (<span id="ref34549364.30613966" class="cite"><span id="ref34549364.30613966" class="cite">Harkcom <em>et al.</em> 2019</span></span>). Activation of the neuronal Kv7/KCNQ/M-current represents an attractive therapeutic strategy for treatment of hyperexcitability-related neuropsychiatric disorders such as epilepsy, pain, and depression, and channel openers for treatment of antiepilepsy have been developed (<span id="ref34549767.31063701" class="cite">Zhang <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). The relationship between mutation locations in KCNQ1, which is a major gene in long QT syndrome (LQTS), and phenotype has been analyzed and used for risk stratification (<span id="ref34550227.29439887" class="cite">Yagi <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). The proximal C-terminal regions of KCNQ1 and KCNE1 participate in a physical and functional interaction during channel opening that is sensitive to perturbation (<span id="ref34550630.31834838" class="cite">Chen <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Retigabine analogs are activators of Kv7 channels (<span id="ref34550999.31815462" class="cite">Ostacolo <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). People with borderline QTc prolongations were carriers of KCNQ1 mutations in TMSs 2 and 5, leading to haploinsufficiency, and they are potentially at risk of developing drug-induced arrhythmia (<span id="ref34551751.15194462" class="cite">Gouas <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). Collision induced unfolding differentiates functional variants of the KCNQ1 voltage sensor domain (<span id="ref34552095.32960579" class="cite">Fantin <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). The activated KCNQ1 channel promotes a fibrogenic response in hereditary gingival fibromatosis via clustering and activation of Ras (<span id="ref34552452.33381870" class="cite">Gao <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). QT syndrome (LQTS) increases the risk of life-threatening arrhythmia in young individuals with structurally normal hearts. It may involve sixteen genes such as the KCNQ1, KCNH2, and SCN5A (<span id="ref34553107.31565860" class="cite">Lin <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). The human KCNQ1 voltage sensing domain (VSD) has been studied in lipodisq nanoparticles by electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) spectroscopy (<span id="ref34553341.32130007" class="cite">Sahu <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Structural mechanisms for the activation of the human cardiac KCNQ1 channel by electro-mechanical coupling enhancers have been reviewed (<span id="ref34555937.36763058" class="cite">Ma <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The pathogenicity of KCNQ1 variants&nbsp; using zebrafish as a model has been reviewed (<span id="ref34556310.36898499" class="cite">Cui <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Phosphatidyl-inositol-4,5-bisphosphate (PIP<sub>2</sub>) is required for coupling between the voltage sensor and the pore of the potassium voltage-gated K<sub>V</sub>7 channel family, especially the K<sub>V</sub>7.1 channel. Modulation of the I(KS) channel by PIP<sub>2</sub> requires two binding sites per monomer (<span id="ref34556421.37082259" class="cite">Kongmeneck <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; Divergent regulation of the KCNQ1/E1 channel can be accomplished by targeted recruitment of protein kinase A to distinct sites on the channel complex (<span id="ref34557385.37650513" class="cite">Zou <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; Rare missense variants with a clear phenotype of Long QT Syndrome, type 1 (LQTS) have a high likelihood to be present within the pore and adjacent TMSs (S5-Pore-S6) (<span id="ref34557605.37897496" class="cite">Novelli <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; LHFPL5 is a key element in force transmission from the tip link to the hair cell mechanotransducer channel (<span id="ref34557711.38194445" class="cite">Beurg <em>et al.</em> 2024</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KCNQ1 of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (P51787)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.15.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.15.7&tcname=">1.A.1.15.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Ion&nbsp; channel transporter of 296 aas and 5 putative TMSs.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Ion channel of <em>Mycoplasma</em> sp. Pen4</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.15.8"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.15.8&tcname=">1.A.1.15.8</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KCNQ1 of 647 aas and 6 TMSs.&nbsp;<span id="ref34554508.35176427" class="cite">Xiong <em>et al.</em> 2022</span> characterized KCNQ1 which functions in shell biomineralisation of pearl oyster, <em>Pinctada fucata</em> martensii.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KCNQ1 of <em>Pinctada fucata</em> martensii</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.16.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.16.1&tcname=">1.A.1.16.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The small conductance Ca<sup>2+</sup>-activated K<sup>+</sup> channel, SkCa2, Sk2 or Kcnn2 (not inhibited by arachidonate) (activated by three small organic molecules, the 1-EBIO and N5309 channel enhancers and the DCEBIO channel modulation (<span>Pedarzani <em>et al</em>., 2005</span>)).&nbsp; It is inhibited by protonation of outer pore histidine residues (<span>Goodchild <em>et al.</em>, 2009</span>). The same is true for SK3 (K(Ca) 2.3 (Q9UGI6)). Regulates endothelial vascular function (<span>Sonkusare <em>et al.</em>, 2012</span>).&nbsp; Distinct subcellular mechanisms enhance the surface membrane expression by its interacting proteins, &alpha;-actinin 2 (TC# 8.A.66.1.3) and filamin A (TC# 8.A.66.1.4) (<span>Zhang <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). SK channel activators can compensate for age-related changes of the
autorhythmic functions of the cerebellum (<span>Karelina <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). SK2 proteins are more abundant in Purkinje cells than in the ventricular myocytes of normal rabbit ventricles (<span>Reher <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Apamin inhibits and isoproterenol activates this and other SK (KCNN) channels, and activation by isoproterenol is sex-dependent (<span>Chen <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).&nbsp; Diverse interactions between KCa and TRP channels integrate cytoplasmic Ca<sup>2+</sup>, oxidative, and electrical signaling affecting cardiovascular physiology and pathology (<span id="ref34549995.30893836" class="cite">Behringer and Hakim 2019</span>). This channel may be present in mitochondria (<span id="ref34550683.31834993" class="cite">Parrasia <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). A non-neuronal hSK3 isoform has a dominant-negative effect on hSK3 currents (<span id="ref34554284.14976403" class="cite">Wittekindt <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). Medicinal plant products can interact with SKCa (<span id="ref34554622.35273505" class="cite">Rajabian <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Varients may cause conformational changes that alter the ability of the protein to modulate ion channel activities (<span id="ref34557032.37510285" class="cite">d'Apolito <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Mammals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SkCa2 of <em>Homo sapiens </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.16.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.16.2&tcname=">1.A.1.16.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The intermediate conductance, Ca<sup>2+</sup>-activated K<sup>+</sup> channel, IKCa, Kcnn4, SK4, Sk4, Smik, Ik1 hIK1, IKCa or KCa3.1, also called the Gardos channel, of 543 aas and 6 TMSs. It is inhibited by 1 &mu;M arachidonate which binds in the pore (<a>Hamilton <em>et al</em>., 2003</a>)).&nbsp;Nucleoside diphosphate kinase B (NDPK-B) activates KCa3.1 via histidine phosphorylation, resulting in receptor-stimulated Ca<sup>2+</sup>&nbsp;flux and T cell activation (<a>Di <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). It regulates endothelial vascular function (<a>Sonkusare <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>).&nbsp; Tissue-specific expression of splice variants of the orthologous rat KCNN4 protein have been reported (<a>Barmeyer <em>et al.</em> 2010</a>).&nbsp; Residues involved in gating have been identified (<span>Garneau <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). It is also present in the inner mitochondrial membrane where increases of
mitochondrial matrix [Ca<sup>2+</sup>] cause mtKCa3.1 opening, thus linking inner membrane K<sup>+</sup> permeability and transmembrane potential to Ca<sup>2+</sup> signalling (<span>De Marchi <em>et al.</em> 2009</span>). KCa3.1 (IKCa) channels are expressed in CA1 hippocampal pyramidal
cells and contribute to the slow afterhyperpolarization that regulates spike accommodation (<span>Turner <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).  SK channel activators can compensate for age-related changes of the
autorhythmic functions of the cerebellum (<span>Karelina <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). The activation mechanism has been revealed by the cryoEM structure of the SK4-calmodulin complex (<span>Lee and MacKinnon 2018</span>).&nbsp; It is responsible for hyperpolarization in some tumor cells (<span id="ref34550167.31545164" class="cite">Lazzari-Dean <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Mutations are linked to dehydrated hereditary stomatocytosis (xerocytosis) (<span id="ref34550219.26178367" class="cite">Andolfo <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). This channel is present in mitochondria (<span id="ref34550683.31834993" class="cite">Parrasia <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). KCNN4 promotes the progression of lung adenocarcinoma by activating the AKT and ERK signaling pathways (<span id="ref34553042.33896824" class="cite">Xu <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). KCa3.1 channels in human microglia link extracellular ATP-evoked Ca<sup>2+</sup> transients to changes in membrane conductance with an inflammation-dependent mechanism, and suggests that during brain inflammation, the KCa3.1-mediated microglial response to purinergic signaling may be reduced (<span id="ref34553780.33588880" class="cite">Palomba <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Both IK(Ca) and BK(Ca) regulate cell volume in human glioblastoma cells (<span id="ref34556840.37431808" class="cite">Michelucci <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Mammals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>hIK1 of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (AAC23541)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.16.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.16.3&tcname=">1.A.1.16.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Small conductance calcium-gated potassium (SK) channel. Three charged residues in TMS S6 of SK channels near the inner mouth of the pore collectively control the conductance and rectification through an electrostatic mechanism (<span id="ref8086.21422289" class="cite">Li and Aldrich, 2011</span>). The SK channel inhibitors NS8593 and UCL1684 prevent the development of atrial fibrillation via atrial-selective inhibition of sodium channel activity (<span id="ref34551639.32453071" class="cite">Burashnikov <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SK of <em>Drosophila melanogaster</em> (Q7KVW5)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.16.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.16.4&tcname=">1.A.1.16.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><pre>Small conductance Ca<sup>2+</sup>-activated K<sup>+</sup> channel, KCNL-2 of 672 aas.  Plays a role in the rate of egg laying (<span id="ref10506.24040423" class="cite">Chotoo <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).</pre></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KCNL-2 of <em>Caenorhabditis elegans</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.16.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.16.5&tcname=">1.A.1.16.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plasma membrane small conductance calcium-activated K<sup>+</sup> channel of 396 aas, TSKCa; probably involved in immunoregulation (<span id="ref34543599.19481607" class="cite">Cong <em>et al.</em> 2009</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals (fish)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TSKCa of <em>Psetta maxima</em> (Turbot) (<em>Pleuronectes maximus</em>)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.16.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.16.6&tcname=">1.A.1.16.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Small conductance calcium-activated K<sup>+</sup> channel, KCNN1 or SK, of 543 aas and 6 TMSs. It is a druggable risk factor for opioid use disorder (OUD) (<span id="ref34556801.36171425" class="cite">Kember <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SK of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.16.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.16.7&tcname=">1.A.1.16.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Small conductance calcium-activated potassium channel protein 3 of 736 aas and 6 TMSs, SK3 or KCNN3. It forms <span>a voltage-independent potassium channel, activated by intracellular calcium (<span id="ref34552528.31155282" class="cite">Bauer <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).
 Activation is followed by membrane hyperpolarization and is thought to 
regulate neuronal excitability by contributing to the slow component of 
synaptic after-hyperpolarization. The channel is blocked by apamin.</span> Contrary to its bradycardic effect in the sinus node, blockage of its current by apamin accelerates ventricular automaticity and causes repeated, nonsustained, ventricular tachycardia in normal ventricles. Ryanodine receptor 2 blockage reversed the apamin effects on ventricular automaticity (<span id="ref34552527.30445170" class="cite">Wan <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Dextran sodium sulfate treatment causes loss of transient relaxation due to downregulation of SK3 channels and may increase contractile responses due to increased Ca<sup>2+</sup> sensitization of smooth muscle cells via protease-activated receptor_1 (PAR1) [TC# P25116; TC# 9.A.14.13.37] activation (<span id="ref34555546.36202914" class="cite">Sung <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The specificity of Ca<sup>2+</sup>-activated K<sup>+</sup> channel modulation in atherosclerosis and aerobic exercise training has been discussed (<span id="ref34555662.36368871" class="cite">Mokelke <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KCNN3 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.16.8"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.16.8&tcname=">1.A.1.16.8</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Small conductance plasma membrane calcium-activated potassium channel of 553 aas and 6 TMSs (<span id="ref34552960.33878308" class="cite">Paul <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>BK channel of <em>Leishmania donovani</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.17.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.17.1&tcname=">1.A.1.17.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The archaeal voltage-regulated K<sup> </sup> channel, KvAP (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref2365">Ruta <em>et al</em>., 2003</a>). X-ray and solution structures are available. The latter shows phospholipid interactions with the isolated voltage sensor domain (<span id="ref9108.20851706" class="cite">Butterwick and MacKinnon 2010</span>; <span id="ref11067.24413055" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). The gating-charge arginine in TMS4 of the voltage sensor forms part of the helical hairpin "paddle", and it moves 15-20 &Aring; through the membrane to open the pore (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref7606">Ruta <em>et al.</em>, 2005</a>). The orientation and depth of insertion of the voltage-sensing S4 helix has been determined (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref8092">Doherty <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>).&nbsp;A synthetic S6 segment derived from the KvAP channel self-assembles, permeabilizes lipid vesicles, and exhibits ion channel activity in bilayer lipid membrane (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref8558">Verma <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>).&nbsp; Thus the gating mechanism combines structural rearrangements and electric-field remodeling ( <span id="ref11067.24413055" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).&nbsp; KvAP has been reconstituted in Giant Unilamellar Vesicles (GUVs) (<span id="ref34543720.25650630" class="cite">Garten <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; TMS4 (S4) which senses voltage also promotes membrane insertion of the voltage-sensor domain (<span id="ref34545881.27694387" class="cite">Mishima <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). KvAP has a configuration consistent with a water channel, possibly underlying the conductance of protons, and other cations, through voltage-sensor domains (<span id="ref34546829.17012321" class="cite">Freites <em>et al.</em> 2006</span>). The structural dynamics of the paddle motif loop in the activated conformation of the KvAP voltage sensor have been studied from biophysical standpoints (<span id="ref34550273.31547975" class="cite">Das <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). The S4 alpha-helix, which is straight in the experimental crystal structure solved under depolarized conditions (Vm approximately 0), breaks into two segments when the cell membrane is hyperpolarized (Vm &lt;&lt; 0) and reversibly forms a single straight helix following depolarization (Vm = 0) ((<span id="ref34552096.32850956" class="cite">Bignucolo and Bern&egrave;che 2020</span>). The outermost segment of S4 translates along the normal to the membrane, bringing new perspective to previously paradoxical accessibility experiments that were initially thought to imply the displacement of the whole VSD across the membrane. The breakage of S4 under (hyper)polarization could be a general feature of Kv channels with a non-swapped topology. The surface charge of the membrane does not significantly affect the topology and structural dynamics of the sensor loop in membranes (<span id="ref34552603.33529577" class="cite">Das and Raghuraman 2021</span>). The dynamic variability of the sensor loop is preserved in both zwitterionic (POPC) and anionic (POPC/POPG) lipid membranes. The lifetime distribution analysis for the NBD-labelled residues by the maximum entropy method (MEM) demonstrates that, in contrast to micelles, the membrane environment not only reduces the relative discrete population of sensor loop conformations, but also broadens the lifetime distribution peaks. The local curvature of cellular membranes acts as a driving force for the targeting of membrane-associated proteins to specific membrane domains, as well as a sorting mechanism for transmembrane proteins, as demonstrated for KvAP (<span id="ref34554505.35122737" class="cite">Kluge <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Archaea</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KvAP of&nbsp;<em>Aeropyrum pernix </em>(Q9YDF8)&nbsp;</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.17.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.17.2&tcname=">1.A.1.17.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Voltage-gated K<sup>+ </sup>channel, Kv (<span id="ref6448.19029373" class="cite">Santos <em>et al.</em>, 2008</span>).<br /</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Kv of<em> Listeria monocytogenes</em> (Q8Y5K1)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.18.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.18.1&tcname=">1.A.1.18.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The two-pore domain potassium channel, TRESK-1 (<a>Czirjak <em>et al</em>., 2004</a>) (provides the background K<sup>+</sup> current in mouse DRG neurons (<a>Dobler <em>et al</em>., 2007</a>)) TRESK (TWIK-related spinal cord K<sup>+</sup> channel) is reversibly activated by the calcium/calmodulin-dependent protein phosphatase, calcineurin. <a>Czirjak <em>et al</em>. 2008</a> reported that 14-3-3 proteins directly bind to the intracellular loop to TRESK and control the kinetics of the calcium-dependent regulation. Cloxyquin (5-chloroquinolin-8-ol) is an activator (<a>Wright <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>).&nbsp; A cytoplasmic loop binds tubulin (<a>Enyedi <em>et al.</em> 2014</a>). Channel activity is modified by phosphorylation (inactive) and dephosphorylation (active) of the unusually long intracellular loop between the 2nd and 3rd TMS (<span>Lengyel <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).&nbsp; The distal short intracellular C-terminal region (iCtr) following the fourth TMS is a major positive determinant of TRESK function (<span id="ref34556384.37084812" class="cite">Debreczeni <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TRESK-1 of <em>Mus musculus</em> (AAQ91836)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.18.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.18.2&tcname=">1.A.1.18.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TRESK-2 or potassium channel subfamily K member 18 of 348 aas and 6 TMSs. TRESK-2 is a functional member of the K(2P) channel family and contributes to the background K<sup>+</sup> conductance in many types of cells (<span id="ref34551934.15123670" class="cite">Kang <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). The distal short intracellular C-terminal region (iCtr) following the 
fourth TMS is a major positive determinant of TRESK function (<span id="ref34556384.37084812" class="cite">Debreczeni <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; Cloxyquin activates hTRESK by allosteric modulation of the selectivity filter (<span id="ref34556969.37464013" class="cite">Schreiber <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TRESK-2 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.19.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.19.1&tcname=">1.A.1.19.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Alkalinizatioin-activated Ca<sup>2+</sup>-selective channel, sperm-associated cation channel, CatSper, required for male fertility and the hyperactivated motility of spermatozoa (<span>Kirichok <em>et al.</em> 2006</span>). These channels require auxiliary subunits, CatSper&beta;, &gamma; and &delta; for activity (<a>Chung <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>).&nbsp; The primary channel protein is CatSper1 (<a>Liu <em>et al</em>., 2007</a>), and it
may be a target for immunocontraception (<span>Li <em>et al.</em> 2009</span>). CatSper channels have been reported to regulate
sperm motility (<span>Vicente-Carrillo <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Sperm competition is selective for a disulfide-crosslinked macromolecular architecture. CatSper channel opening occurs in response to pH, 2-arachidonoylglycerol, and mechanical force. A flippase function is hypothesized, and a source of the concomitant disulfide isomerase activity is found in CatSper-associated proteins beta, delta and epsilon (<span>Bystroff 2018</span>). More recently, it has been reported that rotational motion and rheotaxis of human sperm do not require functional CatSper channels or transmembrane Ca<sup>2+</sup> signaling (<span id="ref34550923.31957048" class="cite">Schiffer <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Instead, passive biomechanical and hydrodynamic processes may enable sperm rolling and rheotaxis, rather than calcium signaling mediated by CatSper or other mechanisms controlling transmembrane Ca<sup>2+</sup> flux. The Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel CatSper is not activated by cAMP/PKA signaling but directly affected by chemicals used to probe the action of cAMP and PKA (<span id="ref34551968.32703901" class="cite">Wang <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). The cation channel of sperm (CatSper) is essential for sperm motility and fertility. CatSper comprises the pore-forming proteins CATSPER1-4 and multiple auxiliary subunits, including CATSPERbeta, gamma, delta, epsilon, zeta, and EFCAB9.&nbsp;<span id="ref34553640.34225353" class="cite">Lin <em>et al.</em> 2021</span> reported the cryo-EM structure of the CatSper complex isolated from mouse sperm. CATSPER1-4 conform to the conventional domain-swapped voltage-gated ion channel fold, following a counterclockwise arrangement. The auxiliary subunits CATSPERbeta, gamma, delta and epsilon - each of which contains a single transmembrane segment and a large extracellular domain - constitute a pavilion-like structure that stabilizes the entire complex through interactions with CATSPER4, 1, 3 and 2, respectively. The EM map revealed several previously uncharacterized components, exemplified by the organic anion transporter SLCO6C1.&nbsp;<span id="ref34553640.34225353" class="cite">Lin <em>et al.</em> 2021</span> named this channel-transporter ultracomplex the CatSpermasome. The assembly and organizational details of the CatSpermasome lay the foundation for the development of CatSpermasome-related treatments for male infertility and non-hormonal contraceptives. CatSper is a target for inhibition, for use in male contraception, causing inhibition of sperm motility (<span id="ref34556370.37141450" class="cite">Mariani <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). A CUG-initiated CATSPER&theta; functions in the CatSper channel assembly and serves as a checkpoint for flagellar trafficking (<span id="ref34557248.37725640" class="cite">Huang <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Mammals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CatSper of <em>Homo sapiens</em> <br /> CatSper1 (Q96P76)<br /> CatSper3 (Q86XQ3)<br /> CatSper4 (Q7RTX7)<br /> CatSper&beta; (Q9H7T0)<br />CatSper&gamma; (Q6ZRH7)<br />CatSper&delta; (Tmem146) (Q86XM0) <br />CatSper&epsilon; (B1AQM6)<br />CatSperzeta (Q9NTU4)<br />EFCAB9 (A8MZ26)<br />Slco6C1 (mouse; Q3V161)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.19.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.19.2&tcname=">1.A.1.19.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Sperm-associated cation channel, CatSper2 with 530 aas and 6 TMSs; it is a voltage-gated calcium channel that plays a central role in 
calcium-dependent physiological responses essential for successful 
fertilization, such as sperm hyperactivation, acrosome reaction and 
chemotaxis towards the oocyte (<span id="ref34557424.21412338" class="cite">Str&uuml;nker <em>et al.</em> 2011</span>). The CatSper calcium channel is indirectly activated by extracellular 
progesterone and prostaglandins following the sequence: progesterone 
&gt; PGF1-alpha = PGE1 &gt; PGA1 &gt; PGE2 &gt;&gt; PGD2 (<span id="ref34557425.21412339" class="cite">Lishko <em>et al.</em> 2011</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Mammals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CatSper2 of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (26051223)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.19.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.19.3&tcname=">1.A.1.19.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>

<p><a id="1.A.1.19.3"></a></p>
<p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="1.A.1.19.3"></a></p>
<p>Alkalinization-activated, Ca<sup>2+</sup>-selective cation channel of sperm 1, CatSper1, required for male fertility and the hyperactivated motility of spermatozoa. These channels require auxiliary subunits, CatSper &beta;, &gamma; and &delta;&nbsp;for activity (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref9081">Chung <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>; <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref4236">Liu <em>et al</em>., 2007</a>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CatSper of <em>Mus musculus</em><br /> CatSper1 (Q91ZR5)<br /> CatSper3 (Q86XQ3)<br /> CatSper4 (Q8BVN3)<br /> CatSper&beta; (Q8C0R2)<br />CatSper&gamma; (C6KI89)<br />CatSper&delta; (Tmem146) (E9Q9F6)&nbsp;</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.2.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.2.1&tcname=">1.A.1.2.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-sensitive K<sup>+</sup> channel (P<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>/P<sub>K</sub><sup>+</sup> &asymp; 0.1)&nbsp;Shaker and Shab K<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;channels are blocked by quinidine (<span id="ref7909.20547671" class="cite">Gomez-Lagunas, 2010</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Shab11 of <em>Drosophila melanogaster </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.2.10"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.2.10&tcname=">1.A.1.2.10</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-gated K<sup>+</sup> channel, chain A, Shaker-related, K<sub>v</sub>1.2 or KCNA2 (Crystal structure known, <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref5630">Long <em>et al</em>., 2007</a>; <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref9712">Chen <em>et al.</em> 2010</a>). It functions with the auxiliary subunit, Iv&beta;1.2; 8.A.5.1.1) (<span id="ref11011.19713757" class="cite">Peters <em>et al.</em> 2009</span>).&nbsp; Delemotte et al. (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref7451">2010</a>) described the effects of sensor domain mutations on molecular dynamics of K<sub>v</sub>1.2.&nbsp; The Sigma 1 receptor (Q99720; Sigma non-opioid intracellular receptor 1) interacts with K<sub>v</sub>1.2 to shape neuronal and behavioral responses to cocaine (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref10252">Kourrich <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>).&nbsp; Amino acid substitutions cause Shaker to become heat-sensing (opens with increasing temperature as for TrpV1) or cold-sensing (opens with decreasing temperature as for TrpM8) (<span id="ref11313.25156949" class="cite">Chowdhury <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).&nbsp; The Shaker Kv channel was truncated after the 4th transmembrane
helix S4 (Shaker-iVSD) which showed altered gating kinetics and formed a
cation-selective ion channel with a strong preference for protons (<span id="ref34545890.27710769" class="cite">Zhao and Blunck 2016</span>).&nbsp; Direct axon-to-myelin linkage by abundant K<sub>V</sub>1/Cx29 (TC# 1.A.24.1.12) channel 
interactions in rodent axons supports the idea of an electrically active
 role for myelin in increasing both the saltatory
conduction velocity and the maximal propagation frequency in mammalian 
myelinated axons (<a class="reflink" href="../../search/result.php?tc=1.A.24#ref34546289"><span id="ref34546289.26763782" class="cite">Rash <em>et al.</em> 2016</span></a>). A cryoEM structure (3 - 4 &Aring; resolution; paddle chimeric channel; closed form) in nanodiscs has been determined (<span id="ref34548742.30109985" class="cite">Matthies <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Possible gating mechanisms have been discussed (<span id="ref34548750.30231473" class="cite">Kariev and Green 2018</span>; <span id="ref34549225.30498243" class="cite">Infield <em>et al.</em> 2018)</span>. Pathogenic variants in KCNA2, encoding the voltage-gated potassium channel Kv1.2, have been identified as the cause for an evolving spectrum of neurological disorders. Affected individuals show early-onset developmental and epileptic encephalopathy, intellectual disability, and movement disorders resulting from cerebellar dysfunction (<span id="ref34553045.33802230" class="cite">D&ouml;ring <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). In addition, individuals with a milder course of epilepsy, complicated hereditary spastic paraplegia, and episodic ataxia have been reported. Biophysical properties of a delayed rectifier K<sup>+</sup> current can contribute to its role ingenerating spontaneous myogenic activity (<span id="ref34553252.33946248" class="cite">Hu <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). The local curvature of cellular membranes acts as a driving force for 
the targeting of membrane-associated proteins to specific membrane 
domains, as well as a sorting mechanism for transmembrane proteins, as 
demonstrated for the chimeric channel, Kv1.2/2.1; KvChim induces a strong positive membrane curvature (<span id="ref34554505.35122737" class="cite">Kluge <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). 2-Aminoethoxydiphenyl borate (2-APB) has inhibitory effects on three K<sub>V</sub>1 channels, Kv1.2, Kv1.3 and Kv1.4 (<span id="ref34555807.36677928" class="cite">Zhao <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Voltage-gated K<sup>+</sup> channels have two distinct gates that regulate ion flux: the activation gate (A-gate) formed by the bundle crossing of the S6 transmembrane helices and the slow inactivation gate in the selectivity filter. These two gates are bidirectionally coupled. <span id="ref34556597.37212728" class="cite">Szanto <em>et al.</em> 2023 </span>suggested that the coupling between the A-gate and the slow inactivation gate is mediated by rearrangements in the S6 segment. S6 rearrangements are consistent with a rigid rod-like rotation of S6 around its longitudinal axis upon inactivation.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>K<sub>v</sub>1.2 of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (P16389)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.2.11"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.2.11&tcname=">1.A.1.2.11</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-gated K<sup>+</sup> channel, Shab-related, K<sub>v</sub>2.1 or KCNB1 (858aas) The crystal structure is known (<span id="ref5630.18004376" class="cite">Long <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>). Rat K<sub>v</sub>2.1 and K<sub>v</sub>2.2 (long) are colocalized in the somata and proximal dendrites of cortical pyramidal neurons and are capable of forming functional heteromeric delayed rectifier channels. The delayed rectifer currents, which regulate action potential firing, are encoded by heteromeric K<sub>v</sub>2 channels in cortical neurons (<span id="ref7226.20202934" class="cite">Kihira <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>). Phosphorylation by AMP-activated protein kinase regulates membrane excitability (<span id="ref8078.22006306" class="cite">Ikematsu <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>).&nbsp;Functional interactions between residues in the S1, S4, and S5 domains of Kv2.1 have been identified (<span id="ref8663.21455829" class="cite">Bocksteins <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>).&nbsp; Missense variants in the ion channel domain and loss-of-function variants in this domain and the C-terminus cause neurodevelopmental disorders, sometimes with seizures (<span id="ref34547200.28806457" class="cite">de Kovel <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).&nbsp; Kv2.1 channels consist of two types of alpha-subunits: (1) electrically-active Kcnb1 alpha-subunits and (2) silent or modulatory alpha-subunits plus beta-subunits that, similar to silent alpha-subunits, regulate electrically-active subunits (<span id="ref34550189.31512327" class="cite">Jędrychowska and Korzh 2019</span>). It plays a role&nbsp; in neurodevelopmental disorders, such as epileptic encephalopathy. The N- and C-terminal domains of the alpha-subunits interact to form the cytoplasmic subunit of hetero-tetrameric potassium channels. Kcnb1-containing channels are involved in brain development and reproduction. Modification of Kv2.1 K<sup>+</sup> currents is mediated by the silent Kv10 subunits (<span id="ref34552808.15046870" class="cite">Vega-Saenz de Miera 2004</span>). The clinical expression of KCNB1 encephalopathy is variable (<span id="ref34554120.34877642" class="cite">P&uacute;a-Torrej&oacute;n <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Variants of KCNB1, located in the S1 segment, may be associated with a milder outcome of seizures (<span id="ref34555586.36257979" class="cite">Hiraide <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Altered neurological and neurobehavioral phenotypes have been observed in a mouse model of the recurrent KCNB1 -p.R306C voltage-sensor variant (<span id="ref34556348.37034689" class="cite">Kang <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). A point mutation (M340I) in K<sub>V</sub>2.1 may cause severe and treatment-resistant obsessive-compulsive disorder (trOCD) (<span id="ref34556394.37131580" class="cite">Chen <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>; <span id="ref34556393.37116083" class="cite">Ji <em>et al.</em> 2023).<br /></span>It may play a role in Parkinson's Disease (<span id="ref34556493.37089789" class="cite">Zhou <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>K<sub>v</sub>2.1/K<sub>v</sub>2.2 of <em>Homo sapiens</em><br />(Q14721)<br />(Q92953)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.2.12"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.2.12&tcname=">1.A.1.2.12</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-gated K<sup>+</sup> channel, Kv1.1 or KCNA1.<span style="text-decoration: underline;"><strong> </strong></span>It is palmitoylated, modulating voltage sensing (<span id="ref34550125.15837928" class="cite">Gubitosi-Klug <em>et al.</em> 2005</span>). It is regulated by syntaxin (TC family 8.A.91) through dual action on channel surface expression and conductance (<span>Feinshreiber <em>et al.</em>, 2009</span>).<span> Defects cause </span>episodic ataxia type 1 (EA1), an autosomal dominant K<sup>+</sup> channelopathy accompanied by short
attacks of cerebellar ataxia and dysarthria (<span>D'Adamo <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>; <span id="ref34551957.32705822" class="cite">Yuan <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Direct axon-to-myelin linkage by abundant K<sub>V</sub>1/Cx29 channel 
interactions in rodent axons supports the idea of an electrically active
 role for myelin in increasing both the saltatory
conduction velocity and the maximal propagation frequency in mammalian 
myelinated axons (<a>Rash <em>et al.</em> 2016</a>). Kv1.1 is present in bull sperm where it is necessary for normal sperm progressive motility, percent capacitated spermatozoa (B-pattern) and the acrosome reaction (<span>Gupta <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Gating induces large aqueous volumetric remodeling (<span id="ref34548708.30038023" class="cite">D&iacute;az-Franulic <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). <span id="ref34551409.32316562" class="cite">Paulhus <em>et al.</em> 2020</span> have reviewed the pathology of mutants in this protein and showed that epilepsy or seizure-related variants tend to cluster in the S1/S2 transmembrane domains and in the pore region of Kv1.1, whereas EA1-associated variants occur along the whole length of the protein, but variants at the C-terminus are more likely to suffer from seizures and neurodevelopmental disorders (<span id="ref34551957.32705822" class="cite">Yuan <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Mutation in KCNA1 has been identified that impairs voltage sensitivity (<span id="ref34552633.33466780" class="cite">Imbrici <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Altering expression of the genes encoding Kv1.1, Piezo2, and TRPA1 regulate the response of mechanosensitive muscle nociceptors (<span id="ref34553923.34566566" class="cite">Nagaraja <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Genetic variants have expanded the functional, molecular, and pathological diversity of <em>KCNA1</em> channelopathies (<span id="ref34556530.37240170" class="cite">Paulhus and Glasscock 2023</span>). Carbamazepine suppresses the impaired startle response and brain hyperexcitability in kcna1a(-/-) zebrafish but had no effect on the seizure frequency in Kcna1(-/-) mice, suggesting that this EA1 zebrafish model might better translate to humans than rodents (<span id="ref34557495.37209379" class="cite">Dogra <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Kv1.1 of <em>Homo sapiens</em> <strong></strong>(Q09470)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.2.13"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.2.13&tcname=">1.A.1.2.13</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-gated K<sup>+ </sup>channel subfamily C member 3,KCNC3 or Kv3.3. It is negatively modulated by protein kinase C (<span id="ref7811.18539595" class="cite">Desai <em>et al.</em>, 2008</span>).&nbsp;Phosphorylation of Kv3.3 by PKC may allow neurons to maintain action potential height during stimulation at high frequencies, and therefore contributes to stimulus-induced changes in the intrinsic excitability of neurons such as those of the auditory brainstem (<span id="ref7811.18539595" class="cite">Desai <em>et al.</em>, 2008</span>).&nbsp; N-glycosylation impacts the sub-plasma membrane localization and activity of Kv3.1b-containing channels, and N-glycosylation processing of Kv3.1b-containing channels contributes to neuronal excitability (<span id="ref34548928.30271698" class="cite">Hall <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Spinocerebellar ataxia (SCA), a genetically heterogeneous disease characterized by cerebellar ataxia, involves the abnormal expansion of repeat sequences as well as the mutation of K<sup>+</sup> and Ca<sup>2+</sup> channel genes (<span id="ref34550814.31907387" class="cite">Tada <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). A missense mutation in Kcnc3 causes hippocampal learning deficits in mice (<span id="ref34555096.35881790" class="cite">Xu <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Kv3.3 of<em> Homo sapiens</em> (Q14003)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.2.14"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.2.14&tcname=">1.A.1.2.14</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-gated delayed rectifier K<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;channel, Kv1 of the octopus. Control of transport (pore) function&nbsp; by the voltage sensor may involve more than one mechanism (<span id="ref34546645.28360219" class="cite">Tomczak <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).&nbsp; The silent (non transporting) behaviour of Kv6.3 in the ER is caused by the C-terminal part of its sixth transmembrane domain that causes ER retention (<span id="ref34549863.16096342" class="cite">Ottschytsch <em>et al.</em> 2005</span>).&nbsp; De novo missense variants in KCNH1 encoding Kv10.1 are responsible for two clinically recognisable phenotypes: Temple-Baraitser syndrome (TBS) and Zimmermann-Laband syndrome (ZLS) (<span id="ref34554822.33811134" class="cite">Aubert Mucca <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The clinical overlap between these two syndromes suggests that they belong to a spectrum of KCNH1-related encephalopathies. Affected patients have severe intellectual disability (ID) with or without epilepsy, hypertrichosis and distinctive features such as gingival hyperplasia and nail hypoplasia/aplasia (<span id="ref34554822.33811134" class="cite">Aubert Mucca <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Kcng3 or K<sub>v</sub>10.1of <em>Rattus norvegicus </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.2.18"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.2.18&tcname=">1.A.1.2.18</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Potassium voltage-gated channel subfamily F member 1 (Voltage-gated potassium channel subunit Kv5.1) (kH1)</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Animals</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>KCNF1 of <em>Homo sapiens </em></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.2.19"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.2.19&tcname=">1.A.1.2.19</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The voltage-gated K<sup>+</sup> channel subfamily D member 3, KCND3 or K<sub>v</sub>4.3.&nbsp; Mutations cause spinocerebellar ataxia type 19 (<span id="ref9676.23280838" class="cite">Duarri <em>et al.</em> 2012</span>). Modulation of the <em>Drosophila</em> Shaw2 Kv channel by 1-alkanols and 
inhaled anesthetics correlates with the involvement of the S4-S5 
linker and C-terminus of S6, consistent with stabilization of the 
channel's closed state (<span id="ref34553251.23031574" class="cite">Zhang <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Shaw2 of <em>Drosophila melanogaster </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.2.20"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.2.20&tcname=">1.A.1.2.20</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Shaker K<sup>+</sup> channel, Shk-1, Shk1, Kv1 of 536 aas and 6 TMSs.&nbsp; <span>Mediates
 the voltage-dependent potassium ion permeability of excitable 
membranes. It plays a role in repolarization and in regulating the

K<sub>v</sub>1.3 associates with the sequence similar (&gt;80%) K<sub>v</sub>1.5 protein in macrophage forming heteromers that like K<sub>v</sub>1.3 homomers are r-margatoxin sensitive (<span id="ref3743.17038323" class="cite">Vicente <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>).
