The K<sub>v</sub>1.3 mitochondrial potassium channel is involved in apoptotic signalling in lymphocytes (<span id="ref8153.20114030" class="cite">Gulbins <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>). Interactions between the C-terminus &nbsp;of K<sub>v</sub>1.5 and K<sub>v</sub>&beta; regulate pyridine nucleotide-dependent changes in channel gating (<span id="ref8453.22426702" class="cite">Tipparaju <em>et al.</em>, 2012</span>).&nbsp; Intracellular trafficking of the K<sub>V</sub>1.3 K<sup>+ </sup>channel is regulated by the pro-domain of a matrix metalloprotease (<span id="ref9678.23300077" class="cite">Nguyen <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).&nbsp; Direct binding of caveolin regulates K<sub>v</sub>1 channels and allows association with lipid rafts (<span id="ref34545120.26931497" class="cite">P&eacute;rez-Verdaguer <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). Addtionally, Na<sub>v</sub>Beta1 interacts with the voltage sensing domain (VSD) of K<sub>v</sub>1.3 through W172 in the transmembrane segment to modify the gating process (<span id="ref34546646.28349975" class="cite">Kubota <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). During insertion of Kv1.3, the extended N-terminus of the second&nbsp;&alpha;-helix, S2, inside the ribosomal tunnel is converted into a helix in a transition that depends on the nascent peptide sequence at specific tunnel locations (<span id="ref34546725.28478285" class="cite">Tu and Deutsch 2017</span>). The microRNA, mmumiR449a, reduced the mRNA expression levels of transient receptor potential cation channel subfamily A member 1 (TRPA1), and calcium activated potassium channel subunit alpha1 (KCNMA1) and increased the level of transmembrane phosphatase with tension homology (TPTE) in the DRG cells (<span id="ref34546726.28498403" class="cite">Lu <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). This channel is regulation by sterols (<span id="ref34547287.28863820" class="cite">Balajthy <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Loss of function causes atrial fibrillation, a rhythm disorder characterized by chaotic electrical activity of cardiac atria (<span id="ref34547373.16772329" class="cite">Olson <em>et al.</em> 2006</span>). The N-terminus and S1 of Kv1.5 can attract and coassemble with the rest of the channel (i.e. Frag(304-613)) to form a functional channel independently of the S1-S2 linkage (<span id="ref34548762.30121572" class="cite">Lamothe <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). This channel may be present in mitochondria (<span id="ref34550683.31834993" class="cite">Parrasia <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Kv1.3 plays an essential role in the immune response mediated by leukocytes and is functional at both the plasma membrane and the inner mitochondrial membrane. Plasma membrane Kv1.3 mediates cellular activation and proliferation, whereas mitochondrial Kv1.3 participates in cell survival and apoptosis (<span id="ref34554675.35402277" class="cite">Capera <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Kv1.3 uses the TIM23 complex to translocate to the inner mitochondrial membrane. This mechanism is unconventional because the channel is a multimembrane spanning protein without a defined N-terminal presequence. Transmembrane domains cooperatively mediate Kv1.3 mitochondrial targeting involving the cytosolic HSP70/HSP90 chaperone complex as a key regulator of the process (<span id="ref34554675.35402277" class="cite">Capera <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Kv1.3 homomers and Kv1.3/Kv1.5 heteromers of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (P22001)<br /> Kv1.3 (P22001)<br /> Kv1.5 (P19024)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.2.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.2.5&tcname=">1.A.1.2.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-gated K<sup>+</sup> channel subfamily D, member 2, Kv4.2 or KCND2, in neurons and muscle; forms complexes with auxiliary subunits and scaffolding proteins via its N-terminus, influencing trafficking, temperature-sensitivity and gating (<span id="ref9617.23291429" class="cite">Radicke <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).These subunits are (1) dipeptidyl-peptidase-like type II transmembrane proteins typified by DPPX-S (e.g., protein 6, P42658; 865 aas, TC#8.A.51),
and (2) cytoplasmic Ca<sup>2+</sup> binding proteins known as K<sup>+</sup> channel interacting proteins (KChIPs; TC#8.A.82.2.2; Q6PIL6) (<span id="ref34543896.19441798" class="cite">Seikel and Trimmer 2009</span>).&nbsp; The C-terminus interacts with KChIP2 to influence gating, surface trafficking and gene expression (<span id="ref3744.16820361" class="cite">Han <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>; <span id="ref5317.18458082" class="cite">Schwenk <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>). KChIPs (250 aas for mouse KChIP4a; Q6PHZ8) are homologous to domains in NADPH oxidases (5.B.1).&nbsp;Heteropoda toxin 2 (P58426; PDB 1EMX; TC#8.B.5.2.2) interactions with Kv4.3 and Kv4.1 give rise to differences in gating modifications (<span id="ref8597.21540294" class="cite">DeSimone <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>).&nbsp; Mutations cause autism and seizures due to a slowing of channel inactivation (<span id="ref10908.24501278" class="cite">Lee <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).&nbsp; The stoichiometry of Kv4.2 and DPP6 is 4:4 (<span id="ref34545154.18364354" class="cite">Soh and Goldstein 2008</span>). Neferine, an isoquinoline alkaloid from plants, inhibits Kv4.3 channels, probably by blocking the open state (<span id="ref34545704.26592512" class="cite">Wang <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). SUMOylating (derivatizing with a small ubiquitin-like modifier) at two distinct sites on Kv4.2 increases surface expression and decreases current amplitude (<span id="ref34550047.31213982" class="cite">Welch <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Modulation of voltage-gated potassium (Kv) channels by auxiliary subunits is central to the physiological function of channels in the brain and heart. Native Kv4 tetrameric channels form macromolecular ternary complexes with two auxiliary beta-subunits-intracellular Kv channel-interacting proteins (KChIPs) and transmembrane dipeptidyl peptidase-related proteins (DPPs)-to evoke rapidly activating and inactivating A-type currents, which prevent the backpropagation of action potentials (see above).&nbsp;<span id="ref34553862.34552243" class="cite">Kise <em>et al.</em> 2021</span> investigated the modulatory mechanisms of Kv4 channel complexes, reporting cryo-EM structures of the Kv4.2-DPP6S-KChIP1 dodecameric complex, the Kv4.2-KChIP1 and Kv4.2-DPP6S octameric complexes, and Kv4.2 alone. The structure of the Kv4.2-KChIP1 complex revealed that the intracellular N terminus of Kv4.2 interacts with its C-terminus that extends from the S6 gating helix of the neighbouring Kv4.2 subunit. KChIP1 captures both the N and the C terminus of Kv4.2. Thus, KChIP1 prevents N-type inactivation and stabilizes the S6 conformation to modulate gating of the S6 helices within the tetramer. Unlike the reported auxiliary subunits of voltage-gated channel complexes, DPP6S interacts with the S1 and S2 helices of the Kv4.2 voltage-sensing domain, which suggests that DPP6S stabilizes the conformation of the S1-S2 helices. DPP6S may therefore accelerate the voltage-dependent movement of the S4 helices. KChIP1 and DPP6S do not directly interact with each other in the Kv4.2-KChIP1-DPP6S ternary complex. Thus, two distinct modes of modulation contribute in an additive manner to evoke A-type currents from the native Kv4 macromolecular complex (<span id="ref34553862.34552243" class="cite">Kise <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Kv4.2 of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (Q9NZV8)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.2.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.2.6&tcname=">1.A.1.2.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a></a></p>
<p>Voltage-gated K<sup>+ </sup> channel, Shaker. Shaker and Shab K<sup>+ </sup> channels are blocked by quinidine (<a>Gomez-Lagunas, 2010</a>). Also regulated by unsaturated fatty acids (<a>B&ouml;rjesson and Elinder, 2011</a>).&nbsp; TMSs 3 and 4 comprise the voltage sensor paddle (<span>Xu <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).&nbsp; Partially responsible for action potential repolarization during synaptic transmission (<span>Ford and Davis 2014</span>). Shaker K<sup>+</sup> channels can be mutated in S4 to create an analogous "omega" pore (<span>Held <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). The NMR structure of the isolated Shaker voltage-sensing domain in LPPG micelles has been reported (<span id="ref34549983.31301804" class="cite">Chen <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Substituting the first S4 arginine with a smaller amino acid opens a high-conductance pathway for solution cations in the Shaker K<sup>+</sup> channel at rest. The cationic current does not flow through the central K<sup>+</sup> pore and is influenced by mutation of a conserved residue in S2, suggesting that it flows through a protein pathway within the voltage-sensing domain (<span id="ref34550627.15694325" class="cite">Tombola <em>et al.</em> 2005</span>). The current can be carried by guanidinium ions, suggesting that this is the pathway for transmembrane arginine permeation. <span id="ref34550627.15694325" class="cite">Tombola <em>et al.</em> 2005</span> proposed that when S4 moves, it ratchets between conformations in which one arginine after another occupies and occludes to ions in the narrowest part of this pathway. Specific resin acids activate and open voltage-gated channels&nbsp; dependent on its exact binding dynamics (<span id="ref34552914.33683319" class="cite">Silver&aring; Ejneby <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Charge-voltage curves of a Shaker potassium channel are not hysteretic at steady state (<span id="ref34555907.36692860" class="cite">Cowgill and Chanda 2023</span>). <em>shaker</em> is a critical sleep regulator in <em>Drosophila </em>(<span id="ref34557241.15858564" class="cite">Cirelli et al. 2005</span>).</p>

Involved in chemotaxis, mechanotransduction and locomotion (<span id="ref3967.12533541" class="cite">Bianchi <em>et al</em>., 2003</span>)). KVS-1 and KVS-2 are homologous; MPS-1 is  member of the MiNK family (8.A.10). KVS-1/MPS-1 association involves hydrophobic forces (<span id="ref6659.17604313" class="cite">Wang and Sesti, 2007</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KVS-1 (&alpha;)/ MPS-1 (MiRP&beta;) of <em>Caenorhabditis elegans</em><br /> KVS-1 (&alpha;) (Q86GI9)<br /> MPS-1 (MiRP&beta;) (Q86GJ0)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.2.9"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.2.9&tcname=">1.A.1.2.9</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Brain-specific regulatory &alpha;-chain homologue that coassembles with other &alpha;-subunits to form active heteromultimeric K<sup>+</sup> channels of unique kinetic properties, Kv2.3r. The functional expression of this regulatory &alpha;-subunit represents a novel mechanism without precedents in voltage-gated channels, which contributes to the functional diversity of K<sup>+</sup> channels (<span id="ref3968.9169526" class="cite">Castellano <em>et al</em>., 1997</span>). Beta subunits regulate the response of human Kv4.3 to protein kinae C phosphorylation and provide a potential mechanism for modifying the response of ion conductance to alpha-adrenergic regulation in vivo (<span id="ref34547351.28919864" class="cite">Abbott 2017</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Kv2.3r of <em>Rattus norvegicus</em> (P97557)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.20.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.20.1&tcname=">1.A.1.20.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>K<sup>+ </sup> voltage-gated ether-a-go-go-related channel, H-ERG (KCNH2; Erg; HErg; Erg1, Kv11.1) subunit K<sub>v</sub>11.1 (long QT syndrome type 2) (<a>Gong <em>et al</em>., 2006</a>; <a>Chartrand <em>et al.</em> 2010; </a><a>McBride <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>). Selective expression of HERG and Kv2 channels influences proliferation of uterine cancer cells (<span id="ref34551645.15202000" class="cite">Suzuki and Takimoto 2004</span>). H-ERG forms a heteromeric K<sup>+ </sup> channel regulating cardiac repolarization, neuronal firing frequency and neoplastic cell growth (<a>Szab&oacute; <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>).  Oligomerization is due to N-terminal interactions between two splice variants, hERG1a and hERG1b (<a>Phartiyal <em>et al</em>., 2007</a>). Structure function relationships of ERG channel activation and inhibition have been reviewed (<a>Durdagi <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). Interactions between the N-terminal domain and the transmembrane core modulate hERG K<sup> </sup> channel gating (<a>Fern&aacute;ndez-Trillo <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>). The marine algal toxin azaspiracid is an open state blocker (<a>Twiner <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>). Verapamil blocks channel activity by binding to Y<sub>652</sub> and F<sub>656</sub> in TMS S<sub>6</sub> (<span>Duan <em>et al.</em> 2007</span>).&nbsp; Hydrophobic interactions between the voltage sensor and the channel domain mediate inactivation (<span>Perry <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>), but voltage sensing by the S4 segment can be transduced to the channel gate in the absence of
physical continuity between the two modules (<span>L&ouml;rinczi <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; Mutations give rise to long QT syndrome (<span>Osterbur <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; Polyphenols such as caffeic acid, phenylethyl ester (CAPE) and curcumin inhibit by modification of gating, not by blocking the pore (<span>Choi <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>). Potassium ions
can inhibit tumorigenesis through inducing apoptosis of hepatoma cells by upregulating potassium
ion transport channel proteins HERG and VDAC1 (<span>Xia <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).&nbsp; Incorrectly folded hERG can be degraded by Bag1-stimulated Trc-8-dependent proteolysis (<span>Hantouche <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). The S1 helix regulates channel activity. Thus, S1 region mutations reduce both the action potential repolarizing current passed by Kv11.1 channels in cardiac myocytes, as well as the current passed in response to premature depolarizations that normally helps protect against the formation of ectopic beats (<span>Phan <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Interactions of beta1 integrins with hERG1 channels in cancer cells stimulate distinct signaling pathways that depended on the conformational state of hERG1 (<span>Becchetti <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). ERG1 is sensitive to the alkaloid, ginsenoside 20(S) Rg3 which alters the gating of hERG1 channels by interacting with and stabilizing the voltage sensor domain in an activated state (<span>Gardner <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Channels split at the S4-S5 linker, at the intracellular S2-S3 loop, and at the extracellular S3-S4 loop are fully functional channel proteins (<span>de la Pe&ntilde;a <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). IKr is the rapidly activating component of the delayed rectifier potassium current, the ion current largely responsible for the repolarization of the cardiac action potential. Inherited forms of long QT syndrome (LQTS) in humans are linked to functional modifications in the Kv11.1 (hERG) ion channel and potentially life threatening arrhythmias. hERG1b affects the generation of the cardiac Ikr and plays an important role in cardiac electrophysiology (<span>Perissinotti <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). X-ray crystallography and cryoEM have revealed features of the "nonswapped" transmembrane architecture, an "intrinsic ligand," and small hydrophobic pockets off a pore cavity. Drug block and inactivation mechanisms are discussed (<span id="ref34550406.31669064" class="cite">Robertson and Morais-Cabral 2019</span>). It forms a complex with &beta;-integrin (TC#9.B.87.1.25) and NHE1 (TC# 2.A.36.1.13) (<span id="ref34551799.32587517" class="cite">Iorio <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Cardiotoxicity is caused mainly by the inhibition of human ether-a-go-go related gene (hERG) channel protein which leads to a life-threatening condition known as cardiac arrhythmia and is due to probable collapse of the pore. (<span id="ref34552670.33494645" class="cite">Koulgi <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Transmembrane hERG channel currents have been measured based on solvent-free lipid bilayer microarrays (<span id="ref34552694.33478052" class="cite">Miyata <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). A computational method for identifying an optimal combination of existing drugs to repair the action potentials of SQT1 ventricular myocytes has been published (<span id="ref34553531.34383746" class="cite">J&aelig;ger <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Ginsenoside Rg3 may be a promising cardioprotective agent against vandetanib-induced QT interval prolongation through targeting hERG channels (<span id="ref34554061.34659631" class="cite">Zhang <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Insight has been obtained into the potassium currents of hERG (<span id="ref34556437.37110748" class="cite">Guidelli 2023</span>). Two novel KCNH2 mutations contribute to long QT syndrome (<span id="ref34557670.38181028" class="cite">Owusu-Mensah <em>et al.</em> 2024</span>). Channel activity is affected by moxifloxacin, terfenadine, arsenic, pentamidine, erythromycin, and sotalol (<span id="ref34557780.38334240" class="cite">Goineau <em>et al.</em> 2024</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals (Mammals)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>H-ERG of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (Q12809)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.20.10"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.20.10&tcname=">1.A.1.20.10</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The KCNH1 K+ channel protein of 989 aas and 6 TMSs.&nbsp; <span id="ref34557334.36285361" class="cite">Tian <em>et al.</em> 2023</span> expanded the phenotypic spectrum of KCNH1 and explored the correlations between epilepsy and molecular sub-regional locations.&nbsp; They found two novel missense variants of KCNH1 in three individuals with isolated FS/epilepsy. Variants caused a spectrum of epileptic disorders ranging from a benign form of genetic isolated epilepsy/FS to intractable form of epileptic encephalopathy. The genotypes and variant locations helped explain the phenotypic variation of patients with KCNH1 variants (<span id="ref34557334.36285361" class="cite">Tian <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KCNH1 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.20.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.20.2&tcname=">1.A.1.20.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Erg2 (Kv11.2; KCNH6) K<sup>+</sup> channel with slowly activating delayed rectifier (expressed only in the nervous system) (<span id="ref4073.9390998" class="cite">Shi <em>et al</em>., 1997</span>). The human ortholog of 994 aas and 6 TMSs (Q9H252) is 86% identical to the rat protein. <span class="highlight">KCNH6</span> in humans and mice plays a key role in insulin secretion and glucose hemostasis (<span id="ref34551206.30590050" class="cite">Yang <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Erg2 of <em>Rattus norvegicus</em><br /> (O54853)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.20.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.20.3&tcname=">1.A.1.20.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Erg3, Kv11.3, Eag3, KCNH7, K<sup>+</sup> channel with a large transient current at positive potentials (expressed only in the nervous system) (<span id="ref4073.9390998" class="cite">Shi <em>et al</em>., 1997</span>). Erg3-mediated suppression of neuronal intrinsic excitability prevents seizure generation (<span id="ref34548871.30016551" class="cite">Xiao <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). The human ortholog (Q9NS40) is 1196 aas long with 6 TMSs and is 94% identical to the rat protein.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Erg3 of <em>Rattus norvegicus</em><br /> (O54852)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.20.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.20.4&tcname=">1.A.1.20.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>K<sup>+</sup> voltage-gated channel, rEAG1; K<sub>v</sub>10.1; rat ether a go-go channel 1 (962 aas). Blocked by Cs<sup>+</sup>, Ba<sup>2+</sup> and quinidine (<span id="ref4845.18248412" class="cite">Schwarzer <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>). Cysteines control the N- and C-linker-dependent gating of KCNH1 potassium channels &nbsp;(<span id="ref8462.22310694" class="cite">Sahoo <em>et al.</em>, 2012</span>).&nbsp; The 3-d structure has been determined at 3.8 &Aring; resolution using single-particle cryo-EM with calmodulin bound. The structure suggests a novel mechanism of voltage-dependent gating. Calmodulin binding closes the potassium pore (<span id="ref34545702.27516594" class="cite">Whicher and MacKinnon 2016</span>). Eag1 has three intracellular domains: PAS, C-linker, and CNBHD.&nbsp;<span id="ref34550163.31490124" class="cite">Whicher and MacKinnon 2019</span> demonstrated that the Eag1 intracellular domains are not required for voltage-dependent gating but likely interact with the VS to modulate gating. Specific interactions between the PAS, CNBHD, and VS domains modulate voltage-dependent gating, and VS movement destabilizes these interactions to promote channel opening. Mutations affecting these interactions render Eag1 insensitive to calmodulin inhibition (<span id="ref34550163.31490124" class="cite">Whicher and MacKinnon 2019</span>). The structure of the calmodulin insensitive mutant in a pre-open conformation suggests that channel opening may occur through a rotation of the intracellular domains, and calmodulin may prevent this rotation by stabilizing interactions between the VS and the other intracellular domains. Intracellular domains likely play a similar modulatory role in voltage-dependent gating of the related Kv11-12 channels. The human ortholog, EAG or EAG-1, is 989 aas long and is 95% identical to the rat protein.&nbsp; In ether-a-go-go K<sup>+</sup> channels, voltage-dependent activation is modulated by ion binding to a site located in an extracellular-facing crevice between transmembrane segments S2 and S3 in the voltage sensor.&nbsp;<span id="ref34551203.15347589" class="cite">Silverman <em>et al.</em> 2004</span> found that acidic residues, D278 in S2 and D327 in S3, are able to coordinate a variety of divalent cations, including Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Mn<sup>2+</sup>, and Ni<sup>2+</sup>, which have qualitatively similar functional effects, but different half-maximal effective concentrations. EAG (ether-a-go-go) voltage-dependent K<sup>+ </sup>channels with similarities and Differences in the structural organization and gating (<span id="ref34551361.32351384" class="cite">Barros <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p>

valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.3.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.3.1&tcname=">1.A.1.3.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Large conductance, voltage- and Ca<sup>2+</sup>-activated K<sup>+</sup> (BK or Slo) channel. Four pairs of RCK1 and RICK2 domains form the Ca<sup>2+</sup>-sensing apparatus known as the "gating ring" in Big Potassium (BK) channel proteins (<span id="ref8744.22556415" class="cite">Savalli <em>et al.</em>, 2012</span>). Gating of BK channels does not seem to require a physical gate. Instead, changes in the pore shape and surface hydrophobicity in the Ca<sup>2+</sup>-free state allow the channel to readily undergo hydrophobic dewetting transitions, giving rise to a large free energy barrier for K<sup>+</sup> permeation (<span id="ref34548753.30143620" class="cite">Jia <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).&nbsp; Voltage-dependent dynamics of the BK channel cytosolic gating ring are coupled to the membrane-embedded voltage sensor (<span id="ref34549268.30526860" class="cite">Miranda <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Slo channels  are targets for insecticides and antiparasitic drugs.&nbsp;<span id="ref34554103.34887422" class="cite">Raisch <em>et al.</em> 2021</span> reported structures of <em>Drosophila</em> Slo in the Ca<sup>2+</sup>-bound and Ca<sup>2+</sup>-free forms and in complex with the fungal neurotoxin verruculogen and the anthelmintic drug emodepside. The architecture and gating mechanism of Slo channels are conserved, but potential insect-specific binding pockets are present. Verruculogen inhibits K<sup>+</sup> transport by blocking the Ca<sup>2+</sup>-induced activation signal and precludes K<sup>+</sup> from entering the selectivity filter while emodepside decreases the conductance by suboptimal K<sup>+</sup> coordination and uncouples ion gating from Ca<sup>2+</sup> and voltage sensing (<span id="ref34554103.34887422" class="cite">Raisch <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). In neurosecretion, allosteric communication between voltage sensors and Ca<sup>2+</sup> binding in BK channels is crucially involved in damping excitatory stimuli.&nbsp;<span id="ref34554935.35704760" class="cite">Carrasquel-Ursulaez <em>et al.</em> 2022</span> demonstrated that two arginines in the transmembrane segment S4 (R210 and R213) function as the BK gating charges. The energy landscape of the gating particles is electrostatically tuned by a network of salt bridges contained in the voltage sensor domain (VSD).&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Ca<sup>2+</sup>-activated K<sup>+</sup> channel of <em>Drosophila melanogaster</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.3.10"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.3.10&tcname=">1.A.1.3.10</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Calcium-, magnesium- and voltage-activated K<sup>+</sup> channel, Slo1 (Kcma1; KCNMA, KCNMA1), a BK channel, of 1236 aas and 6 N-terminal TMSs. <span>Its activation dampens the excitatory events that elevate the cytosolic Ca<sup>2+</sup> concentration and/or depolarize the cell membrane. It therefore 
contributes to repolarization of the membrane potential, and it plays a key 
role in controlling excitability in a number of systems. </span><span><span>Ethanol and carbon monoxide-bound heme increase channel activation while heme inhibits channel activation (<span id="ref34550607.14523450" class="cite">Tang <em>et al.</em> 2003</span>). The </span></span>molecular structures of the human Slo1 channel in complex with beta4 has been solved revealing four beta4 subunits, each containing two transmembrane helices, encircling Slo1, contacting it through helical interactions inside the membrane. On the extracellular side, beta4 forms a tetrameric crown over the pore. Structures with high and low Ca<sup>2+</sup> concentrations show that identical gating conformations occur in the absence and presence of beta4, implying that beta4 serves to modulate the relative stabilities of 'pre-existing' conformations rather than creating new ones (<span id="ref34550608.31815672" class="cite">Tao and MacKinnon 2019</span>). BK channels show increased activities in Angelman syndrome due to genetic defects in the ubiquitin protein ligase E3A (UBE3A) gene (<span id="ref34550668.31857479" class="cite">Sun <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). It is a large-conductance potassium (BK) channel that can be synergistically and independently activated by membrane voltage and intracellular Ca<sup>2+</sup>. The only covalent connection between the cytosolic Ca<sup>2+</sup>-sensing domain and the TM pore and voltage sensing domains is a 15-residue 'C-linker' which plays a direct role in mediating allosteric coupling between BK domains (<span id="ref34551863.32597752" class="cite">Yazdani <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).&nbsp; Site specific deacylation by the alpha/beta acyl-hydrolase domain-containing protein 17A, ABHD17a (Q96GS6, 310 aas), controls BK channel splice variant activity (<span id="ref34552099.32913120" class="cite">McClafferty <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Compared with the structure of isolated hSlo1 Ca<sup>2+</sup> sensing gating rings, two opposing subunits in hBK unfurled, resulting in a wider opening towards the transmembrane region of hBK. In the pore gate domain, two opposing subunits moved downwards relative to the two other subunits (<span id="ref34555163.36005673" class="cite">Tonggu and Wang 2022</span>). A gating lever, mediated by S4/S5 segment interactions within the transmembrane domain, rotates to engage and stabilize the open conformation of the S6 inner pore helix upon V sensor activation (<span id="ref34555782.36516264" class="cite">Sun and Horrigan 2022</span>). An indirect pathway, mediated by the carboxyl-terminal cytosolic domain (CTD) and C-linker connects the CTD to S6, and stabilizes the closed conformation when V sensors are at rest (<span id="ref34555782.36516264" class="cite">Sun and Horrigan 2022</span>). Co-dependent regulation of p-BRAF (TC# 8.A.23.1.48) and the potassium channel KCNMA1 levels drives glioma progression (<span id="ref34555935.36763212" class="cite">Xie <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Potassium channelopathies associated with epilepsy-related syndromes and directions for therapeutic interventionhave been reviewed (<span id="ref34555939.36646291" class="cite">Gribkoff and Winquist 2023</span>). The influx of Ca<sup>2+, </sup>mediated by the hypotonic-induced activation of mechanosensitive channels, is a key step for opening both the BK(Ca) and the IK(Ca) channels. The influx of Ca<sup>2+</sup>, mediated by the hypotonic-induced activation of mechanosensitive channels, is a key step for opening both the BK(Ca) and the IK(Ca) (TC# 1.A.1.16.2) channels (<span id="ref34556840.37431808" class="cite">Michelucci <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; Disease-associated KCNMA1 variants decrease circadian clock robustness in channelopathy mouse models (<span id="ref34557249.37728576" class="cite">Dinsdale <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; High-resolution structures illuminate key principles underlying voltage and LRRC26 regulation of Slo1 channels (<span id="ref34557713.38187713" class="cite">Kallure <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Kcma1 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.3.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.3.2&tcname=">1.A.1.3.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>

<p><a id="1.A.1.3.2"></a></p>
<p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="1.A.1.3.2"></a></p>
<p>Large conductance or L-type Ca<sup>2+</sup> and voltage-activated K<sup>+ </sup> channel (LTCC), &alpha;-subunit (subunit &alpha;1), BK, BK<sub>Ca</sub>, Kca1.1, Slowpoke, Slo1, KCNMA1 or MaxiK (functions with four &beta;-subunits (TC# 8.A.14) encoded by genes KCNMB1-4 and the&nbsp;&gamma; subunit (TC# 8.A.43) in humans (<span id="ref10386.24077696" class="cite">Toro <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>; <span id="ref34545340.27022192" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>); the positions of beta2 and beta3 have been determined (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref9519">Wu <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>).&nbsp; The KB channel is inhibited by 3 scorpion toxins, charybda toxin, iberiotoxin and slotoxin.&nbsp; It forms a ''Ca<sup>2+&nbsp; </sup>nanodomain'' complex with Cav1.2 (L-type; 1.A.1.11.4), Cav2.1 (P/Q-type; 1.A.1.11.5) and Cav2.2 (N-type; 1.A.1.11.6) where Ca<sup>2+</sup> influx through the Cav channel activates BK<sub>Ca</sub> (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref3689">Berkefeld <em>et al</em>., 2006</a>; <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref3745">Romanenko <em>et al</em>., 2006</a>). The RCK2 domain is a Ca<sup>2+</sup> sensor (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref4640">Yusifov <em>et al</em>., 2008</a>). Binding of Ca<sup>2+</sup> to D367 and E535 changes the conformation around the binding site and turns the side chain of M513 into a hydrophobic core, explaining how Ca<sup>2+</sup> binding opens the activation gate of the channel (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref7196">Zhang <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). A structural motif in the C-terminal tail of Slo1 confers carbon monoxide sensitivity to human BK<sub>Ca&nbsp;</sub>channels (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref4642">Williams <em>et al</em>., 2008</a>; <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref5102">Hou <em>et al</em>., 2008</a>). These channels are present in the inner mitochondrial membrane of rat brain (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref4997">Douglas <em>et al</em>., 2006</a>).The Stress-Axis Regulated Exon (STREX) is responsible for stretch sensitivity. Ca<sup>2+</sup> binds to two sites. Ca<sup>2+</sup> binding to the RCK1 site is voltage dependent, but Ca<sup>2+</sup> binding to the Ca<sup>2+</sup> bowl is not (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref7105">Sweet and Cox <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>).&nbsp;Type 1 IP3 receptors activate BKCa channels via local molecular coupling in arterial smooth muscle cells (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref7772">Zhao <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). The open structure is known (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref8230">Yuan <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>). BK<sub>Ca</sub>&nbsp;is essential for ER calcium uptake in neurons and cardiomyocytes (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref8361">Kuum <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>) and link Ca<sup>2+</sup> signaling to action potential firing and neurotransmitter release via serotonin receptors in many types of neurons (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref9166">Rothberg 2012</a>). The molecular mechanism of pharmacological activation of BK channels has been discussed by Gessner et al. (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref8710">2012</a>).&nbsp;The first TMS of the &beta;2-subunit binds to TMS S1 of the &alpha;-subunit (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref8895">Morera <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>).&nbsp; Mutations in Cav1.2 give rise to Timothy syndrome (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref9035">Dixon <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>).&nbsp; Exhibits low voltage activation by interaction with Cav3 (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref9828">Rehak <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>) as well as Ca<sup>2+</sup>-gating (<span id="ref9834.23616542" class="cite">Berkefeld and Fakler 2013</span>). Single-channel kinetics have been reported (<span id="ref34543727.25653620" class="cite">Geng and Magleby 2014</span>). The &gamma;-subunit has TC# 8.A.43.1.8.&nbsp; RBK channels regulate myogenesis in vascular smooth muscle cells (<span id="ref34545408.27238270" class="cite">Krishnamoorthy-Natarajan and Koide 2016</span>). <span id="ref34546415.27807200" class="cite">Latorre <em>et al.</em> 2017</span> reviewed molecular, physiological and pathological aspects of Slo1.  The microRNA, mmumiR449a, reduced the mRNA expression levels of 
transient receptor potential cation channel subfamily A member 1 
(TRPA1), and calcium activated potassium channel subunit alpha1 (KCNMA1)
 and increased the level of transmembrane phosphatase with tension 
homology (TPTE) in the DRG cells (<span id="ref34546726.28498403" class="cite">Lu <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>), thereby reducing pain. The N-terminal sequence determines its modification by &beta;-subunits (<span id="ref34547159.28750098" class="cite">Lorca <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Inhibition of BKCa negatively alters cardiovascular function (<span id="ref34550207.29932499" class="cite">Patel <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). BKCa may be the target of verteporfin, a benzoporphyrin photosensitizer that alters membrane ionic currents (<span id="ref34550232.31508407" class="cite">Huang <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Globotriaosylceramide (Gb3) accumulates due to mutations in the gene encoding alpha-galactosidase A. Gb3 deposition in skin fibroblasts impairs KCa1.1 activity and activate the Notch1 signaling pathway, resulting in an increase in pro-inflammatory mediator expression, and thus, contributing to cutaneous nociceptor sensitization as a potential mechanism of FD-associated pain (<span id="ref34550582.31778662" class="cite">Rickert <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). This channel may be present in mitochondria (<span id="ref34550683.31834993" class="cite">Parrasia <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). The Slo3 (TC# 1.A.1.3.5) cytosolic module confers pH-dependent regulation whereas the Slo1 cytosolic module confers Ca<sup>2+</sup>-dependent regulation (<span id="ref34551655.15201331" class="cite">Xia <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). Elevated extracellular Ca<sup>2+</sup> aggravates iron-induced neurotoxicity because LTCCs mediate iron transport in dopaminergic neurons and this, in turn, results in elevated intracellular Ca<sup>2+</sup> and further aggravates iron-induced neurotoxicity (<span id="ref34552500.31482520" class="cite">Xu <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Agonists include BMS-191011, NS1619, NS11021, epoxyeicosatrienoic acid isoforms, while inhibitors include iberiotoxin and penitrem A which have been used to study the system in megakaryocytes and platelets (<span id="ref34552777.33590615" class="cite">Balduini <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Medicinal plant products can interact with BKCa (<a class="reflink" href="https://tcdb.org/search/result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref34554622">Rajabian <em>et al.</em> 2022</a>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>BK<sub>Ca</sub> or MaxiK channel of <em>Rattus norvegicus</em> (Q62976)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.3.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.3.3&tcname=">1.A.1.3.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Ca<sup>2+</sup>-activated K<sup>+</sup> channel Slo-1 (Maxi K; BK channel) (ethanol-activated; responsible for intoxication) (<span id="ref2887.14675531" class="cite">Davies <em>et al.</em>, 2003</span>); tyrosyl phosphorylation regulates BK channels via cortactin (<span id="ref4567.18039661" class="cite">Tian <em>et al.</em> 2008a</span>), but palmitoylation gates phosphorylation-dependent regulation of BK potassium channels (<span id="ref6570.19098106" class="cite">Tian <em>et al.</em>, 2008b</span>). Also regulated by Mg<sup>2+</sup> which mediates interaction between the voltage sensor and cytosolic domain to activate BK channels (<span id="ref4632.17984060" class="cite">Yang <em>et al.</em>, 2007</span>). Modulated by the ss2 subunit (<span id="ref7731.21123563" class="cite">Lee <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>). The structure of the gating ring from the human large-conductance Ca<sup>2+</sup>-gated K<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;channel has been reported (<span id="ref8164.20574420" class="cite">Wu <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>).&nbsp;Four pairs of RCK1 and RICK2 domains form the Ca<sup>2+</sup>-sensing apparatus known as the "gating ring" in BK channel proteins (<span id="ref8744.22556415" class="cite">Savalli <em>et al.</em>, 2012</span>).&nbsp; The dystrophin (Q9TW65) dystrobrevin (Q9Y048) complex controls BK channel localization and muscle activity as well as neurotroansmitter release (<span id="ref10731.20019812" class="cite">Kim <em>et al.</em> 2009</span>, <span id="ref34544519.22131396" class="cite">Chen <em>et al.</em> 2011).</span>&nbsp; Syntrophin (Q93646) <span>links various receptors and transporters to the actin cytoskeleton and the dystrophin glycoprotein complex (DGC), and &alpha;-catulin (CTN-1; 759 aas, 0 TMSs) facilitates targeting. </span>The BK channel is a tetramer where the pore-forming &alpha;-subunit contains seven transmembrane segments (<span id="ref34554184.34764886" class="cite">Gonz&aacute;lez-Sanabria <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). It has a modular architecture containing a pore domain with a highly potassium-selective filter, a voltage-sensor domain and two intracellular Ca<sup>2+</sup> binding sites at the C-terminus. BK is found in the plasma membrane of different cell types, the inner mitochondrial membrane (mitoBK) and the nuclear envelope's outer membrane (nBK). Like BK channels in the plasma membrane (pmBK), the open probability of mitoBK and nBK channels are regulated by Ca<sup>2+</sup> and voltage and modulated by auxiliary subunits. BK channels share common pharmacology to toxins such as iberiotoxin, charybdotoxin, paxilline, and agonists of the benzimidazole family (<span id="ref34554184.34764886" class="cite">Gonz&aacute;lez-Sanabria <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>BK K<sup>+</sup> channel of <em>Caenorhabditis elegans</em> (Q95V25)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.3.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.3.4&tcname=">1.A.1.3.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The one or two component intracellularly Na<sup>+</sup> and Cl<sup>-</sup>-activated delayed rectifier K<sup>+</sup> channel, <em>rSlo2</em>.2 (Slack; KCNT1)/r Slo2.1 (Slick; KCNT2; TC# 1.A.1.3.6) provides protection against ischemia (<span id="ref3969.12628167" class="cite">Yuan <em>et al</em>., 2003</span>). The N-terminal domain of Slack determines the formation and trafficking of Slick/Slack heteromeric sodium-activated potassium channels (<span id="ref6620.19403831" class="cite">Chen <em>et al.</em>, 2009</span>). Slick and Slack can also form separate homooligomeric channels. These channels are widely distributed in the mammalian CNS and they play roles in slow afterhyperpolarization, generation of depolarizing afterpotentials and in setting and stabilizing the resting potential (<span id="ref34547751.29124216" class="cite">Rizzi <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). The small cytoplasmic protein beta-synuclein TC# 1.C.77.1.2) and the transmembrane protein 263 (TMEM 263; TC# 8.A.101.1.1) are interaction partners of both Slick and Slack channels. In sperm, it gives rise to pH-dependent outwardly rectifying K<sup>+</sup> currents. (required for the ensuing acrosome reaction; activated by phosphatidylinositol 4,5-bisphosphate (PIP(2)) (<span id="ref7351.20392696" class="cite">Tang <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>). The Slo3 cytosolic module confers pH-dependent regulation whereas the Slo1 (TC# 1.A.1.3.2)&nbsp; cytosolic module confers Ca<sup>2+</sup>-dependent regulation (<span id="ref34551655.15201331" class="cite">Xia <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). When mammalian sperm are released in the female reproductive tract, they are incapable of fertilizing the oocyte. They need a prolonged exposure to the alkaline medium of the female genital tract before their flagellum gets hyperactivated and the acrosome reaction can take place, allowing the sperm to interact with the oocyte (<span id="ref34554674.35399518" class="cite">de Prelle <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Ionic fluxes across the sperm membrane are involved in two essential aspects of capacitation: the increase in intracellular pH and membrane hyperpolarization. The SLO3 potassium channel and the sNHE sodium-proton exchanger are necessary for the capacitation process to occur. As the SLO3 channel is activated by an increase in intracellular pH and sNHE is activated by hyperpolarization, they act together as a positive feedback system (<span id="ref34554674.35399518" class="cite">de Prelle <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Slo3 of <em>Mus musculus</em> (O54982)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.3.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.3.6&tcname=">1.A.1.3.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Human outward rectifying potassium channel, Slo2.1 (also called KCNT2 and Slick) of 1135 aas. Produces rapidly activating outward rectifier K<sup>+</sup> currents. Activated by high intracellular sodium and chloride levels. 
Channel activity is inhibited by ATP and by inhalation of anesthetics such
 as isoflurane<acronym>.&nbsp; </acronym>Inhibited upon stimulation of G-protein coupled receptors such as CHRM1 and GRIA1.&nbsp; Orthologous to 1.A.1.3.4 (<span id="ref10382.24166878" class="cite">Garg <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>) and can form a heteromeric complex with it and several other proteins (see TC# 1.A.1.3.4).&nbsp; Hydrophobic interactions

<span id="ref34544785.14612577" class="cite">Santi <em>et al.</em> 2003).</span>&nbsp; SLO2 protects from hypoxic injury by increasing the permeability of the mitochondrial inner membrane to K<sup>+</sup> (<span id="ref34544786.22145034" class="cite">Wojtovich <em>et al.</em> 2011</span>). <span class="highlight">SLO-2</span> is functionally coupled with CaV1 and regulates neurotransmitter release (<span id="ref34544787.25300429" class="cite">Liu <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).&nbsp;  Partially responsible for action potential repolarization during synaptic transmission (<span id="ref34544887.25355206" class="cite">Ford and Davis 2014</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Slo-2 of <em>Caenorhabditis <span class="highlight">elegans</span></em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.3.9"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.3.9&tcname=">1.A.1.3.9</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Voltage-gated calcium-activated potassium channel of&nbsp;862 aas and 6 or 7 TMSs.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>VIC protein of <em>Entamoeba histolytica</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.30.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.30.1&tcname=">1.A.1.30.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Uncharacterized putative chloride channel protein of 219 aas and 2 TMSs.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>UP of <em>Vibrio </em>phage 1.081.O._10N.286.52.C2</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.4.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.4.1&tcname=">1.A.1.4.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>K<sup>+</sup> channel, AKT1; may form heteromeric channels with KC1 (TC # 1.A.1.4.9) (<span id="ref6623.19509299" class="cite">Geiger <em>et al.</em>, 2009</span>).&nbsp; Required for seed development and postgermination growth in low potassium (<span id="ref10044.20413648" class="cite">Pyo <em>et al.</em> 2010</span>).&nbsp; Functions optimally with intermediate potassium concentrations (~1 mM) (<span id="ref34545256.24810767" class="cite">Nieves-Cordones <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). In barley, it may play a role in drought resistance (<span id="ref34550901.31443572" class="cite">Cai <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).&nbsp; HAK/KUP/KT8, GrAKT2.1 and GrAKT1.1 potassium channels may function in response to abiotic stress in <em>Gossypium raimondii</em> (<span id="ref34554157.34864561" class="cite">Azeem <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Plants obtain nutrients from the soil via transmembrane transporters and channels in their root hairs, from which ions radially transport in toward the xylem for distribution across the plant body.&nbsp;<span id="ref34554304.34964607" class="cite">Dickinson <em>et al.</em> 2021</span> determined structures of the hyperpolarization-activated channel, AKT1, from <em>Arabidopsis</em><em> thaliana</em>, which mediates K<sup>+</sup> uptake from the soil into plant roots. The structures of AtAKT1, embedded in lipid nanodiscs, show that the channel undergoes a reduction of C4 to C2 symmetry, possibly to regulate its electrical activation.</p>

<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SPORK2 of <em>Samanea saman</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.4.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.4.2&tcname=">1.A.1.4.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>K<sup>+</sup>channel, KDC1 (voltage and pH-dependent; inward rectifying). H<sub>2</sub>S signaling not only activates the ion channel proteins located in the guard cell membrane to induce stomatal closure, but also regulates the transcriptional expression and the activity of RuBisCO, a non-stomatal factor to enhance the photosynthetic efficiency of leaves. There is therefore a beneficial balance between the regulation of H<sub>2</sub>S signaling on stomatal factors and non-stomatal factors due to drought stress (<span id="ref34556321.36938567" class="cite">Zhang <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>GORK of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (CAC17380)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.4.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.4.5&tcname=">1.A.1.4.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Root stelar K<sup>+</sup> outward rectifying channel, SKOR (involved in K<sup>+</sup> release into the xylem sap; part of the plant water stress response) (<span id="ref2539.9741629" class="cite">Gaymard <em>et al</em>., 1998</span>). SKOR is an outwardly rectifying K<sup>+</sup> channel that mediates the delivery of K<sup>+</sup> from stelar cells to the xylem in the roots, a critical step in the long-distance distribution of K<sup>+</sup> from roots to the upper parts of the plant. <span id="ref34547719.16623888" class="cite">Liu <em>et al.</em> 2006</span> and&nbsp;<span id="ref34547720.16623889" class="cite">Johansson <em>et al.</em> 2006</span> reported that SKOR channel activity is strictly dependent on intracellular and extracellular K<sup>+</sup> concentrations. Activation by K<sup>+</sup> did not affect the kinetics of voltage dependence, indicating that a voltage-independent gating mechanism underlies K<sup>+</sup> sensing. The C-terminal non-transmembrane region is required for sensing. The intracellular K<sup>+</sup> sensing mechanism couples SKOR activity to the K<sup>+</sup> status of the 'source cells', thereby establishing a supply-based unloading system for the regulation of K<sup>+</sup> distribution (<span id="ref34547719.16623888" class="cite">Liu <em>et al.</em> 2006</span>; <span id="ref34547720.16623889" class="cite">Johansson <em>et al.</em> 2006</span>). SKOR may be involved in droght resistance in barley (<span id="ref34550903.31443572" class="cite">Cai <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SKOR of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (AAF26975)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.4.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.4.6&tcname=">1.A.1.4.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Heterotetrameric K<sup>+</sup> channel, KAT2/AKT2/KCT2 (<span id="ref10589.24406792" class="cite">Nieves-Cordones <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). AKT2 functions in phloem loading and unloading and operates as an inward-rectifying channel that allows H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase-energized K<sup>+</sup> uptake. Through reversible post-translational modifications, it can also function as an open, K<sup>+</sup>-selective channel, providing energy for transmembrane transport processes. It is present in a complex of several proteins in which it interacts with the receptor-like kinase, MRH1/MDIS2 (<span id="ref34546547.28300158" class="cite">Sklodowski <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). The ortholog in <em>Brassica rapa </em>(Chinese cabbage), KCT2, is induced by stress. <p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>AKT2/KAT2 of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> <br /> AKT2 (Q38898) <br /> KAT2 (Q38849)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.4.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.4.7&tcname=">1.A.1.4.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The voltage-sensitive inward rectifying K<sup>+</sup> channel, KAT1 (similar to 1.A.1.4.3; activated by protein 14-3-3 (AAF87262)) (<span id="ref3748.16990282" class="cite">Sottocornola <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>). May also transport Na<sup>+</sup> and Cs<sup>+</sup> (<span id="ref6760.19742379" class="cite">Nakamura and Gaber, 2009</span>). Forms heterotetrameric channels with KAT2 with a stoichiometry of 2:2 (<span id="ref6902.20040603" class="cite">Lebaudy <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>). The pH-sensor is built of a sensory cloud rather than of single key amino acids (<span id="ref8190.22070190" class="cite">Gonzalez <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>). The transmembrane core region of KAT1 is important for its activity in <em>S. cerevisiae</em>, and this involves not only the pore region but also parts of its voltage-sensor domain (<span id="ref34547350.28933647" class="cite">Saito <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Electromechanical coupling and gating polarity in KAT1 displays a depolarized voltage sensor, which interacts with a closed pore domain directly via two interfaces and indirectly via an intercalated phospholipid. Direct interaction between the sensor and the C-linker hairpin in the adjacent pore subunit is the primary determinant of gating polarity (<span id="ref34551635.32461693" class="cite">Clark <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Possibly an inward motion of the S4 sensor helix of 5-7 &Aring; underlies a direct-coupling mechanism, driving a conformational reorientation of the C-linker and ultimately opening the activation gate formed by the S6 intracellular bundle. KAT1, and presumably other hyperpolarization-gated plant CNBD channels, can open from an S4-down VSD conformation homologous to the divalent/proton-inhibited conformation of EAG family K<sup>+</sup> channels (<span id="ref34552428.33275659" class="cite">Zhou <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KAT1 of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (Q39128)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.4.8"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.4.8&tcname=">1.A.1.4.8</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Inward rectifying Shaker K<sup>+</sup> channel SPIK (AKT6) (expressed in pollen, and involved in pollen tube development) (<span id="ref3974.11825875" class="cite">Mouline <em>et al</em>., 2002</span>).&nbsp; The shaker potassium Channel family includes 24 members in <em>Gossypium hirsutum</em> L. IH pacemaker current. As with other voltage-gated members of the 6-transmembrane KV channel
superfamily, opening of HCN channels involves dilation of a helical bundle formed by the
intracellular ends of S6, but this is promoted by inward, not outward, displacement of S4. Direct
agonist binding to a ring of cyclic nucleotide-binding sites, one of which lies immediately distal
to each S6 helix, imparts cAMP sensitivity to HCN channel opening. At depolarized potentials, HCN
channels are further modulated by intracellular Mg<sup>2+</sup> which blocks the open channel pore and blunts
the inhibitory effect of outward K<sup>+</sup> flux. <span id="ref11275.24983358" class="cite">Lyashchenko <em>et al.</em> 2014</span> showed that cAMP binding to the gating ring
enhances not only channel opening but also the kinetics of Mg<sup>2+</sup> block.&nbsp; Mutations in HCN4 cause sick sinus and the Brugada syndrome, cardiac abnormalities. HCN4 is associated with famiial sinus bradycardia (<span id="ref34546604.28174302" class="cite">Boulton <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Activation of Hcn4 by cAMP has been reviewed (<span id="ref34551920.32733276" class="cite">Porro <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). The HCN1-4 channel family is responsible for the hyperpolarization-activated cation current If/Ih that controls automaticity in cardiac and neuronal pacemaker cells.&nbsp;<span id="ref34553457.34166608" class="cite">Saponaro <em>et al.</em> 2021</span> presented cryo-EM structures of HCN4 in the presence or absence of bound cAMP, displaying the pore domain in closed and open conformations. Analysis of cAMP-bound and -unbound structures shed light on how ligand-induced transitions in the channel cytosolic portion mediate the effect of cAMP on channel gating and highlighted the regulatory role of a Mg<sup>2+</sup> coordination site formed between the C-linker and the S4-S5 linker. Comparison of open/closed pore states shows that the cytosolic gate opens through concerted movements of the S5 and S6 transmembrane helices. Furthermore, in combination with molecular dynamics analyses, the open pore structures provide insights into the mechanisms of K<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> permeation (<span id="ref34553457.34166608" class="cite">Saponaro <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>).&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Hcn4 of <em>Homo sapiens</em>&nbsp;(Q9Y3Q4)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.5.11"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.5.11&tcname=">1.A.1.5.11</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Hyperpolarization-activated cyclic nucleotide-gated (HCN) inward current-carrying cationic channel, I(f), (HCN2/HCN4) (<span id="ref8214.19574228" class="cite">Ye and Nerbonne, 2009</span>).&nbsp; Functional interactions between the HCN2 TM region and C-terminal region govern multiple CNB fold-mediated mechanisms, implying that the molecular mechanisms of autoinhibition, open-state trapping, and Quick-Activation include participation of TM region structures (<span id="ref34551110.32146488" class="cite">Page <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Rhythmic activity in pacemaker cells, as in the sino-atrial node in the heart, depends on the activation of HCN channels. As in depolarization-activated K<sup>+</sup> channels, the fourth transmembrane segment S4 functions as the voltage sensor in hyperpolarization-activated HCN channels (<span id="ref34553782.34504015" class="cite">Wu <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). S4 in HCN channels moves in two steps in response to hyperpolarizations, and the second S4 step correlates with gate opening (<span id="ref34553782.34504015" class="cite">Wu <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). It is a nuclear hormone receptor that binds estrogens with an affinity similar to 
that of ESR1/ER-alpha, and activates expression of reporter genes 
containing estrogen response elements (ERE) in an estrogen-dependent 
manner (<span id="ref34556047.20074560" class="cite">Koyama <em>et al.</em> 2010</span>). It may lack
 ligand binding ability and has no or only very low ERE binding activity,
 resulting in the loss of ligand-dependent transactivation ability. Male moujse ejaculation drives sexual satiety and selectively activates Esr2neurons in the BNSTpr of both sexes (<span id="ref34556048.36758107" class="cite">Zhou <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; Changes in binding affinity, rather than changes in cAMP concentration, can modulate HCN channels (<span id="ref34557768.38287019" class="cite">Porro <em>et al.</em> 2024</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>HCN2/HCN4 channels of <em>Homo sapiens</em>&nbsp;<br />HCN2 (Q9UL51)<br />HCN4 (Q9Y3Q4)&nbsp;</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.5.12"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.5.12&tcname=">1.A.1.5.12</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cyclic nucleotide-gated cation channel &alpha;3 (CNGA3 or CNG3); photoreceptor cGMP-gated channel &alpha;-subunit. Also possibly expressed in inner ear cell cells where it binds to an intracellular C-terminal domain of EMILIN1 (<span id="ref8687.22248097" class="cite">Selvakumar <em>et al.</em>, 2012</span>).&nbsp; Elastic network model analysis of the CNGA3 channel supports a
modular model of allosteric gating, according to which protein domains are quasi-independent: they
can move independently but are coupled to each other allosterically (<span id="ref34543770.25474149" class="cite">Gofman <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). An intact S4 is required for proper protein folding and/or assembly involving two glycosylation sites in the endoplasmic reticulum membrane (<span id="ref34553278.15024024" class="cite">Faillace <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). It may function with CNGB3 (TC# 1.A.1.5.37; Q9NQW8; 809 aas and 6 TMSs).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CNGA3 of <em>Homo sapiens</em>&nbsp;(Q16281)&nbsp;</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.5.13"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.5.13&tcname=">1.A.1.5.13</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Trout cyclic nucleotide-gated cation channel &alpha;3 (CNGA3 or CNG3). 2012</span>).&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CNGA3 of <em>Oncorhynchus mykiss</em>&nbsp;(G9BHJ0)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.5.17"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.5.17&tcname=">1.A.1.5.17</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cyclic nucleotide-gated K<sup>+</sup>channel, SthK, of 430 aas, probably with 6 TMSs in a 2 + 2 + 1 + P-loop + 1 TMS arrangement.&nbsp; The channel is activated by cAMP, not by cGMP, and is highly specific for K<sup>+</sup> over Na<sup>+</sup>.&nbsp; It has a C-terminal hydrophilic cAMP-binding domain linked to the 6 TMS channel domain (<span id="ref11126.24821777" class="cite">Brams <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). An SthK C-linker domain is essential for coupling cyclic nucleotide binding to channel opening (<span id="ref34551343.32358188" class="cite">Evans <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). An agonist-dependent conformational change in which residues of the B'-helix displayed outward movement with respect to the symmetry axis of the channel in the presence of cAMP was observed, but not with the partial agonist, cGMP. This conformational rearrangement was observed both in detergent-solubilized SthK and in channels reconstituted into lipid nanodiscs. In addition to outward movement of the B'-helix, channel activation involves upward translation of the cytoplasmic domain with formation of state-dependent interactions between the C-linker and the transmembrane domain (<span id="ref34551343.32358188" class="cite">Evans <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Three-deminsional structures are available (7RSY_A-D). SthK is active in a sparsely tethered lipid bilayer membranes (<span id="ref34556303.37072125" class="cite">Andersson <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Spirochaetes</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Channel of <em>Spirochaeta thermophila</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.5.18"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.5.18&tcname=">1.A.1.5.18</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cyclic nucleotide-gated cation (CNG) channel of 665 aas.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals (Insects)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CNG of <em>Drosophila melanogaster</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.5.19"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.5.19&tcname=">1.A.1.5.19</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TAX-2 cyclic nucleotide-gated cation channel-B (CNGB) of 800 aas (<span id="ref10571.23886944" class="cite">Wojtyniak <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TAX-2 CNGB of <em>Caenorhabditis elegans</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.5.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.5.2&tcname=">1.A.1.5.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Hyperpolarization-activated and cyclic nucleotide-gated K<sup>+</sup> channel, HCN (bCNG-1) (P<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>/P<sub>K</sub><sup>+</sup> &asymp; 0.3). The human orthologue (O88703) is 863 aas in length and also catalyzes mixed monovalent cation currents K<sup>+</sup>:Na<sup>+</sup>= 4:1 (Lyashchenko and Tibbs et al., 2008). <span id="ref6699.19584315" class="cite">Biel <em>et al.</em> (2009)</span> presented a detailed review of hyperpolarization-activated cation-channels. They are inhibited by nicotine and epibatidine which bind to the inner pore (<span id="ref7272.20702707" class="cite">Griguoli <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>). They control cardiac and neuronal rhythmicity. HCN channels contain cyclic nucleotide-binding domains (CNBDs) in their C-terminal regions, linked to the pore-forming transmembrane segment with a C-linker. The C-linker couples the conformational changes caused by the direct binding of cyclic nucleotides to the HCN pore opening. Cyclic dinucleotides antagonize the effect of cyclic nucleotides in HCN4 but not in HCN2 channels. Interaction of the C-linker/CNBD with other parts of the channel appears to be necessary for cyclic-dinucleotide binding in HCN4 channels (<span id="ref34547362.28950029" class="cite">Hayoz <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). A conformational trajectory of allosteric gating of the human cone photoreceptor cyclic nucleotide-gated channel has been documented (<span id="ref34556970.37463923" class="cite">Hu <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; The voltage-sensor rearrangements, directly influenced by membrane lipid domains, can explain the heightened activity of pacemaker HCN channels when localized in cholesterol-poor, disordered lipid domains, leading to membrane hyperexcitability and diseases (<span id="ref34557463.37852983" class="cite">Handlin and Dai 2023</span>).&nbsp; Opioid-induced hyperalgesia and tolerance are driven by HCN ion channels (<span id="ref34557822.38124021" class="cite">Han <em>et al.</em> 2024</span>).</p>
<hr /></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>HCN of <em>Mus musculus</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.5.20"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.5.20&tcname=">1.A.1.5.20</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TAX-4 cyclic nucleotide-gated cation channel A (CNGA) of 733 aas (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref10571">Wojtyniak <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>).&nbsp; <span id="ref34546383.28099415" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> 2017</span> determined the   3.5 &Aring; resolution single-particle
electron cryo-microscopy structure&nbsp; in the cyclic guanosine monophosphate (cGMP)-bound open state. The channel has an unusual voltage-sensor-like domain, accounting for its deficient voltage dependence. A carboxy-terminal linker
connecting S6 and the cyclic-nucleotide-binding domain interacts directly with both the voltage
