These three channels form a complex with the potassium permeable channel, DMI1 (TC# 1.A.1.23.1), in the nuclear envelope.&nbsp; They are expressed in flowers and pods, and mutants in these channels have decreased fertilization rates (<span id="ref34545357.27230377" class="cite">Charpentier <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CNG15 of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.5.28"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.5.28&tcname=">1.A.1.5.28</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The cyclic nucleotide-gated cation channel, CNG-1 of 661 aas and 6 TMSs. CNG-1 functions in multiple capacities to link
nutritional information with behavioral output (<span id="ref34545791.27561605" class="cite">He <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CNG-1 of <em>Caenorhabditis elegans</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.5.29"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.5.29&tcname=">1.A.1.5.29</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>spHCN1 is a pacemaker hyperpolarization-activated cyclic nucleotide-gated (HCN) non-selective cation channel of 767 aas and 6 TMSs that opens due to inward movement of the positive charges in the fourth TMS (S4). This channel is similar to a COOH-terminal-deleted HCN1 channel, suggesting that the main functional differences between spHCN and HCN1 channels are due to differences in their COOH termini (<span id="ref34554874.14676284" class="cite">Vemana <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). These channels open after only two S4s have
moved, and S4 motion is rate limiting during voltage activation of spHCN channels (<span id="ref34546137.17591986" class="cite">Bruening-Wright <em>et al.</em> 2007</span>). HCN channels regulate electrical activity in the heart and brain. Distinct from mammalian isoforms, the sea urchin (spHCN) channel exhibits strong voltage-dependent inactivation in the absence of cAMP (<span id="ref34549324.30541772" class="cite">Idikuda <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). The voltage sensor undergoes a large downward motion during hyperpolarization (<span id="ref34550000.31285608" class="cite">Dai <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Sea urchin HCN1 and 2 (TC# 1.A.1.5.33) (spHCN) channels undergo inactivation with hyperpolarization which occurs only in the absence of cyclic nucleotide (<span id="ref34553349.33990563" class="cite">Dai <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Removing cAMP produces a largely rigid-body rotation of the C-linker relative to the transmembrane domain, bringing the A' helix of the C-linker in close proximity to the voltage-sensing S4 helix. In addition, rotation of the C-linker is elicited by hyperpolarization minus cAMP. Thus, in contrast to electromechanical coupling for channel activation - the A' helix serves to couple the S4-helix movement for channel inactivation, which is likely a conserved mechanism for CNBD-family channels (<span id="ref34553349.33990563" class="cite">Dai <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>).</p>
<pre><em>	</em></pre></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>HPN1 of <em>Strongylocentrotus purpuratus</em> (Purple sea urchin)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.5.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.5.3&tcname=">1.A.1.5.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="1.A.1.5.3"></a></p>
<p>Heterotetrameric (3A:1B) rod photoreceptor cyclic GMP-gated cation channel, CNGA1 or CNCG or CNCG1 (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref2339">Zhong <em>et al</em>., 2002</a>) of 686 aas and 6 TMSs. Cyclic nucleotides are required to open the channel. Gating is proposed to be initiated by an anticlockwise rotation of the N-terminal region of the C-linker, which is then, transmitted through the S6 transmembrane helices to the P-helix, and in turn from this to the pore lumen, which opens from 2 to 5 &Aring;, thus allowing for ion permeation (<span id="ref34550255.15792804" class="cite">Giorgetti <em>et al.</em> 2005</span>). Defects produce channelopathies (Biel &amp; Michalakis, 2007). A ring of four glutamate residues (Glu363) in the outer vestibule, and a ring of four threonines (Thr360) in the inner vestibule of the pore of CNGA1 channels constitute binding sites for permeating ions (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=1.A.1#ref9076">Marchesi <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>).&nbsp; The tetraspanning peripherin-2 (TC# 8.A.40.1.2) links rhodopsin to this cyclic nucleotide-dependent channel in the outer 
segments of rod photoreceptors.&nbsp; The G266D retinitis pigmentosa mutation
 in TMS 4 of rhodopsin abolishes binding of peripherin-2 and prevents 
association with the CNGA1/CNGB1a subunits present in the complex (<span id="ref11285.24963162" class="cite">Becirovic <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).&nbsp; External protons cause inactivation (<span id="ref34543796.25480799" class="cite">Marchesi <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). CNG transmembrane domains have dynamic structures, undergoing conformational rearrangements (<span id="ref34544163.25963832" class="cite">Maity <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; Moreover, structural heterogeneity of CNGA1 channels has been demonstrated (<span id="ref34550223.31457189" class="cite">Maity <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). The structural basis of calmodulin (CaM) modulation of the rod cyclic nucleotide-gated channel has been elucidated by cryoEM. CaM is a constitutive subunit of the rod channel that ensures high sensitivity in dim light (<span id="ref34556550.37011209" class="cite">Barret <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CNG of <em>Homo sapiens</em> <br /> Subunit A1 (CNGA1) <br /> Subunit B1 (CNGB1)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.5.30"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.5.30&tcname=">1.A.1.5.30</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><span class="recommended-name">Hyperpolarization-activated cyclic nucleotide-modulated cation channel splice variant ABs-II of 682 aas and probably 6 TMSs, Ih channel encoded by the PIIH gene (<span id="ref34546382.17409170" class="cite">Ouyang <em>et al.</em> 2007</span>).&nbsp; <br /></span></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Ih channel of <em>Panulirus interruptus</em> (California spiny lobster)<em> (Palinurus interruptus)</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.5.31"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.5.31&tcname=">1.A.1.5.31</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><span class="recommended-name"><span class="recommended-name">Multi-domain c</span>ation channel with a C-terminal cyclic nucleotide-binding domain; of 465 aas and 6 TMS, LliK.&nbsp; </span>Cyclic nucleotide-gated (CNG) and hyperpolarization-activated cyclic nucleotide-regulated (HCN) channels play roles in phototransduction, olfaction, and cardiac pace making.&nbsp; <span id="ref34546626.28396445" class="cite">James <em>et al.</em> 2017</span> used cryoEM to determine the structure of the intact LliK CNG channel. A short S4-S5 linker connects voltage-sensing and pore domains to produce a non-domain-swapped transmembrane architecture. The conformation of the LliK structure may represent a functional state of this channel family not seen before (<span id="ref34546626.28396445" class="cite">James <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>LliK of <em>Leptospira licerasiae</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.5.32"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.5.32&tcname=">1.A.1.5.32</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>HCN1 is a hyperpolarization-activated cyclic nucleotide-gated (HCN) 
channel of 890 aas and 6 TMSs that opens due to inward movement of the 
positive charges in the fourth transmembrane
domain (S4).&nbsp; These channels open after only two S4s have
moved, and S4 motion is rate limiting during voltage activation of spHCN
 channels (<span>Bruening-Wright <em>et al.</em> 2007</span>). HCN1 <span>exhibits weak selectivity for potassium over sodium ions.&nbsp; It's structure (3.5&nbsp;&Aring; resolution) is known (<span>Lee and MacKinnon 2017</span>).&nbsp; It contributes to the native pacemaker currents in heart and neurons. It may also mediate responses to sour stimuli. It is </span><span><span>inhibited by Cs<sup>+</sup>, zatebradine, capsazepine and ZD7288 (<span>Gill <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). </span></span>HCN1 mutational variants include epileptic encephalopathy and common generalized epilepsy. HCN1 has a pivotal function in brain development and control of neuronal excitability (<span id="ref34549237.30351409" class="cite">Marini <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). The interaction with filamin A seems to contribute to localizing HCN1 channels to specific neuronal areas and to modulating channel activity (<span id="ref34551284.15292205" class="cite">Gravante <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). The HCN domain is required for HCN channel cell-surface expression, and it couples voltage- and cAMP-dependent gating mechanisms (<span id="ref34551346.32341127" class="cite">Wang <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Changes in the local S4 environment provide a voltage-sensing mechanism for mammalian hyperpolarization-activated HCN channels (<span id="ref34554751.14676285" class="cite">Bell <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). Cation leak is an important pathogenic mechanism in HCN1-mediated developmental and epileptic encephalopathy (DEE), and seizures are exacerbated by sodium channel blockers in patients with HCN1 variants that cause cation leak (<span id="ref34556661.37265603" class="cite">McKenzie <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). HCN1 epilepsy is progressing from genetics and mechanisms to precision therapies (<span id="ref34557134.37565989" class="cite">Bleakley and Reid 2023</span>).&nbsp; Opioid-Induced Hyperalgesia and Tolerance Are Driven by HCN Ion Channels (<span id="ref34557822.38124021" class="cite">Han <em>et al.</em> 2024</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>HCN1 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.5.33"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.5.33&tcname=">1.A.1.5.33</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Hyperpolarization-gated and cyclic nucleotide regulated K<sup>+</sup> channel of 638 aas and 6 TMSs, HCN2, present in the flagellum of sea urchin sperm (<span id="ref34549946.15992765" class="cite">Galindo <em>et al.</em> 2005</span>).&nbsp; See also TC# 1.A.1.5.29.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>HCN2 of <em>Strongylocentrotus purpuratus </em>(Purple sea urchin)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.5.34"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.5.34&tcname=">1.A.1.5.34</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cyclic nucleotide-binding domain-containing protein, Cng-3, of 626 aas and 5 - 7 TMSs. (Transports K<sup>+</sup> and Cs<sup>+</sup>) (<span id="ref2411.12535075" class="cite">Bertl <em>et al</em>., 2003</span>; <span id="ref34544907.18421473" class="cite">Roller <em>et al.</em> 2008</span>).&nbsp; </span>TOKs are outwardly rectifying K<sup>+</sup> channels in fungi with two pore-loops and eight transmembrane spans.&nbsp;<span id="ref34551775.32519783" class="cite">Lewis <em>et al.</em> 2020</span> described the TOKs from four fungal pathogens. These TOKs pass large currents only in the outward direction like this ScTOK. ScTOK, AfTOK1 (<em>Aspergillus fumigatus</em>), and H99TOK (<em>Cryptococcus neoforman</em>s grubii) are K<sup>+</sup>-selective and pass current above the K<sup>+</sup> reversal potential. CaTOK (<em>Candida albicans</em>) and CnTOK (<em>Cryptococcus neoformans</em> neoformans) pass both K<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> and conduct above a reversal potential, reflecting the mixed permeability of their selectivity filter. Mutations in CaTOK and ScTOK at sites homologous to those that open the internal gates in classical K<sup>+</sup> channels are shown to produce inward TOK currents. Possibly the reversal potential determines ion occupancy, and thus, conductivity, of the selectivity filter gate that is coupled to an imperfectly restrictive internal gate, permitting the filter to sample ion concentrations on both sides of the membrane (<span id="ref34551775.32519783" class="cite">Lewis <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). TOK (tandem-pore outward-rectifying K<sup>+</sup>) channels consist of eight TMSs and two pore domains per subunit, organized in dimers. They play a role in cellular K<sup>+</sup> homeostasis and possibly also in plant-fungus symbioses (<span id="ref34555797.36567187" class="cite">Houdinet <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Yeast</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Tok1 outward rectifier K<sup>+</sup><span style="font-family: Times;"> channel of <em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em></span></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.7.10"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.7.10&tcname=">1.A.1.7.10</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TOK1 of 741 aas and 8 TMSs in a 6 + 2 TMS arrangement with P-loops between TMSs 5 and 6 as well as 7 and 8.&nbsp; It transports both Na<sup>+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup>, and has been characterized by <span id="ref34551775.32519783" class="cite">Lewis <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>. </span>Lipid tails
from both the upper and lower leaflets can partially penetrate into the
pore (<span id=""ref34543756.25487004"" class=""cite"">Aryal <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). The lipid tails do not sterically occlude the pore, but there is an inverse correlation
between the presence of water within the hydrophobic barrier and the number of lipids tails within
the lining of the pore (<span id=""ref34543756.25487004"" class=""cite"">Aryal <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Mammals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TWIK-1 of <em>Mus musculus </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.8.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.8.2&tcname=">1.A.1.8.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TASK-2 (KCNK5) two-pore domain, pH-sensitive, voltage-insensitive, outward rectifying K<sup>+</sup> channel (K<sup>+</sup> &gt; Rb<sup>+</sup> &gt;&gt; Cs<sup>+</sup> &gt; NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> &gt; Na<sup>+</sup> &asymp; Li<sup>+</sup>), present in renal epithelia.&nbsp; Regulated [inhibited] via group 1 metabolotropic glutamate receptors and by inositol phosphates (<span id="ref2652.14532113" class="cite">Chemin <em>et al</em>., 2003</span>).&nbsp; TASK-2 gating is controlled by changes in both extra- and intracellular
pH through separate sensors: arginine 224 and lysine 245, located at the extra- and intracellular
ends of transmembrane domain 4, respectively. TASK-2 is inhibited by a direct effect of CO2 and is regulated by
and interacts with G protein subunits. TASK-2 takes part in regulatory adjustments and is a mediator
in the chemoreception process in neurons of the retrotrapezoid nucleus where its pH<sub>i</sub> sensitivity
could be important in regulating excitability and therefore signalling of the O<sub>2</sub>/CO<sub>2</sub> status.
Extracellular pH increases, brought about by HCO<sub>3</sub><sup>- </sup>efflux from proximal tubule epithelial cells may couple to TASK-2 activation to maintain electrochemical gradients favourable to
HCO<sub>3</sub><sup>-</sup> reabsorption. TASK-2 is expressed at the
basolateral membrane of proximal tubule cells (<span id="ref34543613.25315981" class="cite">L&oacute;pez-Cayuqueo <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). Mutations are associated with the Balkan Endemic Nephropathy (BEN) chronic tubulointerstitial renal disease (<span id="ref34545438.27228168" class="cite">Reed <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). pH sensing in TASK2 channels is conferred by the combined action of several charged residues in the large extracellular M1-P1 loop (<span id="ref34549591.16239344" class="cite">Morton <em>et al.</em> 2005</span>). TASK-2, a member of the TALK subfamily of K2P channels, is opened by intracellular alkalization, leading the deprotonation of the K245 residue at the end of the TM4 helix. This charge neutralization of K245 may be sensitive or coupled to the fenestration state. The most important barrier for ion transport under K245<sup>+</sup> and open fenestration conditions is the entrance of the ions into the channel (<span id="ref34550847.31947679" class="cite">Bustos <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Mammals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TASK-2 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.8.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.8.3&tcname=">1.A.1.8.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>The 2P-domain K<sup>+</sup> channel, TWIK 2 (functions in cell electrogenesis (<span id="ref3976.10887187" class="cite">Patel <em>et al</em>., 2000</span>).</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Animals</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>TWIK2 of <i>Homo sapiens</i><br>
(Q9Y257)</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.8.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.8.4&tcname=">1.A.1.8.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Potassium channel subfamily K member 5, Kcnk5a, of 513 aas and 6 TMSs. It is 50% identical with the human ortholog.&nbsp; Genome analysis revealed that its genetic structure in the yellowfin seabream (<em>Acanthopagrus latus</em>) is influenced by a 
variety of factors including salinity gradients, habitat distance, and 
ocean currents (<span id="ref34557797.38147145" class="cite">Wang <em>et al.</em> 2024</span>)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KCNK5A of <em>Danio rerio</em> (Zebrafish) (<em>Brachydanio rerio</em>)&nbsp;</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.9.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.9.1&tcname=">1.A.1.9.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TREK-1 (KCNK2) K<sup>+</sup> channel subunit (Regulated by group 1 metabotropic glutamate receptors and by diacylglycerols and phosphatidic acids) (<a>Chemin <em>et al</em>., 2003</a>).  TREK-1, TREK-2 and TRAAK are all regulated by lysophosphatidic acid, converting these  mechano-gated, pH voltage-sensitive channels into leak conductances (<a>Chemin <em>et al</em>., 2005</a>). The mammalian K2P2.1 potassium channel (TREK-1, KCNK2) is highly expressed in excitable tissues, where it plays a key role in the cellular mechanisms of neuroprotection, anaesthesia, pain perception and depression (<a>Cohen et at., 2008</a>).  Alternative translation initiation in rat brain yields K2P2.1 potassium channels permeable to sodium (<a>Thomas <em>et al.</em> 2008</a>). The crystal structure of the human 2-pore domain K<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;channel, K2P1 has been solved (<a>Miller and Long, 2012</a>). Multiple modalities converge on a common gate to control K2P channel function (<a>Bagriantsev <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>).&nbsp; TREK-1 mediates fast and slow glutamate release in astrocytes upon GPCR activation (<span>Woo <em>et al.</em> 2012</span>). It is a mechanosensitive K<sup>+</sup> channel, present
in rat bladder myocytes, which is activated by swelling and arachidonic acid (<span>Fukasaku <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). The M2-hinges of TREK-1 and TREK-2 channels control their macroscopic current, subcellular localization and gating (<span>Zhuo <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). The human ortholog has acc # O95069 and has an additional N-terminal 15 aas. BL-1249, a compound from the fenamate class of nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drugs, is known to activate K2P2.1(TREK-1), the founding member of the thermo- and mechanosensitive TREK subfamily (<span id="ref34548736.30089357" class="cite">Pope <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Spadin and arachidonic acid, are known to suppress and activate TREK-1 channels, respectively (<span id="ref34551673.32469490" class="cite">Pappa <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Membrane phospholipids control gating of the mechanosensitive potassium leak channel, TREK1 (<span id="ref34556008.36841877" class="cite">Schmidpeter <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). A photoswitchable inhibitor of TREK channels controls pain in wild-type intact freely moving animals (<span id="ref34556023.36859433" class="cite">Landra-Willm <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). TREK-1 is an anesthetic-sensitive K<sup>+</sup> channel (<span id="ref34556433.37027798" class="cite">Spencer <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Mammals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TREK-1 of <em>Mus musculus </em>(P97438)</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.9.10"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.9.10&tcname=">1.A.1.9.10</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Potassium channel subfamily K member 16 (2P domain potassium channel Talk-1) (TWIK-related alkaline pH-activated K<sup>+</sup> channel 1) (TALK-1 or KCNK16) of 309 aas and 6 TMSs. It is an <span>outward rectifying potassium channel that produces rapidly activating and non-inactivating outward rectifier K<sup>+</sup> currents. </span>Allosteric coupling between transmembrane segment 4 and the selectivity filter regulates gating by extracellular pH (<span id="ref34554686.35500647" class="cite">Tsai <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KCNK16 of <em>Homo sapiens </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.9.11"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.9.11&tcname=">1.A.1.9.11</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><span>pH-dependent, voltage-insensitive, background potassium channel protein involved in maintaining the membrane potential, KCNK9, K2P9.1 or TASK3 (TASK-3) of 374 aas&nbsp; (<span>Huang <em>et al.</em> 2011</span>). Terbinafine is a selective activator of TASK3 (<span>Wright <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). </span><span>The response of the tandem pore potassium channel TASK-3 to voltage involves gating at the cytoplasmic 
mouth (<span id="ref6747.19703964" class="cite">Ashmole <em>et al.</em>, 2009</span>).&nbsp; </span>TASK-3 is involved in several physiological and pathological processes including sleep/wake control, cognition and epilepsy (<span id="ref34549781.31015283" class="cite">Tian <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). N-(2-((4-nitro-2-(trifluoromethyl)phenyl)amino)ethyl)benzamide (NPBA) is an activator (<span id="ref34549781.31015283" class="cite">Tian <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).  KCC2 regulates neuronal excitability and hippocampal activity via interaction with Task-3 channels (<span id="ref34550040.31269453" class="cite">Goutierre <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). A&nbsp; biguanide compound, CHET3, is a highly selective allosteric activator, and TASK-3 is  a druggable target for treating pain (<span id="ref34550567.31748231" class="cite">Liao <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). This channel may be present in mitochondria (<span id="ref34550683.31834993" class="cite">Parrasia <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Differential hydroxymethylation levels in the DNA of patient-derived neural stem cells implicated altered cortical development in bipolar disorder syndrome possibly altering KCNK9 expression (<span id="ref34556463.37139321" class="cite">Kumar <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KCNK9 or TASK3 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.9.12"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.9.12&tcname=">1.A.1.9.12</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><address><span>Potassium channel subunit of 330 aas. No channel 
activity was observed in heterologous systems. It probably needs to associate with other proteins (i.e., KCNK3 and KCNK9) to form a functional channel (<span id="ref34544541.21761257" class="cite">Huang et al. 2011</span>).</span></address></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KCNK15 of<em> Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.9.13"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.9.13&tcname=">1.A.1.9.13</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The Kcnk10a (TREK-2A) K<sup>+</sup> channel of 569 aas and 6 TMSs. It localizes in the brain and seems to regulate reproduction (<span id="ref34547460.29074372" class="cite">Loganathan <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TREK-2A of <em>Danio rerio </em>(Zebrafish)<em> (Brachydanio rerio)</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.9.14"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.9.14&tcname=">1.A.1.9.14</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Open rectifier K<sup>+</sup> channel 1, isoform D of 1001 aas and 6 TMSs, Ork1.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ORK1 of <em>Drosophila melanogaster </em>(Fruit fly)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.9.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.9.2&tcname=">1.A.1.9.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KCNK3 K<sup>+</sup> channel (TASK1, OAT1, TBAK1) (the K<sup>+</sup> leak conductance). TASK1 and 3 may play roles in nontumorigenic primary hyperaldosteronism (<span>Davies <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>).&nbsp; KCNK3/9/15 expression limits membrane depolarization and 
depolarization-induced secretion at least in part by maintaining 
intracellular K<sup>+</sup> (<span>Huang <em>et al.</em> 2011</span>). TWIK-related acid-sensitive potassium (TASK) channels, members of the two pore domain potassium (K2P) channel family, are found in neurons, cardiomyocytes and vascular smooth muscle cells, where they are involved in the regulation of heart rate, pulmonary artery tone, sleep/wake cycles and responses to volatile anaesthetics (<span id="ref34551644.32499642" class="cite">R&ouml;dstr&ouml;m <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). K2P channels regulate the resting membrane potential, providing background K<sup>+</sup> currents controlled by numerous physiological stimuli. Unlike other K2P channels, TASK channels are able to bind inhibitors with high affinity, exceptional selectivity and very slow compound washout rates. In general, potassium channels have an intramembrane vestibule with a selectivity filter situated above and a gate with four parallel helices located below, but the K2P channels studied so far all lack a lower gate.&nbsp;<span id="ref34551644.32499642" class="cite">R&ouml;dstr&ouml;m <em>et al.</em> 2020</span> presented the X-ray crystal structure of TASK-1, and showed that it contains a lower gate designated 'X-gate', created by interaction of the two crossed C-terminal M4 transmembrane helices at the vestibule entrance. This structure is formed by six residues ((243)VLRFMT(248)) that are essential for responses to volatile anaesthetics, neurotransmitters and G-protein-coupled receptors. Mutations within the X-gate and the surrounding regions affect both the channel-open probability and the activation of the channel by anaesthetics. Structures of TASK-1 bound to two high-affinity inhibitors showed that both compounds bind below the selectivity filter and are trapped in the vestibule by the X-gate, which explains their exceptionally low washout rates (<span id="ref34551644.32499642" class="cite">R&ouml;dstr&ouml;m <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). TWIK-related acid-sensitive K<sup>+</sup> channel 2 promotes renal fibrosis by inducing cell-cycle arrest (<span id="ref34557071.36465115" class="cite">Zhang <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TASK1 or KCNK3 of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (AAG29340)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.9.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.9.3&tcname=">1.A.1.9.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Neuronal 2-P (4 TMS) domain K<sup>+</sup> membrane tension-gated channel, TRAAK (stimulated by arachidonic acid and polyunsaturated fatty acids (<span id="ref3977.9628867" class="cite">Fink <em>et al</em>., 1998</span>). The crystal structures of conductive and nonconductive human K2P TRAAK K<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;channel has been solved (<span id="ref8332.22282805" class="cite">Brohawn <em>et al.</em>, 2012; <span id="ref34543662.25471887" class="cite">Brohawn <em>et al.</em> 2014</span></span>). Regulated by mechanical deformation of the membrane and temperature as well as polyunsaturated fatty acids (<span id="ref8332.22282805" class="cite">Brohawn <em>et al.</em>, 2012</span>).&nbsp;Multiple modalities converge on a common gate to control K2P channel function (<span id="ref8383.21765396" class="cite">Bagriantsev <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>).&nbsp; In the non-conductive state, a lipid acyl chain accesses the channel 
cavity through a 5 &Aring;-wide lateral opening in the membrane inner leaflet 
and physically blocks ion passage. In the conductive state, rotation of transmembrane helix 4 about a central hinge seals the intramembrane
 opening, preventing lipid block of the cavity and permitting ion entry.
 Additional rotation of a membrane interacting TM2-TM3 segment, unique 
to mechanosensitive K2Ps, against TM4 may further stabilize the 
conductive conformation. Comparison of the structures provodes a 
biophysical explanation for TRAAK mechanosensitivity--an expansion in 
cross-sectional area up to 2.7 nm<sup>2</sup> in the conductive state is expected
 to create a membrane-tension-dependent energy difference between 
conformations that promotes force activation (<span id="ref8332.22282805" class="cite"><span id="ref34543662.25471887" class="cite">Brohawn <em>et al.</em> 2014</span></span>).&nbsp; TM helix straightening and buckling may underlie channel activation (<span id="ref34543767.25500157" class="cite">Lolicato <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). A lipid chain blocks the conducting path in the closed state (<span id="ref34545739.27528747" class="cite">Rasmussen 2016</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TRAAK of <em>Mus musculus&nbsp;</em>(O88454)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.9.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.9.4&tcname=">1.A.1.9.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Outward rectifying mechanosensitive 2-P (4 TMS) domain K<sup>+</sup> channel, TREK-2 (KCNKA; KCNK10; K2P10). Activated by membrane stretch, acidic pH, arachidonic acid and unsaturated fatty acids.&nbsp; <span>Dong <em>et al.</em> 2015</span> described crystal structures of the human TREK-2 channel (up to 3.4 angstrom 
resolution) in two conformations and in complex with norfluoxetine, the 
active metabolite of fluoxetine (Prozac) and a state-dependent blocker 
of TREK channels. Norfluoxetine binds within intramembrane fenestrations
 found in only one of these two conformations. Channel activation by 
arachidonic acid and mechanical stretch involves conversion between 
these states through movement of the pore-lining helices. This 
provides an explanation for TREK channel mechanosensitivity, regulation 
by diverse stimuli, and possible off-target effects of the serotonin 
reuptake inhibitor Prozac (<span>Dong <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; The unique gating properties of TREK-2 and the mechanisms by which extracellular and intracellular stimuli harness pore gating
allosterically have been studied (<span>Zhuo <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). TREK-2 moves from the "down" to the "up" conformation in direct response to membrane stretch.&nbsp;<span>Aryal <em>et al.</em> 2017</span> showed how state-dependent interactions with lipids affect the movement of TREK-2, and how stretch influences both the inner pore and selectivity filter. They also demonstrated why direct pore block by lipid tails does not represent theprincipal mechanism of mechanogating (<span>Aryal <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). The M2-hinges of TREK-1 and TREK-2 channels control their macroscopic current, subcellular localization and gating (<span>Zhuo <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). TREK-2 responds to a diverse range of stimuli. Two states, "up" and "down", are known from x-ray structural crystallographic studies and have been suggested to differ in conductance.&nbsp;<span>Brennecke and de Groot 2018</span> found that the down state is less conductive than the up state. The introduction of membrane stretch in the simulations shifts the state of the channel toward an up configuration. Membrane pressure changes the conformation of the transmembrane helices directly and consequently also influences the channel conductance (<span>Brennecke and de Groot 2018</span>). 3-d structures are known (PDB 4XDJ_!-D). Phosphatidyl-(3,5)-bisphosphate (PI(3,5)P2) activates (<span>Kirsch <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TREK-2 of <em>Rattus norvegicus</em><br /> (Q9JIS4)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.9.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.9.5&tcname=">1.A.1.9.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>The TWiK family muscle K<sup>+</sup> channel protein 18 (TWiK or Two-P domain K<sup>+</sup> channel family) (controls muscle contraction and organismal movement; <span id="ref5629.11027209" class="cite">Kunkel <em>et al</em>., 2000</span>)</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Animals</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>TWK-18 of <i>Caenorhabditis elegans</I><br>
(Q18120)</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.9.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.9.6&tcname=">1.A.1.9.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The pH-sensitive 2 pore (4 TMS) K<sup>+</sup> channel, TASK-4 (Expressed in liver, lung, pancreas and other tissues; <span id="ref3979.11248242" class="cite">Decher <em>et al</em>., 2001</span>). The response of the tandem pore potassium channel TASK-3 (TC# 1.A.1.9.11) (K(2P)9.1) to voltage involves gating at the cytoplasmic mouth (<span id="ref6747.19703964" class="cite">Ashmole <em>et al.</em>, 2009</span>). Models have revealed the convergence of amino acid regions that are known to modulate anesthetic
activity onto a common three-dimensional cavity that forms a putative anesthetic binding site in tandem pore potassium channels (<span id="ref34543598.25340635" class="cite">Bertaccini <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TASK-4 of <em>Homo sapiens</em><br /> (Q96T54)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="1.A.1.9.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=1.A.1.9.7&tcname=">1.A.1.9.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Sup-9 K<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;channel of 329 aas and 6 TMSs. It is <span>involved in coordination of muscle contraction (<span id="ref8209.14534247" class="cite">de la Cruz <em>et al.</em> 2003</span>). Activity is regulated by Sup-18 (<span id="ref34549563.24586202" class="cite">de la Cruz <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>)</span> and by Unc-93 (TC# 2.A.1.5.8).&nbsp; It may also be regulated by Sup-10 (Q17374); it may be a suppressor of Unc-93 (<span id="ref8209.14534247" class="cite">de la Cruz <em>et al.</em>, 2003</span>).
